Second Intelligibles and the Need for a Second Conception
Defines second intelligibles and explains the need for a second conception for mental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how second intelligibles are defined and why mental attributes require a separate act of conception.

Core Idea
Mental attributes require a second glance to be truly seen.

This passage introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic philosophy: between accidents that occur to a quiddity in external reality and those that only occur in the mind. It explains that while external accidents can be judged in the first mental conception, mental accidents require a second mental act to be properly attributed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the examples of external accidents (like universality) and the concept of 'second intelligibles'.
Pay attention to the terms 'عوارض خارجية' and 'عوارض ذهنية'.
2
Understand the two types of accidents
Identify the distinction: external accidents occur only in external existence, while mental accidents occur only in the mind. The passage explains why mental accidents require a second conception.
3
Note the exception for necessary concomitants
The passage mentions 'لوازم الماهية' (necessary concomitants) that occur in both existences, so they can be judged in either consideration without a second conception.
This shows that not all attributes need a second conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم الخارجية، كالكلية والجزئية والذاتية والعرضية والجنسبة والفصلية إلى غير ذلك من أشباهها، ويسمى مثل ذلك معقولات ثانية)، ومحصول الكلام أن الماهية إذا وجدت في الذهن كانت ملحوظة في نفسها وصالحة لأن يحكم عليها بأمور لا تعرض لها إلا في الخارج وهي المسماة بالعوارض الخارجية، وغير صالحة لأن يحكم عليها بأمور لا تعرض لها إلا في الذهن بل لا بد لهذا الحكم من تصورها مرة ثانية ليلاحظ عروض هذه العوارض لها فيحكم بها عليها، وأما لوازم الماهية من حيث هي هي عارضة لها في الوجودين فيصح أن يحكم بها عليها في كل واحدة من الملاحظتين.
The second type - first aim: the addition between the world and the known. External [accidents], such as universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, genus, differentia, and other similar things. Such [accidents] are called 'second intelligibles'. The gist of the discourse is that when the quiddity exists in the mind, it is considered in itself and is suitable for being judged by things that only occur to it in the external, which are called external accidents; and it is not suitable for being judged by things that only occur to it in the mind, but rather this judgment requires conceiving it a second time in order to observe the occurrence of these accidents to it and then judge them of it. As for the necessary concomitants of the quiddity as such, they occur to it in both existences, so it is valid to judge them of it in each of the two considerations.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'second intelligibles' and distinguishes between external and mental accidents. It begins by listing examples of external accidents: universality, particularity, etc. These are called second intelligibles because they are accidental to the quiddity when considered mentally. The author then explains: when the quiddity exists in the mind, it can be considered in itself and is apt for judgments concerning things that only occur to it in external existence, such as being black or white. However, for judgments concerning things that only occur to it in the mind (like universality), a second mental conception is necessary, because the quiddity in its initial consideration is not directly observed as the subject of those mental attributes. Finally, necessary concomitants of the quiddity as such occur in both external and mental existence, so they can be judged in either consideration without a second conception.

Key Terms
معقولات ثانية
عوارض خارجية
عوارض ذهنية
لوازم الماهية
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental accidents can be predicated of the quiddity in its first mental consideration.
Correction: The author explains they require a second explicit conception because the quiddity in the first consideration is seen as a mirror for external realities, not directly as the subject of mental attributes.

Checkpoint
Why do mental accidents require a second conception of the quiddity?
A) Because the quiddity is not present in the mind the first time.
B) Because the quiddity is considered as a mirror for external realities and not directly as the subject of mental attributes.
C) Because mental accidents are not real.
D) Because the mind cannot conceive itself.

Key Takeaway
Second intelligibles are attributes that only occur in the mind, and to attribute them to a quiddity, a second mental conception is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge something to be 'universal' or 'particular', do you realize that these categories exist only in your mind, not in the thing itself? How might confusing mental constructs with external reality lead you to false certainty? Identify one area in your life where you have treated a mental category as if it were an objective fact—what would change if you saw it for what it is?
?
The lesson tells us that to attribute mental properties, we must conceive the thing a second time. How often do you make snap judgments about ideas without that second glance? Pause now and think of a belief you hold about yourself—is it based on an external reality or a mental construct? What would happen if you re-examined it with a 'second conception'?
?
Consider the necessary concomitants of your own existence—things that are true of you whether in thought or reality. Are you living in alignment with these truths, or are you distracted by mental accidents that don't define you? Write down one necessary truth about yourself and one mental accident you need to stop treating as essential.

The First Doctrine on Single Knowledge Relating to Two Known Things
Presents the first doctrine and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'ari doctrine that a single temporal knowledge can relate to two known things in detail, and why its justification via analogy with God's knowledge is refuted.

Core Idea
The first doctrine's analogy with God's knowledge fails due to lack of a valid common ground and inconsistency with power.

This passage plunges into a central debate in Ash'ari theology: can one act of knowledge encompass two distinct objects in detail? The first doctrine answers yes, appealing to God's all-encompassing knowledge as a model. The refutation that follows dismantles this analogy through two incisive arguments, exposing a critical flaw in reasoning from the divine to the created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing at commentary markers. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
The passage uses bracketed insertions; these are part of the original commentary structure.
2
Identify the question and the four doctrines
Find where the question is posed: can a single temporal knowledge relate to two known things? Note that the passage mentions four doctrines but only expands on the first.
The question is explicitly about temporal knowledge, excluding the eternal.
3
Study the first doctrine and its justification
The first doctrine asserts absolute permissibility, citing God's knowledge as evidence. Understand the reasoning: since God's single knowledge knows all, created knowledge should be able to know multiple things.
4
Analyze the refutation
The refutation makes two points: (1) the analogy is invalid because there is no common ground between divine and created knowledge; (2) an analogous argument from power leads to contradiction, as temporal power cannot affect two objects.
The refutation aims to show that the doctrine leads to inconsistency within Ash'ari principles.
5
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary clarifies two issues: the rebuttal of the view that knowledge is a real attribute, and the 'story of the inanimate object' illustrating that knowledge is not mere presence of form.
This commentary helps reinforce the refutation by showing alternative solutions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الثاني : العلم الواحد وتعلقه بمعلومين
عن القائل بأنَّ العلم إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية مستلزمة للإضافة، وأما الإشكال عليه في العلم بالمعدومات الخارجية فإنما يندفع عنه إما باعتبار الوجود الذهني، كما ذهب إليه الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية، وادعى أن العلم إضافة مخصوصة لا صورة عقلية لما عرفت من قصة الجماد، وإما بأنَّ الإضافة تتوقف على الامتياز الذي لا يتوقف على وجود المتمايزين لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن .
[ المقصد الثاني : العلم الواحد وتعلقه بمعلومين ]
( العلم الواحد الحادث قيده بالحدوث لأن العلم الواحد القديم يجوز تعلقه بأمور غير متناهية هل يجوز تعلقه بمعلومين) أي على سبيل التفصيل، إذ لا خلاف في أنَّ العلم الواحد الإجمالي يتعلق بما فيه كثرة ، ( فيه مذاهب ) أربعة ( الأول لبعض أصحابنا : ( من الأشاعرة ( يجوز) ذلك مطلقاً ( كعلم الله تعالى)، فإنه علم واحد متعلق بمعلومات متعددة ( قلنا : ( هذا ) تمثيل ) وقياس للشاهد على الغائب ، ( بلا جامع فيكون باطلاً، وأيضاً يلزم على من احتج من أصحابنا بذلك القدرة، فإن القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلق بمقدورين على أصلنا، كما سيأتي من أنَّ القدرة القديمة لا يجوز تعلقها بمقدورين فصاعداً، والفرق بين العلم والقدرة في ذلك متعذر،
قوله : ( وأما الإشكال عليه أي على القائل المذكور وأما القائل بأنه الصورة الحاضرة فلا إشكال عليه لكون المعدومات موجودة في الذهن.
قوله : ( من قصة (الجماد وهي أنَّ ماهية السواد حاصلة له مع عدم العلم، فلو كان العلم هو الصورة الحاصلة للشيء لكان الجماد عالماً بالسواد.
Type Two - Second Aim: The single knowledge and its relation to two known things. Concerning the claim that knowledge is a pure relation or a real attribute entailing a relation, and as for the objection against it concerning knowledge of external non-existents, it is rebutted either by considering mental existence, as the Imam al-Razi held in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, claiming that knowledge is a specific relation not a mental form because of the story of the inanimate object, or by [saying] that the relation depends on distinction which does not depend on the existence of the two distinct things, neither externally nor mentally. [The Second Aim: The Single Knowledge and Its Relation to Two Known Things] (The single temporal knowledge—qualified by temporality because the single eternal knowledge may relate to infinite things—may it relate to two known things?) i.e., in detail, since there is no disagreement that the single summary knowledge relates to what has multiplicity. (Concerning it are four doctrines) Four. (The first: of some of our companions (from the Ash'aris): it is absolutely permissible, like the knowledge of God Almighty, for He is one knowledge relating to multiple known things. (We say: this is an analogy) and a comparison of the witnessed to the absent, (without a common ground, so it is invalid. Also, what is incumbent upon those of our companions who argue by that is [the case of] power, for temporal power does not relate to two objects of power according to our school, as will come that eternal power may not relate to two or more objects of power, and the distinction between knowledge and power in that is impossible.) (His saying: 'And as for the objection against him'—i.e., against the aforementioned claimant—and as for the claimant that it is the present form, there is no objection against him because non-existents exist in the mind. His saying: 'From the story of the inanimate object'—which is that the essence of blackness is present to it without knowledge, so if knowledge were the form present to a thing, the inanimate object would be knowledgeable of blackness.)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines the second topic (al-maqsad al-thani) concerning whether a single knowledge can relate to two distinct known things in detail, noting that summary knowledge is not disputed. It then enumerates four doctrines (mazahib). The first doctrine, held by some Ash'aris, asserts absolute permissibility, citing God's knowledge as a model. The refutation follows: first, this is an invalid analogy (tamthil) due to the absence of a relevant common ground (jami'); second, it entails a problem with power (qudra), since according to Ash'ari doctrine, temporal power cannot relate to two objects of power, and no distinction between knowledge and power can be made to avoid the inconsistency. The commentary clarifies two points: (1) the objection against the view that knowledge is a real attribute is rebutted by mental existence or by the independence of relation from distinction; (2) the 'story of the inanimate object' illustrates that if knowledge were the mere presence of a form, inanimate objects would be knowing.

Key Terms
العلم الواحد
تعلقه بمعلومين
مذاهب
تمثيل
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's single knowledge knows all, then any single knowledge (including created) must be able to know multiple things in detail.
Correction: The passage shows that this analogy is invalid because there is no common ground (jami') between divine and created knowledge, and a similar argument regarding power leads to a contradiction within Ash'ari principles.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is the analogy with God's knowledge invalid?
A) Because God's knowledge is eternal and cannot be compared to temporal knowledge.
B) Because there is no common ground (jami') between the witnessed and the absent.
C) Because the Ash'aris reject all analogies in theology.
D) Because power provides a better analogy than knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The first doctrine's reliance on a simple analogy with God's knowledge is refuted by the lack of a valid common ground and by the inconsistent implications for power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge and power, do you ever catch yourself assuming that what is true for created things is automatically true for the Creator? What does this passage teach you about the danger of such assumptions?
?
How often do you examine the 'common ground' in your own reasoning about religious matters? Are there beliefs you hold simply because they 'make sense' to you without checking whether the analogy is valid?
?
If you were to apply the same standard of consistency to your own beliefs about God's attributes that this passage applies to power and knowledge, would any of your beliefs fail the test? What would you need to change?

The First Proof: Absolute Impermissibility and the Priority Argument
Presents the second doctrine and its first proof with critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the permissibility of one knowledge relating to multiple knowns, and why it is considered weak.

Core Idea
The argument from priority fails because priority is not absent in reality.

The second doctrine holds that a single knowledge cannot relate to more than one known object. The first proof argues that if it could, no number would have priority, leading to an infinite regress. However, the commentator shows that this reasoning is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. It presents the doctrine and the first proof with its critique.
Notice how the proof is introduced with 'ليس عدد أولى من عدد'.
2
Identify the proof and its weakness
The proof argues that if one knowledge could relate to multiple knowns, then no number would have priority, leading to infinite knowns. The commentator denies the lack of priority in reality.
The critique says 'عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ممنوع'.
3
Examine the counter-example using power
The passage then uses a counter-argument against Mu'tazilites: if they accept that a single temporal power can relate to multiple objects but not infinite, the same applies to knowledge.
This shows a parallel between knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني وهو مذهب الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري ) وكثير من المعتزلة : لا يجوز ذلك مطلقا، (إذ
قوله: (مطلقا ) أي سواء كانا نظريين أو بديهيين جاز الانفكاك بينهما أو لا
ليس عدد أولى من عدد فيلزم من جواز تعلقه بأكثر من واحد ( تعلقه) بل جواز تعلقه ( بأمور غير متناهية)، فيلزم أن يجوز كون أحدنا عالماً بعلم واحد بمعلومات لا تتناهى وهو باطل قطعاً، ( وقد عرفته ) وأنه ضعيف جداً، لأن عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ممنوع، وعدمها عندنا لا يجدي شيئاً، والمحتج بهذه الحجة إن كان معتزلياً ورد عليه القدرة الواحدة الحادثة فإنها على أصله يجوز تعلقها بمقدورين وأكثر، ولا يجوز تعلقها بمقدورات لا تتناهى ( وأيضاً فلا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر)، هذا دليل ثان على المذهب الثاني وهو أن يقال : لو تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين لسد العلم بأحدهما مسد العلم بالآخر ضرورة أن الشيء يسد مسد نفسه، والتالي باطل ) فإنَّ التعلق بالمعلوم (داخل) في حقيقته أي حقيقة العلم فإذا علم أحد المعلومين كان التعلق به داخلاً في هذا العلم، دون التعلق بالمعلوم الآخر، وإذا علم الآخر انعكس الحال، فلا يتصور قيام العلم بأحدهما مقام العلم بالآخر، (ونقض) هذا الدليل الثاني (بعلم الله تعالى، فإنه جار فيه مع كونه متعلقاً بأمور متعددة، (وبسائر) أي
قوله : ( ضرورة إلخ إن أريد أنه يسد مسد نفسه مع قطع النظر عن التعلقين فظاهر البطلان إذ لا اثنينية حتى يتصور المسدية بينهما، وإن أريد مع اعتبار التعلقين فممنوع لأن التعلقين يمنعان المسدية لأن المعلومين مختلفان وهذا تفصيل ما ذكره بقوله : وليس يلزم من وحدة الذات إلخ .
أن تحقق الوجود الذهني، إنما هو بعد تحقق تلك الإضافة رتبة أو معه فلا يفيد سبق تحقق المضاف إليه، فليتأمل. قوله : (يجوز ذلك مطلقا سواء كان المعلومان نظريين أم لا قوله : ( وأنه ضعيف جداً لأن عدم الأولوية إلخ) ورد أيضاً بأنه لم لا يجوز ذلك في حقنا كما جاز في حقه تعالى وإن لم يكن واقعا في حقنا.
قوله : (داخل) في حقيقته مبني على أن العلم عبارة عن صفة ذات إضافة أي عن هذا المقيد من حيث أنه مقيد إذ لو كان عبارة عن نفس الإضافة لم يكن التعلق داخلاً في حقيقته بل يكون نفسه إلا أن يريد بالدخول عدم الخروج.
قوله : ( ونقض بعلم الله هذا هو النقض الإجمالي والنقض التفصيلي منع أن التعلق داخل في حقيقة العلم، كما أشار إليه الشارح وقد يقال : النقض بعلم الله تعالى غير ظاهر، لأن جريان الدليل فيه ممنوع فإن محصل الدليل أن التعلق الحادث داخل في حقيقة العلم الحادث ولا يتصور كون التعلقِ الحادث داخلاً في حقيقة العلم القديم، وأنت خبير بأن الدال على عدم سد أحد العلمين سداً الآخر دخول التعلقين المخصوصين فيهما، والفرق بين التعلقين بالقدم والحدوث لا يفيد إذ ليس دليل عدم السد الانفكاك بل الضرورة شاهدة بذلك كما يفهم من تقرير الشارح فتأمل.
(The second: and it is the doctrine of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and many of the Mu'tazila: it is absolutely impermissible, (since) (His saying: 'absolutely' meaning whether the two are theoretical or intuitive, whether separation between them is permissible or not.) No number has priority over another, so from the permissibility of its relation to more than one (its relation) rather the permissibility of its relation (to infinite matters) it would follow that it is possible for one of us to know with one single knowledge infinite objects of knowledge, which is absolutely false. (And you have known it) and it is very weak, because the lack of priority in the very matter is denied, and its lack in our view does not avail anything, and the one arguing with this proof if he is a Mu'tazilite then against him [stands] the single temporal power, for according to his principle it may relate to two or more objects of power, but it may not relate to infinite objects of power. (And also: one does not suffice instead of the other): this is a second proof for the second doctrine, which is to say: if the single knowledge relates to two known things, then knowledge of one would suffice instead of knowledge of the other necessarily because a thing suffices instead of itself, but the consequent is false. For the relation to the known is (included) in its reality, i.e., the reality of knowledge; so when one knows one of the two knowns, the relation to it is included in this knowledge, but not the relation to the other known; and when he knows the other, the situation is reversed, so it is not conceivable that knowledge of one of them stands in the place of knowledge of the other. (And this second proof is refuted) (by the knowledge of God Almighty, for it applies to it while it relates to multiple things, and by other [things]) i.e. (His saying: 'Necessarily, etc.': if it is meant that it suffices instead of itself disregarding the two relations, it is clearly false because there is no duality to conceive of sufficiency; if it is meant with consideration of the two relations, it is denied because the two relations prevent sufficiency since the two knowns are different. And this is the detail of what he stated by his saying: 'And it does not follow from the unity of essence etc.' That the realization of mental existence is only after the realization of that relation in rank or with it, so it does not benefit precedence of the realization of the related thing, so ponder. His saying: 'It is absolutely permissible whether the two known things are theoretical or not' – And his saying: 'And it is very weak because the lack of priority etc.' – it is also responded that why is it not permissible in our case as it is permissible in His case, even if it does not occur in our case. His saying: 'Included' in its reality is based on the fact that knowledge is an attribute with relation, i.e., this qualified thing insofar as it is qualified, because if it were the relation itself, then the relation would not be included in its reality but would be itself, unless by 'inclusion' he means non-exclusion. His saying: 'And the refutation by the knowledge of God' – this is the summary refutation, and the detailed refutation is the denial that the relation is included in the reality of knowledge, as the commentator indicated. And it may be said: the refutation by the knowledge of God is not apparent, because the application of the proof to it is denied; the gist of the proof is that the temporal relation is included in the reality of temporal knowledge, and it is not conceivable that the temporal relation be included in the reality of eternal knowledge. And you are aware that the indicator of non-sufficiency of one of the two knowledges instead of the other is the inclusion of the two specific relations in them, and the distinction between the two relations by eternity and temporality does not avail, for the proof of non-sufficiency is not separation but rather necessity witnesses to that, as understood from the commentator's exposition, so reflect.)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the second mazhab (impermissibility) held by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and many Mu'tazila, along with two proofs and a rebuttal. The first proof argues that if a single knowledge could relate to more than one known object, then no number would have priority, leading to the possibility of infinite known objects, which is impossible. The commentator explains that this argument is weak because the lack of priority in reality is denied; also, a Mu'tazilite opponent would be refuted by the analogous case of power (qudra). The second proof states that if one knowledge related to two knowns, then knowledge of one would suffice for knowledge of the other (since a thing suffices for itself), but this is false because the relation to each known is included in the knowledge's reality. This second proof is then rebutted by the example of God's knowledge, which is one yet relates to multiple things. The commentator then analyzes the rebuttal: the summary refutation is that the relation is not necessarily included in the reality of knowledge; the detailed refutation denies the inclusion for eternal knowledge. Further discussion notes that the rebuttal may not be apparent because the proof specifically concerns temporal knowledge, while God's knowledge is eternal, so the distinction may avoid the objection.

Key Terms
مذهب
عدد أولى
أمور غير متناهية
القدرة الواحدة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof from priority is decisive because it appears to lead to a contradiction.
Correction: The proof is weak because the claim 'no number has priority' is not established in reality; priority may exist inherently.

Checkpoint
What weakness does the commentator point out in the first proof?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of known objects.
B) The lack of priority in the very matter is denied.
C) The proof only applies to theoretical knowledge, not intuitive.
D) The Mu'tazila do not accept the premises.

Key Takeaway
The first proof fails because its central premise—that no number has priority—is not accepted as a given reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly strong proof can collapse if its foundational premise is not granted. Think of a conviction you hold—what is the one unproven assumption it rests on? Will you honestly investigate it today, even if it means unsettling your certainty?
?
The Mu'tazilite held that a single power can relate to multiple but not infinite, yet the proof for knowledge would force them to deny the multiple for power. Are there inconsistencies in your own life—where you accept a principle in one area but reject it in another—that this lesson exposes? Commit to aligning your practices with your principles.
?
The commentator did not accept the proof simply because it was presented; he challenged its premise. When was the last time you allowed someone to challenge a core assumption of yours, and you listened without defensiveness? Set a time this week to seek out a perspective that might shake one of your comfortable assumptions.

The Second Proof and Its Rebuttal Using God's Knowledge
Presents the second proof (sadd) and its rebuttal using God's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof (sufficiency argument) and its rebuttal using God's knowledge, along with the distinction between temporal and eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
The sufficiency argument is refuted by God's knowledge, which is one yet relates to multiple things.

The second proof argues that if one knowledge related to two knowns, knowledge of one would suffice for the other, which is impossible. However, this proof is rebutted by the example of God's knowledge, sparking a debate about the nature of knowledge and relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic text from 'وأيضاً فلا يسد' to understand the proof that knowledge of one known would suffice for the other.
The proof relies on the idea that 'a thing suffices instead of itself'.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The proof is rebutted by the example of God's knowledge: God's knowledge is one but relates to multiple things. This challenges the proof's assumption.
The rebuttal is stated as 'ونقض بعلم الله تعالى'.
3
Analyze the distinction
The commentator then discusses whether the rebuttal is valid by distinguishing temporal knowledge (where relation is part of reality) from eternal knowledge.
This leads to a deeper analysis of the nature of knowledge and relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(The second: and it is the doctrine of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and many of the Mu'tazila: it is absolutely impermissible, (since) (His saying: 'absolutely' meaning whether the two are theoretical or intuitive, whether separation between them is permissible or not.) No number has priority over another, so from the permissibility of its relation to more than one (its relation) rather the permissibility of its relation (to infinite matters) it would follow that it is possible for one of us to know with one single knowledge infinite objects of knowledge, which is absolutely false. (And you have known it) and it is very weak, because the lack of priority in the very matter is denied, and its lack in our view does not avail anything, and the one arguing with this proof if he is a Mu'tazilite then against him [stands] the single temporal power, for according to his principle it may relate to two or more objects of power, but it may not relate to infinite objects of power. (And also: one does not suffice instead of the other): this is a second proof for the second doctrine, which is to say: if the single knowledge relates to two known things, then knowledge of one would suffice instead of knowledge of the other necessarily because a thing suffices instead of itself, but the consequent is false. For the relation to the known is (included) in its reality, i.e., the reality of knowledge; so when one knows one of the two knowns, the relation to it is included in this knowledge, but not the relation to the other known; and when he knows the other, the situation is reversed, so it is not conceivable that knowledge of one of them stands in the place of knowledge of the other. (And this second proof is refuted) (by the knowledge of God Almighty, for it applies to it while it relates to multiple things, and by other [things]) i.e. (His saying: 'Necessarily, etc.': if it is meant that it suffices instead of itself disregarding the two relations, it is clearly false because there is no duality to conceive of sufficiency; if it is meant with consideration of the two relations, it is denied because the two relations prevent sufficiency since the two knowns are different. And this is the detail of what he stated by his saying: 'And it does not follow from the unity of essence etc.' That the realization of mental existence is only after the realization of that relation in rank or with it, so it does not benefit precedence of the realization of the related thing, so ponder. His saying: 'It is absolutely permissible whether the two known things are theoretical or not' – And his saying: 'And it is very weak because the lack of priority etc.' – it is also responded that why is it not permissible in our case as it is permissible in His case, even if it does not occur in our case. His saying: 'Included' in its reality is based on the fact that knowledge is an attribute with relation, i.e., this qualified thing insofar as it is qualified, because if it were the relation itself, then the relation would not be included in its reality but would be itself, unless by 'inclusion' he means non-exclusion. His saying: 'And the refutation by the knowledge of God' – this is the summary refutation, and the detailed refutation is the denial that the relation is included in the reality of knowledge, as the commentator indicated. And it may be said: the refutation by the knowledge of God is not apparent, because the application of the proof to it is denied; the gist of the proof is that the temporal relation is included in the reality of temporal knowledge, and it is not conceivable that the temporal relation be included in the reality of eternal knowledge. And you are aware that the indicator of non-sufficiency of one of the two knowledges instead of the other is the inclusion of the two specific relations in them, and the distinction between the two relations by eternity and temporality does not avail, for the proof of non-sufficiency is not separation but rather necessity witnesses to that, as understood from the commentator's exposition, so reflect.)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the second mazhab (impermissibility) held by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and many Mu'tazila, along with two proofs and a rebuttal. The first proof argues that if a single knowledge could relate to more than one known object, then no number would have priority, leading to the possibility of infinite known objects, which is impossible. The commentator explains that this argument is weak because the lack of priority in reality is denied; also, a Mu'tazilite opponent would be refuted by the analogous case of power (qudra). The second proof states that if one knowledge related to two knowns, then knowledge of one would suffice for knowledge of the other (since a thing suffices for itself), but this is false because the relation to each known is included in the knowledge's reality. This second proof is then rebutted by the example of God's knowledge, which is one yet relates to multiple things. The commentator then analyzes the rebuttal: the summary refutation is that the relation is not necessarily included in the reality of knowledge; the detailed refutation denies the inclusion for eternal knowledge. Further discussion notes that the rebuttal may not be apparent because the proof specifically concerns temporal knowledge, while God's knowledge is eternal, so the distinction may avoid the objection.

Key Terms
سد مسد
تعلق بالمعلوم
نقض بعلم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the rebuttal using God's knowledge straightforwardly disproves the second proof.
Correction: The rebuttal is not simply a counterexample; it requires analyzing whether the relation is included in the reality of knowledge, especially distinguishing temporal from eternal knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof against the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress of known objects.
B) Knowledge of one known would not suffice for knowledge of the other.
C) The analogy with God's knowledge is invalid.
D) No number has priority over another.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is refuted by God's knowledge, but the strength of the rebuttal depends on whether the relation is considered part of the reality of knowledge, leading to a nuanced theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that God's knowledge is one yet relates to all things challenge your heart when you realize that He knows every detail of your life, including your hidden thoughts and intentions? Will you live today with the awareness that nothing escapes His knowledge?
?
The proof about sufficiency shows that our human knowledge is limited: knowing one thing does not give us knowledge of another. When you consider the limits of your own knowledge, are you humbled before the infinite knowledge of Allah? How will you seek to rely on His knowledge rather than your own?
?
The debate about whether relation is included in the reality of knowledge reminds us that even our deepest understanding is finite. In what area of your life are you acting as if your knowledge is sufficient, ignoring that only Allah's knowledge is complete? What step will you take today to surrender your limited understanding to Him?

The Objection of Infinite Regress and the Answer
Presents an objection that knowledge implies knowledge of itself leading to infinite regress, and the answer denying the implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of infinite regress in self-awareness and how it is answered by distinguishing between knowing and attending to one’s knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowing something does not automatically mean knowing that you know it.

This passage presents a classic objection in Islamic epistemology: if knowing something implies knowing that you know it, then an infinite chain of knowledges would follow, which is impossible. The answer clarifies that knowledge of knowledge only arises when the mind actively attends to its own knowledge, and this attention is not continuous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Absorb the flow of the argument before analyzing it.
Notice that the passage contains both an objection and a counter-argument.
2
Identify the objection’s reasoning
Find the section where the objection is presented: it claims that knowing something implies knowledge of that knowledge, leading to an infinite regress. Understand why this is considered impossible.
The impossibility is based on the idea that actual infinite knowledges cannot exist in a finite being.
3
Understand the answer’s key point about attention
The answer denies the implication by saying that knowledge of knowledge requires a deliberate act of attention (التفات). Without this attention, we can know something without being aware of our knowledge. Note that attention is not perpetual.
The author uses the example of forgetfulness to illustrate that knowledge can exist without awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الثاني : العلم الواحد وتعلقه بمعلومين والجامعة لكن ذلك ضروري البطلان، فظهر أنّ من علم شيئاً علم علمه به ثم يعلم أيضاً علمه بعلمه، به لما ذكرناه من استلزام العلم بالعلم به وهلم جراً، فثمة معلومات غير متناهية فلو لم يجز أن تكون عدة من هذه المعلومات معلومة بعلم واحد، بل استدعى كل معلوم منها علماً على حدة، لزم أن يكون لأحدنا إذا علم شيئاً واحداً علوم غير متناهية بالفعل وأنّه محال والوجدان يحققه أي يشهد بكونه محالاً والجواب : إِنّا لا نسلم أنّ العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بذلك العلم، إذ قد نعلم الشيء ولا نعلم العلم به إلا إذا التفت الذهن إليه، لما مر من أن الموجود في الذهن لا يمكن أن يحكم عليه من حيث هو موجود فيه إلا بأن يتصور مرة ثانية، ويلتفت إليه من حيث أنه في الذهن وهذا الالتفات لا يمكن أن يستمر حتى يلزم علوم غير متناهية بل ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، ولا فرق في ذلك بين معلوم واحد ومعلومات جمة، إذ يجوز الغفلة عن العلم في الكل، ولكن لما كان الالتفات إلى العلم قريباً من الحصول غير محتاج إلى تكلف ظن أنه حاصل بالفعل عليه ما بني.
The second type - the second purpose: The single knowledge and its attachment to multiple known things. And the [؟] but that is necessarily invalid, so it appeared that whoever knows something knows his knowledge of it, then he also knows his knowledge of his knowledge of it, due to what we mentioned of the implication of knowledge of knowledge of it, and so on ad infinitum. So there are infinite known things. So if it is not permissible for several of these known things to be known by a single knowledge, but rather each known thing requires a separate knowledge, then it would be necessary that one of us, when he knows one single thing, has actually infinite knowledges, and that is impossible, and intuition confirms it, i.e., testifies that it is impossible. The answer: We do not concede that knowledge of a thing necessarily implies knowledge of that knowledge, for we may know a thing and not know the knowledge of it unless the mind turns to it. Because what has been mentioned: that an object existing in the mind cannot be judged insofar as it exists in it except by being imagined a second time and turning attention to it insofar as it is in the mind. And this attention cannot continue such that infinite knowledges are necessary; rather it is cut off by the cessation of consideration. And there is no difference in that between a single known thing and many known things, for forgetfulness of knowledge can occur in all. But because attention to knowledge is close to occurrence and does not require strain, it was thought that it is actually present, upon which [the argument] was built.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection argues that if knowing something necessarily implies knowing that one knows it, then an infinite regress follows, making actual infinite knowledge in the knower impossible. The answer denies the implication: we can know a thing without being aware of our knowledge of it unless we turn our attention to it. The mind cannot judge an object as existing in it without a second act of imagination and attention. This attention does not persist indefinitely; it stops when consideration stops. Even for multiple objects, forgetfulness can occur. The illusion that attention is always present leads to the mistaken belief in continuous self-awareness of knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
تعلق
التفات
علوم غير متناهية
تجانس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We are always aware of our own knowledge at all times.
Correction: The passage shows that awareness of knowledge requires deliberate attention, and this attention is not continuous. We can know something without being aware that we know it.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does knowing something not necessarily imply knowing that one knows it?
A) Because knowledge is a simple attribute that cannot be reflected upon.
B) Because the mind cannot pay attention to its own knowledge continuously; attention is required and it ceases.
C) Because infinite regress is impossible only for multiple objects, not for a single object.
D) Because the knowledge of knowledge is a different type of knowledge that does not follow from the first.

Key Takeaway
Knowing something does not automatically give you knowledge of that knowledge; awareness requires active attention, which is not continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed you knew something about your own knowledge without actually reflecting? How does this passage challenge your assumption that self-awareness is automatic?
?
The passage says attention to knowledge is close and effortless, but not actual. In your worship, do you rely on the mere presence of knowledge without actively attending to it? What would change if you consciously turned your attention to what you know of Allah?
?
Consider a time you thought you were certain of a belief. Did you ever examine whether your certainty came from actual attention or mere assumption? How can you test the difference in your daily life?

Al-Amidi's Objection: Why Knowledge Cannot Attach to Itself
Al-Amidi's denial that knowledge can attach to itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's argument that knowledge cannot attach to itself because attachment requires two distinct things.

Core Idea
Attachment requires two distinct things; a thing is not distinct from itself.

Al-Amidi raises a key objection to the idea that knowledge can be known by self-attachment. He argues that the relation of attachment (ta'alluq) can only exist between two distinct entities. Since knowledge and itself are not distinct, self-attachment is impossible. This lesson examines his reasoning and the conceptual framework behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how al-Amidi defines attachment as requiring two distinct things (متغايرين).
The key term is تغاير (distinctness).
2
Identify the premise
Al-Amidi states that the relation (تعلق) cannot exist between a thing and itself because there is no distinction (لا مغايرة). Grasp this logical point.
3
Reflect on the metaphor claim
Al-Amidi also criticizes the phrase 'essence of the thing and itself' as a mere metaphor. Consider why he thinks it has no real meaning here.
He says it is مجاز لا حقيقة له.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من قال يعني به الآمدي فإنه قال في الجواب : الكلام إنما هو في جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين والعلم لا يتعلق بنفسه لأن النسبة التي هي التعلق لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين متغايرين ولا مغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه وقول القائل : ذات الشيء ونفسه يوهم بظاهره نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه إلا أنه مجاز لا حقيقة له ومعنى كون الواحد منا عالما بعلمه لا يزيد على قيام علمه بنفسه فظاهر البطلان لأنّ تعلق العلم بالعلم ليس من قبيل تعلق الشيء بنفسه، بل من قبيل تعلق [؟] قوله : ( يوهم بظاهره) إنّما قال ذلك لأنّه في الحقيقة من إضافة العام إلى الخاص . قوله : ( إنّه مجاز لا حقيقة له فإنَّ المراد منهما لا غير الشيء كما في ما يقوم بنفسه أي لا يقوم بغيره . فثمة معلومات غير متناهية هي معلومات علمية، فلا بد أن يجوز تعلق علم منها باثنين حتى ينقطع التسلسل في درجة قيل على تقدير جواز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد يلزم علوم غير متناهية، أيضاً لأنّ العلم المتعلق بأشياء متعددة يتعلق به علم آخر على تقدير لزوم العلم بالعلم وهلم جراً. والجواب أولاً : جواز أن يتعلق العلم بنفسه حينئذ إذ يكفي فيه تغاير اعتباري كما صرح به في أواخر بحث العلم من الإلهيات . وثانياً جواز أن يتعلق العلم المعلوم بعلمه. قوله: (وأما قول من قال إلخ حاصل كلام الآمدي منع أن طريق معلومية العلم تعلقه بنفسه بل العلم به علم حضوري فلا يلزم تسلسل وإن لم يجوز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد، وقد يدفع دليله بكفاية التغاير الاعتباري أيضاً كما أشرنا إليه.
As for the statement of the one who said—meaning al-Amidi—for he said in the answer: The discussion is only about the permissibility of the attachment of a single knowledge to two known things, and knowledge does not attach to itself because the relation, which is the attachment, can only be conceived between two distinct things, and there is no distinction between a thing and itself. And the saying of the speaker: 'the essence of the thing and itself' apparently suggests a relation of the thing to itself, but it is a metaphor and has no reality. And the meaning of one of us being knowing by his knowledge is nothing more than his knowledge subsisting by itself, so it is apparently invalid because the attachment of knowledge to knowledge is not of the kind of attachment of a thing to itself, but of the kind of attachment of [؟]. His statement: (it apparently suggests) he only said that because in reality it is an addition of the general to the specific. His statement: (it is a metaphor and has no reality) for the intended meaning of the two is nothing other than the thing itself, as in what subsists by itself, i.e., does not subsist by another. So there are infinite known things that are scientific known things, so it is necessary to allow the attachment of one knowledge to two of them so that the regress is cut at some degree. It is said: on the assumption of allowing the attachment of a single knowledge to multiple things, infinite knowledges would also be necessary because the knowledge attached to multiple things would have another knowledge attached to it on the assumption of the implication of knowledge of knowledge, and so on. The answer: First, the permissibility of knowledge attaching to itself then, for a relational distinction suffices, as stated at the end of the discussion of knowledge in Ilahiyyat. Second, the permissibility of the known knowledge attaching to his knowledge. His statement: (as for the saying of the one who said etc.) the gist of al-Amidi's statement is the denial that the way of knowing knowledge is its attachment to itself, rather knowledge of it is presential knowledge, so no regress follows even if the attachment of a single knowledge to multiple things is not allowed. And his argument can be repelled by the sufficiency of the relational distinction as we indicated.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents al-Amidi's objection and the response. Al-Amidi argued that knowledge cannot attach to itself because attachment requires two distinct things, and a thing is not distinct from itself. He also said that saying 'the essence of the thing and itself' is metaphorical. The response counters that knowledge can attach to itself with a relational distinction (i.e., the knowledge and its object are the same in essence but distinct in consideration), or alternatively, knowledge of one's own knowledge is presential (حضوري) and does not involve attachment, thus avoiding regress. The passage also addresses a secondary objection: even if a single knowledge can attach to multiple objects, infinite regress might still follow because that knowledge would itself be known by another knowledge. The answer is that the regress stops either by self-attachment or by presential knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
تغاير
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that self-knowledge is impossible because knowledge cannot reflect on itself.
Correction: Al-Amidi's objection is not that self-knowledge is impossible, but that if it requires attachment, it fails. Later responses offer alternatives.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, why can't knowledge attach to itself?
A) Knowledge is a simple attribute that cannot be reflected upon.
B) Attachment requires two distinct things, and a thing is not distinct from itself.
C) Infinite regress is impossible only for multiple objects, not for a single object.
D) Knowledge of knowledge is always presential.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi argues that self-attachment of knowledge is impossible because attachment requires two distinct entities, and a thing is not distinct from itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know myself,' what kind of relationship are you assuming exists between your knowledge and your self? How does al-Amidi's objection challenge you to reconsider whether that knowledge is truly 'attached' or something more unified?
?
Al-Amidi warns against mistaking metaphor for reality. In your relationship with Allah, where might you be using language that implies separation or attachment (e.g., 'I have a connection with Allah') without realizing it is a metaphor? How can you make your understanding more literal and sincere?
?
If knowledge cannot attach to itself, what does that imply about how you should view your own self-awareness? Are you content to simply 'have' knowledge of yourself, or are you called to become that knowledge in action?

Two Responses: Relational Distinction and Presential Knowledge
The response to al-Amidi: two alternatives to self-attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two solutions to the problem of self-attachment: relational distinction (taghayyur i'tibari) and presential knowledge (ilm huduri).

Core Idea
Knowledge can be known by self-attachment through relational distinction or by presential knowledge.

After presenting al-Amidi's objection, the text offers two ways to defend the possibility of knowing one's own knowledge. The first uses the concept of 'relational distinction' (taghayyur i'tibari), where a single entity is considered from two aspects. The second invokes the idea of presential knowledge (ilm huduri), which does not require attachment. This lesson clarifies both responses and how they resolve the regress argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the response section
Read the Arabic passage and translation from 'لأنّ تعلق العلم بالعلم' onwards. Notice the structure: first a counter-argument about regress, then two answers.
The text addresses a further objection about infinite regress before giving the two solutions.
2
Understand relational distinction
The first answer allows knowledge to attach to itself using a 'relational distinction' (تغاير اعتباري). This means the knowledge and its object are the same in essence but considered from different aspects. Find the sentence that states this.
The term تغاير اعتباري is key.
3
Grasp presential knowledge
The second answer is that knowledge of one's own knowledge is presential (علم حضوري), not acquired through attachment. Al-Amidi himself conceded this. Reflect on how this avoids regress.
The passage ends by noting that even al-Amidi's own view can be repelled by relational distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من قال يعني به الآمدي فإنه قال في الجواب : الكلام إنما هو في جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين والعلم لا يتعلق بنفسه لأن النسبة التي هي التعلق لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين متغايرين ولا مغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه وقول القائل : ذات الشيء ونفسه يوهم بظاهره نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه إلا أنه مجاز لا حقيقة له ومعنى كون الواحد منا عالما بعلمه لا يزيد على قيام علمه بنفسه فظاهر البطلان لأنّ تعلق العلم بالعلم ليس من قبيل تعلق الشيء بنفسه، بل من قبيل تعلق [؟] قوله : ( يوهم بظاهره) إنّما قال ذلك لأنّه في الحقيقة من إضافة العام إلى الخاص . قوله : ( إنّه مجاز لا حقيقة له فإنَّ المراد منهما لا غير الشيء كما في ما يقوم بنفسه أي لا يقوم بغيره . فثمة معلومات غير متناهية هي معلومات علمية، فلا بد أن يجوز تعلق علم منها باثنين حتى ينقطع التسلسل في درجة قيل على تقدير جواز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد يلزم علوم غير متناهية، أيضاً لأنّ العلم المتعلق بأشياء متعددة يتعلق به علم آخر على تقدير لزوم العلم بالعلم وهلم جراً. والجواب أولاً : جواز أن يتعلق العلم بنفسه حينئذ إذ يكفي فيه تغاير اعتباري كما صرح به في أواخر بحث العلم من الإلهيات . وثانياً جواز أن يتعلق العلم المعلوم بعلمه. قوله: (وأما قول من قال إلخ حاصل كلام الآمدي منع أن طريق معلومية العلم تعلقه بنفسه بل العلم به علم حضوري فلا يلزم تسلسل وإن لم يجوز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد، وقد يدفع دليله بكفاية التغاير الاعتباري أيضاً كما أشرنا إليه.
As for the statement of the one who said—meaning al-Amidi—for he said in the answer: The discussion is only about the permissibility of the attachment of a single knowledge to two known things, and knowledge does not attach to itself because the relation, which is the attachment, can only be conceived between two distinct things, and there is no distinction between a thing and itself. And the saying of the speaker: 'the essence of the thing and itself' apparently suggests a relation of the thing to itself, but it is a metaphor and has no reality. And the meaning of one of us being knowing by his knowledge is nothing more than his knowledge subsisting by itself, so it is apparently invalid because the attachment of knowledge to knowledge is not of the kind of attachment of a thing to itself, but of the kind of attachment of [؟]. His statement: (it apparently suggests) he only said that because in reality it is an addition of the general to the specific. His statement: (it is a metaphor and has no reality) for the intended meaning of the two is nothing other than the thing itself, as in what subsists by itself, i.e., does not subsist by another. So there are infinite known things that are scientific known things, so it is necessary to allow the attachment of one knowledge to two of them so that the regress is cut at some degree. It is said: on the assumption of allowing the attachment of a single knowledge to multiple things, infinite knowledges would also be necessary because the knowledge attached to multiple things would have another knowledge attached to it on the assumption of the implication of knowledge of knowledge, and so on. The answer: First, the permissibility of knowledge attaching to itself then, for a relational distinction suffices, as stated at the end of the discussion of knowledge in Ilahiyyat. Second, the permissibility of the known knowledge attaching to his knowledge. His statement: (as for the saying of the one who said etc.) the gist of al-Amidi's statement is the denial that the way of knowing knowledge is its attachment to itself, rather knowledge of it is presential knowledge, so no regress follows even if the attachment of a single knowledge to multiple things is not allowed. And his argument can be repelled by the sufficiency of the relational distinction as we indicated.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents al-Amidi's objection and the response. Al-Amidi argued that knowledge cannot attach to itself because attachment requires two distinct things, and a thing is not distinct from itself. He also said that saying 'the essence of the thing and itself' is metaphorical. The response counters that knowledge can attach to itself with a relational distinction (i.e., the knowledge and its object are the same in essence but distinct in consideration), or alternatively, knowledge of one's own knowledge is presential (حضوري) and does not involve attachment, thus avoiding regress. The passage also addresses a secondary objection: even if a single knowledge can attach to multiple objects, infinite regress might still follow because that knowledge would itself be known by another knowledge. The answer is that the regress stops either by self-attachment or by presential knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that relational distinction is a mere wordplay with no real metaphysical import.
Correction: In kalam, relational distinction (تغاير اعتباري) is a valid conceptual tool used to distinguish aspects of a single reality without multiplying entities.

Checkpoint
What are the two ways the text offers to allow knowledge to be known?
A) Relational distinction and acquired knowledge.
B) Presential knowledge and empirical evidence.
C) Relational distinction and presential knowledge.
D) Infinite regress and self-evidence.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge can be known either by self-attachment via relational distinction or by presential knowledge, both avoiding infinite regress.

?
If God's knowledge knows itself presentially, how does that reassure you about God's awareness of your most hidden struggles? Does your faith rely on an abstract infinite regress, or on a direct, present knowledge from God? What would change if you fully internalized that God's knowledge of you is immediate?
?
The lesson resolves a logical problem with a subtle distinction (taghayyur i'tibari). Are you content with such distinctions, or do you find them evasive? How does your attitude toward subtle theology affect your trust in the kalām tradition's ability to defend the faith? Al-Razi's View and the Possibility Distinction
Al-Razi's view on knowledge and the mental/external possibility distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's resolution of the dispute about whether one knowledge can know multiple objects and the distinction between mental and external possibility.

Core Idea
The definition of knowledge determines whether one knowledge can attach to many objects.

This passage presents Imam al-Razi's position on a classic theological puzzle: can a single instance of knowledge encompass multiple known things? Al-Razi ties the answer to how we define knowledge itself. The author then clarifies that while the mind can conceive of such a thing (mental possibility), its actual existence in reality (external possibility) is a separate and more contentious question.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read al-Razi's claim that the dispute branches from the definition of knowledge. He gives two possibilities: knowledge as the attachment itself, or as an attribute that possesses attachments.
Notice that the first definition leads to multiple attachments requiring multiple knowledge, while the second allows one knowledge with multiple attachments.
2
Understand the author's distinction
The author introduces two types of possibility: mental (جواز ذهني) and external (جواز خارجي). Mental possibility means the mind does not see any immediate contradiction, but it does not guarantee actual existence.
Mental possibility is undisputed here; the debate is about external possibility.
3
Examine the argument for external possibility
Consider the example of knowledge that blackness is opposite to whiteness. If that knowledge is not attached to both blackness and whiteness, it can only know absolute opposition, not the specific opposition between them.
This argument suggests that knowledge of a relation requires attachment to both relata simultaneously.
4
Review the objection and answer
Naqd al-Muhassal objects: knowledge of a whole includes knowledge of its parts, so one knowledge can cover multiple things. But the author clarifies that the dispute concerns detailed knowledge of each particular, not knowledge of a whole as such.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : والمختار عندي أن الخلاف متفرع على تفسير العلم فإن قلنا : إنّه نفس التعلق، فلا شك أن التعلق بهذا غير التعلق بذاك، فلا يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين وإن قلنا : إنّه صفة ذات تعلق جاز أن يكون العلم صفة واحدة يتعدد تعلقاته وكثرة التعلقات الخارجة عن حقيقة الصفة لا تجعل الصفة متكثرة في ذاتها قال المصنف : واعلم أن الجواز الذهني لا نزاع فيه و الجواز الخارجي مما يناقش فيه يعني أنا إذا نظرنا إلى أن العلم صفة ذات تعلق جوز العقل أن تكون هذه الصفة واحدة شخصية متعلقة بأمور أن العقل بمجرد هذه الملاحظة لا يحكم بامتناع تعلق علم واحد بمعلومين وهذا هو المسمى بالإمكان الذهني وليس يلزم منه الإمكان بحسب نفس الأمر لجواز أن يكون ممتنعاً في نفسه لكن العقل لم يطلع على وجه استحالته والاستدلال على إمكانه في نفسه بأنّ العلم المتعلق بكون السواد مضاداً للبياض إن لم يكن هو بعينه متعلقاً بهما لم يمكن متعلقاً بالمضادة التي بينهما، بل بمطلق المضادة وكلامنا في المضادة المخصوصة مفهوم متعلق بهما والعلم بها موقوف على العلم بهما معاً ، فليس هناك علم واحد علم به معلومان وفي نقد المحصل أن العلم إذا فسر بالتعلق جاز تعدد المعلوم مع وحدة العلم، كما إذا علم مجموع من حيث هو هو، فإنّ الأجزاء داخلة فيه والجواب ما مر من أنَّ الخلاف في تعلق العلم الواحد بمتعدد على سبيل التفصيل بأن يكون متعلقاً بخصوصية هذا وخصوصية ذاك معاً، فإنه جوزه جماعة كثيرة وليس العلم المتعلق بالمجموع من هذا القبيل.
Imam al-Razi said: The chosen view in my opinion is that the dispute is based on the definition of knowledge. For if we say it is the attachment itself, then there is no doubt that the attachment to this is other than the attachment to that, so one knowledge cannot be attached to two known things. And if we say it is an attribute possessing attachment, then it is permissible for knowledge to be a single attribute with multiple attachments, and the multiplicity of attachments outside the reality of the attribute does not make the attribute multiple in its essence. The author said: Know that mental possibility is undisputed, while external possibility is debatable. That is, when we consider that knowledge is an attribute possessing attachment, the mind permits that this attribute be a single individual attached to multiple things—the mind by this mere observation does not judge the impossibility of one knowledge attaching to two known things. This is what is called mental possibility, and it does not entail possibility in itself (external) because it may be impossible in itself but the mind has not come to know the aspect of its impossibility. And the argument for its possibility in itself is that the knowledge attached to the fact that blackness is opposite to whiteness—if it is not itself attached to both, it cannot be attached to the specific opposition between them, but rather to absolute opposition. And our discussion concerns the specific opposition, which is a concept attached to both, and knowledge of it is dependent on knowledge of both together. So there is no single knowledge by which two known things are known. And in Naqd al-Muhassal: If knowledge is interpreted as attachment, the multiplicity of known with the unity of knowledge is permissible, as when one knows a whole as such, for the parts are included in it. The answer: As previously mentioned, the dispute is about the attachment of a single knowledge to multiple things in a detailed manner, such that it is attached to the particularity of this and the particularity of that together. Many groups have allowed it, and knowledge related to the whole is not of this kind.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's resolution and the author's elaboration. Al-Razi states that the dispute over whether one knowledge can apply to multiple known objects is based on the definition of knowledge. If knowledge is merely the attachment itself, then each attachment is distinct, so one knowledge cannot attach to multiple objects. If knowledge is an attribute that possesses attachments, then a single attribute can have multiple attachments without being multiplied. The author then introduces a crucial distinction between mental possibility (الجواز الذهني) and external possibility (الجواز الخارجي). The mind can conceive of a single knowledge with multiple attachments, so it is mentally possible. But whether it is actually possible in external reality is another question. The author also considers an argument for external possibility using the example of knowledge of the specific opposition between blackness and whiteness, and then mentions a counterpoint from Naqd al-Muhassal that knowledge of a whole includes the parts, but that is not detailed knowledge of each particular.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
جواز ذهني
جواز خارجي
تفسير العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if something is mentally conceivable, it must be actually possible.
Correction: The author explicitly states that mental possibility does not entail external possibility; something may be impossible in itself but the mind has not discovered its impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what determines whether a single knowledge can be attached to multiple objects?
A) The type of objects (abstract vs. concrete).
B) The definition of knowledge as either the attachment itself or an attribute with attachments.
C) Whether the knowledge is presential or acquired.
D) The number of known objects (two or more).

Key Takeaway
In kalam, mental possibility does not guarantee external reality; the two must be distinguished, and the definition of knowledge determines the scope of its attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read about the distinction between mental possibility and external possibility. Consider a belief you hold strongly—something you are convinced is true but have never tested against reality. Could it be that this belief is only mentally possible, not externally real? How would you know the difference, and what would it mean for your life if it turned out to be only mental?
?
The passage argues that if knowledge is an attribute with attachment, then one knowledge can know many things in a detailed manner. Think about your own understanding of a complex concept—like justice or mercy. Do you experience it as a single, deep understanding that applies to many situations, or as separate pieces of knowledge? What does your answer reveal about how you define knowledge in your own heart?
?
This lesson touches on the nature of God's knowledge. Does it challenge you to think that God's knowledge might be a single, all-encompassing attribute knowing every detail? How does that affect your trust in His plan? Are you willing to let go of the mental possibility that He only knows generalities, and embrace the reality that He knows every particular of your life intimately?

Definition of Composite Ignorance
Defines composite ignorance and clarifies the condition of steadfastness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of composite ignorance and why steadfastness is not a necessary condition.

Core Idea
Composite ignorance is a firm but false belief, even if it can be shaken by doubt.

In this passage, the author defines composite ignorance (الجهل المركب) precisely. He clarifies that it is a firm belief that does not match reality, and importantly, the belief does not have to be deeply rooted or impervious to doubt. This distinguishes it from common understandings. Let's examine the definition and its nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of composite ignorance. Notice the three key elements: firm (جازم), steadfast (ثابت), non-corresponding (غير مطابق).
The term 'ثابت' here means firmly planted, not easily shaken.
2
Identify the controversy
The author mentions that steadfastness is not considered in composite ignorance as is commonly thought. This is a point of clarification.
Some books include steadfastness as a condition, but the author argues otherwise.
3
Understand the name 'composite'
The author explains the name: it is called composite because it combines belief with ignorance—the person believes something false.
So it is a composite of belief and ignorance of the truth.
4
Consider the marginal notes
The notes elaborate on steadfastness and a logical point. They clarify that steadfastness means not being removed by doubt, and discuss the relationship between knowledge of the components and the composite.
The logical note is more advanced and may be studied separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : الجهل المركب الجهل المركب : عبارة عن اعتقاد جازم ثابت غير مطابق)، سواء كان مستنداً إلى شبهة أو تقليد، فليس الثابت معتبراً في الجهل المركب كما هو المشهور في الكتب، وإنما سمي مركباً؛ لأنه يعتقد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه فهذا جهل بذلك قوله : ( فليس الثبات إلخ ) وهو عدم الزوال بالتشكيك . قوله : ( موقوف على العلم بهما فيكون العلم بهما سابقاً على العلم بها فلا يكون عينه، فإن قلت : اللازم مما ذكرتم تغاير العلم بهما والعلم بها لكن العلم بهما واحد مع تعدد المعلوم فيتم الكلام . قلت : وحدة العلم بهما أيضاً ممنوعة. قوله : (كما هو المشهور في الكتب ) أي عدم اعتبار الثبوت .
The third purpose: Composite ignorance. Composite ignorance: It is an expression for a firm, steadfast, non-corresponding belief, whether it is based on a doubt or blind imitation. So steadfastness is not considered in composite ignorance as is well-known in the books. It is called 'composite' because he believes the thing contrary to what it is, so it is ignorance of that. His statement: (so steadfastness is not etc.) meaning it is not subject to removal by doubt. His statement: (dependent on knowledge of both, so knowledge of them precedes knowledge of it, so it is not the same. If you say: The necessary consequence of what you mentioned is the distinction between knowledge of them and knowledge of it, but knowledge of them is one with multiplicity of known, so the argument is complete. I say: The unity of knowledge of them is also denied.) His statement: (as is well-known in the books) i.e., the non-consideration of steadfastness.

Commentary
This passage defines composite ignorance (الجهل المركب). It is a firm and unwavering belief that does not correspond to reality. The definition explicitly states that it does not require the belief to be steadfast (ثابت) in the sense of being immune to doubt; rather, steadfastness is not a necessary condition, contrary to what is commonly stated in many books. The reason it is called 'composite' is that it combines ignorance with belief, i.e., the person believes something that is false, so he is ignorant of the truth. The marginal notes further clarify: steadfastness means the belief does not vanish under skeptical questioning. Another note discusses a logical point about knowledge of both objects preceding knowledge of the relation, and denies that knowledge of the two objects is one.

Correction: The passage clarifies that steadfastness (being immune to doubt) is not a necessary condition; composite ignorance can be a belief that is firm but could be shaken by doubt.

Key Takeaway
Composite ignorance is a firm false belief that does not have to be deeply rooted or resistant to doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
