The author said: 'And in it' i.e., in the second matter built on the opinion of the second faction, 'there is a consideration that I have alerted you to if you remember, where I said to you: In the first section of this type we are in, the mental form is knowledge and the known. That is because we reason what is pure negation and sheer non-existence in the external, and there is no doubt that when we know it, there occurs between us and it a relation and a specific connection, and it is inconceivable that a relation is realized except between two distinct things, and there is no distinction except with the affirmation of each of the two distinct things in general. And since the known here is not in the external, it is in the mind, so the mental form is the essence of the known. Thus knowledge and the known have become united in essence, and it is necessary that the attribute of universality belongs to the intelligible form, and what was said—that the attribute of universality is not the form but the known by it—is invalid.'
His statement: 'Befitting one who holds the known etc.' contains an indication of another interpretation of the text, i.e., interpreting knowledge as the known.
His statement: 'With its end' i.e., his statement: 'Is it not that when the known is...' for the form.
His statement: 'Different from it in essence' and the meaning of the correspondence of knowledge to the known according to them is that the knowledge is a specific suitability with its known, and therefore it became an instrument for witnessing it and knowledge of it without other.
His statement: 'And if he had explicitly stated this expression' the closest in interpreting the author's statement is to make knowledge in the sense of the known.
His statement: 'It is not the form but the known by it' can be applied to the opinion of the first faction, that their intent is that universality is in terms of being known, not in terms of being knowledge; for the apparent meaning of 'form' is the aspect of being knowledge.

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique (nazar) of the second point, which claimed that the universal is the known object, not the form. The author argues that we can conceive of pure non-existence (like non-existence itself) and when we know it, a relation occurs between the knower and the known. A relation requires two distinct things. Since the known (e.g., non-existence) is not external, it must be mental. Hence the mental form is the essence of the known, uniting knowledge and known. Therefore universality must attach to the form, not to some external known. The following glosses then explain that the author's wording can be interpreted in various ways, including that 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm) might mean 'the known' (al-ma’lum), and that the first faction might also say universality pertains to the aspect of being known rather than being knowledge.

Key Terms

اتحاد العلم والمعلوم (ittihad al-‘ilm wa al-ma’lum - unity of knowledge and known)
نفي محض (nafy mahd - pure negation)
تعلق (ta‘alluq - relation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The critique is against mental forms in general, or that it denies that mental forms represent external objects.
Correction: The critique specifically refutes the second faction's claim that the form is not the known; it does not reject mental forms altogether, but rather establishes that the mental form is the known itself, at least in this context.

Key Takeaway
The mental form is the very essence of the known, so universality must be a property of the form, not of an external object.

?
If knowledge and the known are one in essence, then your understanding of Allah's attributes is a mental form—does that reduce His reality? The Second Group's View on Universality of Mental Forms
The author explains the second group's position on universality and mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's claim that universality belongs to the known quiddity, not to the mental form, and why this view follows from their premise that mental existence is metaphorical.

Core Idea
The second group says universality is a property of the known thing, not the mental image.

In this passage, the author explains the position of the philosophers (the second group) regarding mental forms. They hold that mental existence is metaphorical; the mental image is a phantom that relates to the quiddity of the external thing. Universality, they argue, does not attach to these mental phantoms but to the quiddity known through them. The author notes that this view fits those who distinguish between knowledge and the known, unlike the first group who identify them. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the philosophers' view on mental existence and then their view on universality.
2
Understand the concept of 'shabah' (phantom)
The philosophers describe the mental image as a phantom with a specific relation to the quiddity of the external thing. This phantom is knowledge of that thing specifically.
The relation (nisba) is crucial for distinguishing the knowledge of one thing from another.
3
Grasp the argument about universality
Universality does not attach to the mental forms because these phantoms are not predicated of individuals; instead, the quiddity known through them is predicated. So universality belongs to the known quiddity, not the mental form.
4
Identify the author's comment
The author notes that this view fits the second group (who separate knowledge from the known) and not the first group (who identify them).
This sets up the refutation that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية وجود ذهني بحسب الحقيقة بل بحسب المجاز والتأويل كان يقال مثلاً : النار موجودة في الذهن، ويراد أنَّه يوجد فيه شبح له نسبة مخصوصة إلى ماهية النار بسببها كان ذلك الشبح علماً بالنار لا بغيرها من الماهيات وكنا قد أشرنا إلى ذلك فيما سبق، وكذا على قولهم : لا تكون الكلية عارضة للصور العقلية حقيقة؛ لأن تلك المثل والأشباح ليست محمولة على أفراد المعلومات بتلك الصور، بل المحمول عليها ماهيتها المعلومة بها، فأشار المصنف إلى أنَّ القول بأنَّ الصورة العقلية ليست كلية، إنما الكلي هو المعلوم بها يليق بمذهب هؤلاء لا بمذهب الفرقة الأولى إذ المعلوم والعلم عندهم متحدان ذاتاً. فقوله : يرى العلم غير الصور الذهنية أراد به ما ذكرناه من أنه يرى العلم غير الصور الذهنية المساوية للمعلومات في الماهية، بل يراه صوراً ذهنية مخالفة في الماهية لما علم بها، فمحصول كلامه : يليق بمن يرى المعلوم غير الصور الذهنية، ولو صرّح بهذه العبارة لانتظم أول الكلام مع آخره الذي سيأتي بلا حاجة إلى تأويل كما يشهد به كل فطرة سليمة . قال المصنف : ( وفيه ) أي في الأمر الثاني المبني على رأي الفرقة الثانية ( نظر قد نبهتك عليه إن كنت على ذكر منك حيث قلت لك : ( في المقصد الأول من هذا النوع الذي نحن فيه ( الصورة الذهنية العلم والمعلوم) وذلك لأنا نعقل ما هو نفي محض وعدم صرف في الخارج، ولا شك أنا إذا علمناه حصل بيننا وبينه تعلق، وإضافة مخصوصة ولا يتصور تحقق النسبة إلا بين شيئين متمايزين ولا تمايز إلا مع ثبوت كل من المتمايزين في الجملة، وإذ ليس المعلوم هاهنا في الخارج فهو في الذهن فالصورة الذهنية هي ماهية المعلوم فقد اتحد العلم والمعلوم بالذات ووجب أن يكون المتصف بالكلية هي الصورة العقلية وبطل ما قيل من أن المتصف بالكلية ليس هو الصورة بل المعلوم بها
The second type - the sixth purpose: The saying of the philosophers regarding mental forms: mental existence is not in reality but metaphorically and by interpretation. It is said, for example: 'Fire exists in the mind,' meaning that there exists in it a phantom having a specific relation to the quiddity of fire, due to which that phantom becomes knowledge of fire and not of other quiddities. And we have already indicated that previously. Similarly, according to their saying: 'Universality does not actually attach to mental forms because those likenesses and phantoms are not predicated of the individuals of the known things by those forms; rather, what is predicated of them is their quiddity known by them.' So the author pointed out that the statement that the mental form is not universal, rather the universal is what is known by it, befits the doctrine of these (the second group) and not the doctrine of the first group, since the known and knowledge are identical in essence according to them. His saying: 'He sees knowledge as other than mental forms' — he meant what we mentioned: that he sees knowledge as other than mental forms that are equal to the known things in quiddity, rather he sees it as mental forms that differ in quiddity from what is known by them. So the upshot of his speech: 'It befits one who sees the known as other than mental forms.' And if he had made this expression explicit, the beginning of the speech would have been consistent with its end, which will come, without need for interpretation, as every sound nature testifies. The author said: 'And in it' — i.e., in the second matter based on the opinion of the second group — 'there is a consideration that I have alerted you to, if you are mindful, where I said to you: (In the first purpose of this type we are in: the mental image is knowledge and the known).' That is because we understand what is pure negation and absolute non-existence in the external world, and there is no doubt that when we know it, there occurs between us and it a relation and specific attribution. And the realization of the relation cannot be conceived except between two distinct things, and there is no distinctness except with the subsistence of each of the two distinct things in some way. And since the known here is not in the external world, it is in the mind. So the mental image is the quiddity of the known. Therefore, knowledge and the known have become identical in essence, and it is necessary that what is described by universality is the mental form, and what was said — that what is described by universality is not the form but the known by it — is invalidated.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second group's view that universality (al-kulliyya) belongs to the known (al-ma'lum) rather than the mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyya). The passage begins by explaining the philosophers' position: mental existence is metaphorical; the mental image is a phantom (shabah) that has a specific relation to the quiddity (mahiyya) of the thing, making it knowledge of that thing. On their view, universality does not actually attach to mental forms because these phantoms are not predicated of individuals; rather, the quiddity known by them is what is predicated. The author then notes that this view (that the mental form is not universal, but rather the known is universal) fits the second group, not the first group (who hold that knowledge and known are identical in essence). The author then presents an objection (nazar) to this second group's position. The objection is based on the nature of relation (nisba). If we conceive a pure negation (nafy mahd) or non-existence ('adam sirf) in the external world, there is still a relation between the knower and the known. But a relation requires two distinct things (shay'an mutamayyizan), and distinctness requires each to subsist in some way. Since the known (e.g., a negation) does not exist externally, it must exist in the mind. Thus the mental image is the quiddity of the known, so knowledge and the known are identical in essence. Therefore, the mental form must be what is described by universality. The claim that universality belongs to the known, not the form, is invalidated.

الكلية
الوجود الذهني
الشبح
النسبة
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality is a property of the mental image itself (the phantom).
Correction: The second group holds that universality belongs to the known quiddity, not the mental form, because the mental forms are particular likenesses that are not predicated of individuals.

Checkpoint

B) The known quiddity (mahiyyat al-ma'lum)
C) The external object (al-'ayn al-kharijiyya)
D) The intellect (al-'aql)

Key Takeaway
The second group denies that universality attaches to mental forms and ascribes it to the known quiddity, consistent with their view that mental existence is metaphorical.

This lesson shows that mental images are merely phantoms with a relation to quiddity. Are you willing to admit that your mental constructs may not reflect the true essence?
?

Refutation: Identity of Knowledge and Known Requires Universality of the Mental Form
The author presents an objection (nazar) that refutes the second group's position by arguing for the identity of knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the mental form must be what is described by universality because the known, when it is a pure negation, must exist in the mind, making knowledge and known identical in essence.

Core Idea
If the known were distinct from the mental form, it would need external existence, making it particular, not universal.

The author now presents a powerful objection to the second group's position. He argues that when we know a pure negation or non-existence, there is still a relation between the knower and the known. But a relation requires two distinct things, each existing in some way. Since the known does not exist externally, it must exist in the mind; thus the mental form is the quiddity of the known. Consequently, knowledge and the known are identical, and universality must belong to the mental form. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about knowing pure negation
We can know a pure negation (nafy mahd) or non-existence ('adam sirf) that has no external reality. For example, 'nothingness' or 'absence'. This is a real cognitive act.
2
Recognize the necessity of a relation
When we know something, there is a relation (nisba) between the knower and the known. A relation requires two distinct things (mutamayyizan).
The relation is not possible if the known has no existence at all.
3
Conclude that the known exists in the mind
Since the known (e.g., a negation) does not exist externally, it must exist in the mind. Thus the mental form is the quiddity of the known.
Therefore, the mental form and the known are identical in essence (dhat).
4
Derive the implication for universality
If knowledge and known are identical, then the mental form is what is known. Since universality is a property of the known, it must be a property of the mental form. Thus the second group's claim is invalidated.
This completes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second type - the sixth purpose: The saying of the philosophers regarding mental forms: mental existence is not in reality but metaphorically and by interpretation. It is said, for example: 'Fire exists in the mind,' meaning that there exists in it a phantom having a specific relation to the quiddity of fire, due to which that phantom becomes knowledge of fire and not of other quiddities. And we have already indicated that previously. Similarly, according to their saying: 'Universality does not actually attach to mental forms because those likenesses and phantoms are not predicated of the individuals of the known things by those forms; rather, what is predicated of them is their quiddity known by them.' So the author pointed out that the statement that the mental form is not universal, rather the universal is what is known by it, befits the doctrine of these (the second group) and not the doctrine of the first group, since the known and knowledge are identical in essence according to them. His saying: 'He sees knowledge as other than mental forms' — he meant what we mentioned: that he sees knowledge as other than mental forms that are equal to the known things in quiddity, rather he sees it as mental forms that differ in quiddity from what is known by them. So the upshot of his speech: 'It befits one who sees the known as other than mental forms.' And if he had made this expression explicit, the beginning of the speech would have been consistent with its end, which will come, without need for interpretation, as every sound nature testifies. The author said: 'And in it' — i.e., in the second matter based on the opinion of the second group — 'there is a consideration that I have alerted you to, if you are mindful, where I said to you: (In the first purpose of this type we are in: the mental image is knowledge and the known).' That is because we understand what is pure negation and absolute non-existence in the external world, and there is no doubt that when we know it, there occurs between us and it a relation and specific attribution. And the realization of the relation cannot be conceived except between two distinct things, and there is no distinctness except with the subsistence of each of the two distinct things in some way. And since the known here is not in the external world, it is in the mind. So the mental image is the quiddity of the known. Therefore, knowledge and the known have become identical in essence, and it is necessary that what is described by universality is the mental form, and what was said — that what is described by universality is not the form but the known by it — is invalidated.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second group's view that universality (al-kulliyya) belongs to the known (al-ma'lum) rather than the mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyya). The passage begins by explaining the philosophers' position: mental existence is metaphorical; the mental image is a phantom (shabah) that has a specific relation to the quiddity (mahiyya) of the thing, making it knowledge of that thing. On their view, universality does not actually attach to mental forms because these phantoms are not predicated of individuals; rather, the quiddity known by them is what is predicated. The author then notes that this view (that the mental form is not universal, but rather the known is universal) fits the second group, not the first group (who hold that knowledge and known are identical in essence). The author then presents an objection (nazar) to this second group's position. The objection is based on the nature of relation (nisba). If we conceive a pure negation (nafy mahd) or non-existence ('adam sirf) in the external world, there is still a relation between the knower and the known. But a relation requires two distinct things (shay'an mutamayyizan), and distinctness requires each to subsist in some way. Since the known (e.g., a negation) does not exist externally, it must exist in the mind. Thus the mental image is the quiddity of the known, so knowledge and the known are identical in essence. Therefore, the mental form must be what is described by universality. The claim that universality belongs to the known, not the form, is invalidated.

Key Terms
نفي محض
عدم صرف
نسبة
تماييز
اتحاد
كلية
صورة عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality can be predicated of the known thing independently of the mental form.
Correction: The argument shows that if the known were distinct from the mental form, it would have to exist externally, making it particular. Since the known does not exist externally, it must be the mental form, so the mental form is what is universal.

Checkpoint
According to the objection in this passage, why must the mental form be what is described by universality?
A) Because mental forms are simpler than external objects.
B) Because if the known were distinct from the mental form, it would have to exist externally, making it a particular; and if they are identical, the mental form is universal.
C) Because the philosophers said so without proof.
D) Because universality is a property of the intellect, not of objects.

Key Takeaway
The identity of knowledge and known forces universality upon the mental form, refuting the second group's view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about something that has no external existence—like a fictional character or an abstract concept—do you ever consider that your knowledge of it might be identical to that mental object? How does this affect your sense of what is truly 'out there' versus what is constructed in your mind?
?
If universality resides only in the mind, what does that mean for the truths you hold as universal and objective? Are they dependent on your mental framework? How does this challenge you to reexamine your own assumptions about certainty and knowledge?
?
This passage argues that knowledge and the known become identical. Can you identify a moment in your own learning where the act of knowing something transformed your perception of that thing so deeply that it felt like the two became one? What does that experience reveal about the nature of your faith?

The Necessity of External Existence for the Distinct Known
Sanusi presents the logical consequences of the known being distinct from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if the known is distinct from knowledge, it must have external existence, making it a particular and therefore impossible to be universal.

Core Idea
Distinct knowns are external particulars, not universals.

The passage presents a crucial dichotomy: either the known is identical to the mental form (and thus universal) or it is distinct (and thus must exist externally, making it a particular). This lesson explores the first branch, showing why the opponent's position leads to the known being a particular, which contradicts their claim of universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the condition
The condition is that the known (al-ma'lum) is different from knowledge (al-'ilm) and beyond what is in the mind.
3
Trace the consequence
If it is not in the mind, it must be in the external world to have subsistence. That makes it a particular (shakhs), which cannot be universal.
External existence = determinate individual, contradicting universality.
4
Contrast with the alternative
If the known is identical to the mental form, then the mental form itself is universal. So universality cannot be transferred to a distinct entity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that it is incorrect to negate universality from mental forms and affirm it for the known as distinct from them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كنت تحتاج هاهنا إلى زيادة بيان فاستمع لما يتلى عليك أليس إذا كان المعلوم مغايراً للعلم و أمراً وراء ما في الذهن كان حصوله أي حصول المعلوم وثبوته في الخارج لأنه لا بد من ثبوته في الجملة ليتصور تحقق النسبة بينه وبين العالم، وإذ ليس ثبوته في الذهن كان في الخارج قطعاً فيكون شخصاً أي موجوداً في الخارج متعيناً في حد نفسه متأصلاً في الوجود وهو ينافي الكلية فإذا كان المعلوم مغايراً للعلم لم يتصف بالكلية أصلاً وإذا اتحدا كانت الصورة العقلية متصفة بالكلية فلا يصح نفي الكلية عن الصور وإثباتها للمعلوم بها اللهم إلا أن يصار إلى أن الأمور المتصورة لها ارتسام في غير العقل الإنساني من القوى العاقلة ارتساما عقلياً ظلياً لا كارتسام الأعراض في محلها بحسب الوجود الخارجي وإلا كانت تلك الأمور المتصورة أشخاصاً عينية يستحيل اتصافها بالكلية وهو أي الارتسام في غير العقل ينافي الوجود الذهني في النفس الناطقة الإنسانية لابتنائه على أن لا يكون لما تصورته النفس الناطقة ثبوت في غيرها لا أصيلياً ولا ظلياً، وهو أعني نفي الوجود الذهني خلاف مذهبهم على أنا نقول : المرتسم في سائر القوى العاقلة يجب أن
And if you need here additional clarification, then listen to what is recited to you: 'Is it not the case that if the known is different from knowledge and something beyond what is in the mind, then its occurrence — i.e., the occurrence and subsistence of the known — must be in the external world' — because its subsistence in some way is necessary for the relation between it and the knower to be realized, and since its subsistence is not in the mind, it is necessarily in the external world — so it becomes an individual, i.e., an existent in the external world, determinate in itself, well-founded in existence, and this contradicts universality. Thus, if the known is different from knowledge, it is not described by universality at all; and if they are identical, then the mental form is described by universality. So it is not correct to negate universality from the forms and affirm it for the known by them, unless one goes so far as to say that the conceived things have a representation in something other than the human intellect, among the intellective faculties, a mental shadowy representation, not like the inherence of accidents in their loci according to external existence; otherwise, those conceived things would be external individuals impossible to be described by universality. And this — i.e., the representation in other than the intellect — contradicts mental existence in the human rational soul, because it is based on the proposition that what the human rational soul conceives has no subsistence in anything else, neither originary nor shadowy. And this — i.e., the negation of mental existence — is contrary to their doctrine. However, we say: What is represented in other intellective faculties must ...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the second group's position. It begins by restating the objection: if the known (al-ma'lum) is distinct from knowledge (al-'ilm), then it must have subsistence (thubut) in the external world (al-kharij) in order for a relation between it and the knower to be possible. Since it is not in the mind, it is necessarily external, and thus it is an individual (shakhs) — determinate, well-founded in existence — which contradicts universality (kulliyya). Therefore, if the known is distinct from knowledge, it cannot be universal; if they are identical, the mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyya) is universal. The author then considers an exception: unless one posits that conceived things have a representation (irtisam) in something other than the human intellect, among the intellective faculties (al-quwa al-'aqila), a mental shadowy representation (irtisaman 'aqliyyạn zilliyạn), not like the inherence of accidents in their loci. Otherwise, those conceived things would be external individuals (ashkhas 'ayniyya) and cannot be universal. However, this representation in other than the human intellect contradicts mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) in the human rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa al-insaniyya), because it implies that what the soul conceives has no subsistence in anything else — neither originary (asili) nor shadowy (zilli). And negating mental existence is contrary to their doctrine. The passage ends mid-sentence with "we say: What is represented in other intellective faculties must..." suggesting the argument continues, but the point is clear: the only way to salvage the distinctness of the known from the mental image is to posit representation in other intellects, but that undermines mental existence as understood by the philosophers.

الشخص
العلم
المعلوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The known can be distinct from the mental image yet still be universal.
Correction: The passage shows that if the known is distinct, it must exist externally and thus be a particular, which cannot be universal.

Checkpoint

A) In the mind only
B) In the external world
C) In both mind and external world
D) In neither

Key Takeaway
If the known is distinct from the mental form, it must be an external particular, making universality impossible.

The Exception of Representation in Other Intellects and Its Problem
Sanusi considers and refutes the exception of representation in other intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the only possible exception to the rule that a distinct known must be a particular—representation in other intellective faculties—and why it conflicts with the doctrine of mental existence in the human soul.

Core Idea
Representation in other intellects is the only way to save universality, but it denies mental existence.

After establishing that a distinct known must be an external particular, the author considers an exception: what if the conceived things have a representation in other intellective faculties beyond the human mind? This lesson examines that alternative and shows that it undermines the philosophical doctrine of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the exception
Focus on the part starting with 'unless one goes so far' to the end of the excerpt.
2
Understand the exception
The only way to avoid the known being an external particular is to say it has representation in other intellective faculties, like the Active Intellect.
This representation is described as 'mental shadowy'.
3
See why it is proposed
Without this exception, the conceived things would be external individuals impossible to be universal.
4
Identify the contradiction
Representation in other intellects contradicts mental existence in the human rational soul, because it implies the soul's concepts have no subsistence elsewhere.
5
Note the doctrinal conflict
Negating mental existence is contrary to the philosophers' own doctrine, so the exception is self-defeating.

Source Text

The Core Argument: Universality Belongs to Intellectual Forms
Al-Jurjani presents the core argument for the universality of intellectual forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that intellectual forms become universal when united with knowledge, and why external objects cannot be universal.

Core Idea
External objects are particulars; universality is a property of the mental form when joined with knowledge.

This lesson presents the central argument of the philosophers regarding intellectual forms. They reason that if the known object is separate from knowledge and exists externally, it must be a particular individual, and thus cannot be universal. However, if the known and knowledge are united, the intellectual form acquires universality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of concepts and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by an exception.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: when the known is distinct from knowledge (leading to particularity) and when they are united (leading to universality). Find these in the text.
3
Examine the exception
The passage introduces an exception with 'اللهم إلا'. What is this exception? It posits that the forms might have an impression in separate intellects. Note how it is presented as a potential counterexample.
This exception will be refuted in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this argument matters: it resolves the problem of how universals exist by locating them in the mental realm united with knowledge.
This connects to the realism versus nominalism debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية ( وإن كنت تحتاج هاهنا ) إلى زيادة بيان فاستمع لما يتلى عليك ( أليس إذا كان المعلوم) مغايراً للعلم و ( أمراً وراء ما في الذهن كان حصوله) أي حصول المعلوم وثبوته ( في الخارج ) لأنه لا بد من ثبوته في الجملة ليتصور تحقق النسبة بينه وبين العالم، وإذ ليس ثبوته في الذهن كان في الخارج قطعاً ( فيكون شخصاً) أي موجوداً في الخارج متعيناً في حد نفسه الوجود الذهني خلاف مذهبهم على أنا نقول : المرتسم في سائر القوى العاقلة يجب أن يكون نفس ماهيات المعلومات حتى تصدق الأحكام الإيجابية الجارية عليها وتتحقق النسبة بينها وبين العالم بها، وإذ لم يكن ارتسامها فيها عينياً كان ارتسامها علمياً ويتحد العلم والمعلوم هناك، وتكون المعلومات متصفة بالكلية حال اتحادها بالعلم، وهو المطلوب. هكذا حقق المقال على هذا النسق وذر الذين لا يعلمون في خوضهم يلعبون . قوله : ( وهي الصورة (العقلية لا المعلوم إذ قد لا يكون له وجود في الخارج وإذا اعتبر من حيث وجوده في الذهن فهو الصورة العقلية. قوله : (لم يتصف ) أي بالكلية أصلاً لا في الخارج لكونه شخصاً فيه ولا في الذهن إذ الموجود في الذهن الأشباح. قوله : ( وهو أي الارتسام في غير العقل أي العقل الإنساني ينافي الوجود الذهني إشارة إلى أنهم لا يقولون بالارتسام في المفارقات، بل بالعقل البسيط على ما مر في كلام الشيخ، وكذا لا ما نتصوره في النفوس الفلكية، وما سبق في بحث الوجود الذهني من يقولون بارتسام جميع الارتسام في العقل الفعال وهو الوجود الذهني، فإنا لا نعني به إلا الوجود الظلي سواء كان في القوى القاصرة أو العالية فمبني على فرض الارتسام فيه . من أن أي كونها سبباً لانكشاف الماهية، فلعل الحكم بالبطلان بناء على المتبادر منه، وتعين أن القائل به هو الفرقة الثانية، وإن لم يكن ذلك التعين من هذا الكلام نفسه بل من اقتضاء السياق فيما وقع فيه مثلاً، والله أعلم. قوله : ( لابتنائه على أن لا يكون إلخ) فيه أن هذا خلاف ما اشتهر من مذهب مثبتي الوجود الذهني، فإن صور جميع المعلومات مرتسمة عندهم في العقل الفعال ولذا جعلوه خزانة للنفس الناطقة، وأن المراد بالوجود الذهني، وجود مغاير للوجود الذي هو مصدر الآثار، سواء كان في قوتنا المدركة أو غيرها، وكان كلام المصنف مبني على أن علم المجردات عندهم حضوري لا ارتسامي .
The second type - the sixth purpose: The statement of the philosophers concerning intellectual forms. (And if you need here) an additional explanation, then listen to what is recited to you: (Does not, if the known) is distinct from knowledge and (a thing beyond what is in the mind, its occurrence) i.e., the occurrence and subsistence of the known (in the external world) because its subsistence in general is necessary for the realization of the relation between it and the knower, and since its subsistence is not in the mind, it is necessarily in the external world, (so it becomes a particular) i.e., an existent in the external world, determined in itself, rooted in existence, (and it contradicts universality). So if the known is distinct from knowledge, it is not characterized by universality at all; and if they are united, the intellectual form is characterized by universality, so it is not correct to negate universality from the forms and affirm it for the known through them. (Unless one turns to the view that the conceived things have an impression [irtisām] in something other than the human intellect, from among the rational faculties, as a shadowy intellectual impression, not like the impression of accidents in their locus according to external existence; otherwise those conceived things would be individual entities impossible to be characterized by universality). (And it) i.e., the impression in something other than the intellect (contradicts mental existence [al-wujūd al-dhihnī]) in the human rational soul, because it is based on the premise that what the rational soul conceives has no subsistence in anything else, neither originally nor shadowy; and this—I mean the negation of mental existence—is contrary to their doctrine. Yet we say: what is impressed in all rational faculties must be the very essences of the known things so that the affirmative judgments applying to them are true and the relation between them and the knower is realized; and since their impression in them is not [individual], their impression is intellectual, and knowledge and the known become united there, and the known things are characterized by universality when they are united with knowledge—and that is what is sought. Thus the discourse was verified in this manner, and leave those who do not know to play in their frivolity. He said: (And it is the intellectual form, not the known, for the known may have no existence in the external world, and when considered in terms of its existence in the mind, it is the intellectual form). He said: (it is not characterized) i.e., by universality at all, neither in the external world because it is a particular there, nor in the mind, since what exists in the mind are phantasms. He said: (And it) i.e., the impression in something other than the intellect—i.e., the human intellect—contradicts mental existence, indicating that they do not affirm impression in separate intellects, but rather [affirm] simple intellect as mentioned in the Shaykh's discourse; similarly, not what we conceive in celestial souls; and what preceded in the discussion of mental existence, that those who affirm the impression of all things in the Active Intellect and that it is mental existence—for we mean by it only shadowy existence, whether in lower or higher faculties—it is based on the assumption of impression in it. (From that) i.e., because it is a cause for the disclosure of the essence; perhaps the judgment of falsity is based on the immediate understanding, and it is determined that the one who holds it is the second group, even if that determination is not from this discourse itself but from the requirement of the context in which it occurred, for example, and God knows best. He said: (based on the premise that there is not, etc.) in it [there is discussion] that this is contrary to what is well-known of the doctrine of the affirmers of mental existence, for the forms of all known things are impressed in the Active Intellect according to them, and thus they made it a treasury for the rational soul, and that what is meant by mental existence is an existence distinct from the existence that is the source of effects, whether in our perceptive faculty or others. And the author's statement was based on the view that the knowledge of separate things according to them is presence [ḥuḍūrī], not impressionistic.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of intellectual forms and universality, presented as part of the sixth purpose of the second type. The author (al-Jurjani) begins by quoting a hypothetical question: if the known is distinct from knowledge and exists externally, then it must be a particular individual, contradicting universality. Therefore, if the known is distinct from knowledge, it cannot be universal; if they are united, the intellectual form (the mental image) becomes universal. This is the core claim: universality belongs to the form when united with knowledge, not to the external object. The author then introduces an exception: unless one holds that the conceived objects have an impression (irtisām) in something other than the human intellect—a shadowy intellectual impression—in which case they would still be particular, contradicting universality. This impression (in other rational faculties) would negate the mental existence in the human soul, which is required for the self-sufficiency of the mental image. The author argues against this exception, stating that the impressed forms in all rational faculties must be identical to the essences of the known things for judgments to be true and for the relation to be realized. Since the impression is not individual (aynī), but intellectual (ilmī), knowledge and the known become united, and the known things are characterized by universality in that state of union. This is the desired conclusion. The author then includes glosses: the first distinguishes the intellectual form from the external known; the second clarifies that the known is not characterized by universality anywhere; the third explains that the impression in non-human intellects contradicts mental existence and discusses the views of the philosophers on the Active Intellect and simple intellect; the fourth discusses a point about the disclosure of essences; and the fifth addresses a common objection about mental existence, arguing that mental existence includes both lower and higher faculties. Throughout, the author defends the unity of knowledge and known as the basis for universality.

Key Terms
الصور العقلية
الكلية
الوجود الذهني
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality belongs to external objects themselves.
Correction: The argument shows that external objects are particulars; universality arises only in the mind when the form is united with knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the known is distinct from knowledge and exists externally, what property does it have?
A) Universality
B) Particularity
C) Both universality and particularity
D) Neither universality nor particularity

Key Takeaway
For the philosophers, universality is a characteristic of the intellectual form when it is identical with knowledge, not of external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a universal concept like 'truth', do you instinctively imagine it as something 'out there' in the world, or as an idea in your mind? This lesson argues that universality only exists in the union of knower and known. How does this challenge your usual assumption about where truth resides, and what does it demand from you in your pursuit of knowledge?
?
Consider a moment when you truly understood a deep concept—it became clear and applicable to many situations. According to this argument, that moment was when your knowledge and the known became one. Do you seek such unity in your learning, or do you settle for detached information? What would change if you aimed for that kind of intellectual union?
?
If external objects are mere particulars and cannot be universal, then the general principles you live by—justice, mercy, piety—must be rooted in your mind's act. Are those principles merely subjective projections, or do they correspond to something real? This lesson pushes you to decide: will you ground your convictions in external particulars or in the intellectual forms that emerge from your engagement with truth?

Refuting the Exception: Impression and Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani refutes the impression theory and defends the unity of knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' refutation of the view that intellectual forms are merely impressions in separate intellects, and how they affirm mental existence in the human soul.

Core Idea
The impression in separate intellects still implies mental existence and does not negate universality.

After presenting the core argument, the author addresses an exception: what if the forms are just impressions in higher intellects? He argues that even this impression does not escape mental existence, and that the impressed forms must be identical to the essences of things for judgments to hold. This lesson unpacks that counterargument and the ensuing discussion.

Guided Path
1
Focus on the exception and the author's response
Read the Arabic passage from '(وهو) أي الارتسام' onwards. The author states that such impression contradicts mental existence. Identify the core claims.
2
Understand the key point about the nature of the impression
The author says that the impression in all rational faculties must be of the essences themselves and must be intellectual (ilmī), not individual (aynī). This allows universality.
This is the heart of the refutation.
3
Note the discussion about mental existence
The passage includes remarks about the Active Intellect and mental existence. The author argues that mental existence includes shadowy existence in any faculty, whether lower or higher.
This broadens the definition of mental existence.
4
Reflect on the final remarks
The author ends by noting that the discourse is consistent and that the view of mental existence in the Active Intellect is well-known. Consider how this defends the original argument.

الذهن إذ الموجود في الذهن الأشباح. قوله : ( وهو أي الارتسام في غير العقل أي العقل الإنساني ينافي الوجود الذهني إشارة إلى أنهم لا يقولون بالارتسام في المفارقات، بل بالعقل البسيط على ما مر في كلام ?

?

The Universal Concept as a Tool for Knowing Many
The author explains how a universal concept can serve as a tool to know many particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single universal concept can serve as a tool for knowing many particular individuals, resolving the objection that one knowledge cannot be of many things.

Core Idea
A universal concept can be a mirror to see many individuals at once.

A key objection in epistemology is how one piece of knowledge can cover multiple things. The author here provides a crisp answer: the universal concept is not known for its own sake but is used as a tool to observe its individuals. This distinction protects the unity of knowledge while allowing it to apply to many.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
The passage opens by restating an objection: how can one knowledge be of many things? Underline this statement.
2
Notice the two ways to consider a universal concept
The author explains that a universal concept can be considered in itself or as a tool/mirror for its individuals. Highlight these two modes.
3
Understand the tool metaphor
The intellect uses the concept like a tool to observe the individuals. This allows a single concept to give knowledge of many particulars without having multiple knowledges.
4
Apply the lesson
The author ends by saying these meanings will benefit you in many places. Reflect on how this principle applies to other theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عارض من عوارض الجواب . قلنا الكلام فيما إذا كان المركب حاصلا في الذهن بحقيقته، لا باعتبار عارض من عوارضه، فإن ذلك ليس علماً بأجزائه لا تفصيلاً ولا إجمالاً، وأما قوله : العلم الواحد لا يكون علماً بمعلومات كثيرة، فجوابه : أنا إذا قلنا : كل شيء فهو ممكن بالإمكان العام فلا شك أنا حكمنا على جميع أفراد الشيء، فلا بد أن تكون معلومة لنا، ولا علم بها في هذه الحالة إلا باعتبار مفهوم الشيء الشامل لها بأسرها، فإن العقل جعل هذا المفهوم آلة لملاحظة تلك الأفراد حتى أمكنه الحكم عليها، وتلخيصه : إن المفهوم الكلي قد يلاحظ في نفسه، وبهذه الملاحظة يمكن الحكم عليه لا على أفراده وقد يجعل آلة ومرآة لملاحظة أفراده، فيصح حينئذ أن يحكم على تلك الأفراد ،دونه ولتكن هذه المعاني التي قررناها مضبوطة عندك، فإنها تنفعك في مواضع عديدة
A subordinate of the accident of the answer. We say: The discussion is when the composite is present in the mind by its essence, not considering an accident of its accidents, for that is not knowledge of its parts, neither in detail nor in summary. As for his statement: 'One knowledge cannot be knowledge of many things', its answer is: If we say 'Everything is possible in the general sense of possibility', there is no doubt that we have judged all individuals of the thing, so they must be known to us, and there is no knowledge of them in this state except by virtue of the concept of the thing encompassing all of them, for the intellect made this concept a tool for observing those individuals until it became possible to judge them. Its summary: The universal concept may be observed in itself, and by this observation it is possible to judge it, not its individuals; and it may be made a tool and mirror for observing its individuals, and then it is valid to judge those individuals without it. And let these meanings that we have established be preserved by you, for they benefit you in many places.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the objection that one knowledge cannot be of many things. The author first distinguishes between knowing a composite by its essence versus by an accident, stating that only the former counts as knowledge of parts. He then addresses the specific objection: when we judge that 'everything is possible', we actually judge all individuals of 'thing', but this knowledge occurs solely through the universal concept (mafhum kulli) that encompasses them all. The intellect uses this concept as a tool (ala) to observe the individuals, enabling the judgment. He summarizes that the universal concept can be considered in itself (in which case judgment applies to the concept, not its individuals) or as a tool and mirror for observing its individuals (in which case judgment validly applies to the individuals). The passage concludes by advising the reader to preserve these meanings, as they will prove useful in many places.

Key Terms
عارض (accident)
مركب (composite)
مفهوم كلي (universal concept)
آلة (tool)
ملاحظة (observation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a universal concept directly gives knowledge of all its individuals without any mediation.
Correction: The author clarifies that the universal concept is used as a tool (ala) to observe the individuals; the knowledge is indirect, mediated by the intellect's use of the concept.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the intellect enable knowledge of many individuals through a single concept?
A) By considering the concept in itself
B) By making the concept a tool to observe the individuals
C) By ignoring the individuals entirely
D) By creating multiple concepts for each individual

Key Takeaway
One universal concept can be used as a tool to know many individuals, solving the problem of one knowledge covering many.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a general statement like 'All humans are mortal,' do you truly see each individual you are judging? How does using a universal concept as a tool free you to judge rightly without needing to picture every person? Will you use this insight to ensure your judgments are based on universal truths rather than limited personal experiences?
?
The author urges you to preserve these meanings. Do you often forget deeper truths? How can you make this understanding a part of your thinking so that it benefits you in many situations? Commit to writing down one example from your daily life where you can apply this tool of the universal concept.
?
Have you ever dismissed a universal truth because you focused on a few exceptions? Reflect on how using the universal concept as a mirror for individuals might change your perspective on that issue. What will you do the next time you are tempted to reject a general rule because of outliers?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
Al-Qadi's Thesis and the Metaphorical Interpretation
al-Qadi enumerates the ways known and unknown relate as distinct objects and declares the unity description metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi al-Baqillani's argument that a single thing cannot be literally both known and unknown; such descriptions are metaphorical.

Core Idea
Known and unknown are necessarily distinct objects, even when called 'one thing'.

Al-Qadi opens by stating the logical rule: the known and unknown are necessarily two different things. To explain why we often say a thing is known in one respect and unknown in another, he lists three possible relations between the objects—one being an accident of the other, both being accidents of a third, or other relations like part-whole—and concludes that calling them 'one thing' is merely metaphorical. This lesson unpacks that rule and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the author's argument flow. Then compare with the translation.
The passage states the well-known position (المشهور) before al-Qadi's decisive statement.
2
Identify the three relations
Find the three ways al-Qadi says the known and unknown can be related while remaining distinct. They are: one accident of the other, both accidents of a third, and other relations (like part-whole, connection, contiguity).
Each relation preserves the distinctness of the objects of knowledge and ignorance.
3
Understand the metaphorical claim
Al-Qadi says that calling these cases 'one thing known and unknown' is metaphorical (مجاز). Recognize that the metaphor does not violate the logical distinctness of knowledge and ignorance.
The metaphor is permissible (بابه مفتوح), so there is no dispute about using it figuratively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : المشهور أنَّ الشيء )الواحد (قد يكون معلوماً من وجه دون وجه، قال القاضي:) الباقلاني ( المعلوم غير المجهول ضرورة فمتعلق العلم والجهل شيئان ) متغایران قطعاً ( وإن كان أحدهما عارضاً للآخر) كما إذا علم الإنسان باعتبار ضاحكيته وجهل باعتبار حقيقته (أو هما عارضان لثالث) كما إذا علم باعتبار ضحكه وجهل باعتبار كتابته ) أو بينهما تعلق آخر سوى تعلق العروض على أحد الوجهين (أي تعلق التعلقات كالجزئية والكلية والاتصال والمجاورة فإن هذه التعلقات لا تقتضي اتحاد المعلوم والمجهول بل تغايرما ) والتسمية مجاز ) يعني أنه إذا كان المعلوم عارضاً للمجهول، أو كانا عارضين لثالث، أو كان بينهما تعلق بوجه آخر وأطلق على هذه الصور أنها من قبيل كون الشيء الواحد معلوماً من وجه، ومجهولاً من وجه آخر، كان هذا الإطلاق من باب التجوز ) ولا مشاحة ) ولا منازعة ( فيه ) أي في الإطلاق مجازاً فإن بابه مفتوح، ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه لك أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه فيكون العارض معلوماً مع كون حقيقة الشيء مجهولة فيتغاير المعلوم والمجهول، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظة الشيء، وحينئذ يكون ذلك الشيء معلوماً باعتبار عارضه ومجهولاً باعتبار حقيقته فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى ولا استحالة فيه، وبمثل هذا الذي ذكره القاضي استدل الإمام الرازي على نفي العلم الإجمالي في المحصل . فقال : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه والوجهان متغايران والوجه المعلوم لا إجمال فيه والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء قوله : (قال القاضي المعلوم غير المجهول) قيل : يلزم على القاضي حينئذ أن لا يقول باكتساب التصورات بجريان الوجه الأول المار ذكره من متمسكي الإمام مع أنه قائل به . قوله: (أو هما عارضان لثالث قيل : هذا العارض ليس بمعنى الخارج المحمول والا فالضاحك عارض للكاتب بل بمعنى العام ولك أن تقول : عروض الضاحك لذات الكاتب لا لمفهومه الذي كلامه فيه فتأمل قوله : ( ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه اعتراض على القاضي وما أسلفه هو الذي ذكره في الموقف الأول في جوابه استدلال الإمام على امتناع جريان الكسب في التصورات .
The second branch: What is well-known is that a single thing may be known in one respect and not in another. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) said: The known and the unknown are necessarily two different things; thus the objects of knowledge and ignorance are two distinct things, even if one is an accident of the other, such as when a human is known by virtue of his laughing quality and unknown by virtue of his essence, or they are both accidents of a third, such as when he is known by virtue of his laughter and unknown by virtue of his writing, or there is another relation between them besides the relation of accidentalness in one of the two aspects (i.e., relations such as part-whole, connection, contiguity – for these relations do not necessitate the unity of the known and the unknown but rather their diversity). And the naming is metaphorical, meaning that if the known is an accident of the unknown, or they are two accidents of a third, or there is another relation between them, and these cases are described as being of the type 'the single thing is known in one respect and unknown in another', this description is metaphorical – and there is no dispute or objection in that (i.e., in the metaphorical description, for its gate is open). And let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned: that the accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident is known while the essence of the thing is unknown, thus the known and the unknown are distinct; and it may be made a tool for observing the thing, and then that thing is known by virtue of its accident and unknown by virtue of its essence, so the known and the unknown become one, but it is known in one respect and unknown in another, and there is no impossibility in that. And by something similar to what al-Qadi mentioned, Imam al-Razi argued for the negation of summary knowledge in al-Muhassal. He said: That which is known summarily is known in one respect and unknown in another, and the two respects are distinct; the known respect has no summary character, and the unknown respect is not known at all; but because they come together in a thing... His statement: (Al-Qadi said: the known is not the unknown). It is said: It would then be necessary for al-Qadi not to affirm the acquisition of concepts according to the first aspect mentioned before from the partisans of the Imam, while he does affirm it. His statement: (Or they are two accidents of a third). It is said: This accident is not in the sense of an external attribute; otherwise laughter would be an accident of the writer; rather, it is in the sense of the general, and you may say: the occurrence of laughter to the essence of the writer, not to its concept, as the discussion is about that – so ponder. His statement: (And let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned) is an objection against al-Qadi, and what he previously mentioned is what he stated in the first standpoint in answer to the Imam's argument on the impossibility of the acquisition of concepts.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is the second branch, addressing whether a single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani asserts that the known and the unknown are necessarily two different things; thus if there appears to be one thing both known and unknown, the objects are actually distinct, even if one is an accident of the other, both are accidents of a third, or related in other ways (part-whole, connection, contiguity). Calling such cases 'one thing known and unknown' is metaphorical (majaz). The author then explains a subtle case: the accident of a thing can be observed in itself (making the accident known and the essence unknown) or can be used as a tool (ala) to observe the thing (making the thing known by its accident but unknown by its essence, resulting in the known and the unknown being the same object but under different aspects). Imam al-Razi used this to argue against summary knowledge. The passage concludes with two objections: one regarding al-Qadi's position on acquisition of concepts (iktisab al-tasawwurat) and another clarifying the meaning of 'accident' (arid) in this context. The author also notes that the remark 'let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned' is an objection against al-Qadi.

Key Terms
وجه (aspect)
عارض (accident)
مجاز (metaphor)
معلوم (known)
مجهول (unknown)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a single thing can be literally both known and unknown at the same time in the same respect.
Correction: Al-Qadi shows that the known and unknown are always distinct objects; any appearance of unity is metaphorical and involves different aspects or relations.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), what is the relationship between the known and the unknown when a single thing is said to be known in one respect and unknown in another?
A) They are the same object
B) They are necessarily two different things
C) They are synonyms
D) They are both accidents

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi insists that known and unknown are always distinct objects; calling them 'one thing' is metaphorical and allowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when you say 'I know God is merciful but I don’t understand His decree', are you treating mercy and decree as two different objects? How does al-Qadi’s insistence that known and unknown are necessarily distinct force you to examine your own assumptions about God’s unity?
?
Have you ever found comfort in saying 'I know this but not that' about the same thing, as a way to avoid deeper reflection? How might this lesson challenge you to confront the limits of your knowledge more honestly?
?
If you truly believe that the known and unknown are two distinct things, how should that change the way you speak about your own faith and doubts? Are you willing to stop using the phrase 'I know and I don't know’ loosely?

Two Ways of Considering Accidents and Al-Razi's Argument
The author distinguishes two roles of accidents in knowledge, then connects to al-Razi's argument and raises objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways accidents function in knowledge (as objects or tools) and how al-Razi used this to argue against summary knowledge.

Core Idea
Accidents can make a single essence known in one respect and unknown in another.

The previous lesson established that 'one thing known and unknown' is metaphorical when objects are distinct. Here, the author introduces a deeper nuance: the same accident can be considered either as an object of knowledge itself or as a tool to know the essence. This allows a single thing to be both known and unknown without contradiction—a point Imam al-Razi exploited to deny summary knowledge. This lesson unpacks that subtlety and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two ways
Focus on the sentences explaining how an accident can be observed in itself or used as a tool. Note the difference in outcome: in the first case the known and unknown are distinct; in the second they are unified under different aspects.
The key phrase is 'قد يلاحظ في نفسه' vs 'يجعل آلة'.
2
Understand al-Razi's inference
Al-Razi used the same logic to argue that summary knowledge (العلم الإجمالي) is impossible: the known aspect has no summary character, and the unknown aspect is not known at all.
This shows how the discussion relates to broader kalām debates on knowledge.
3
Examine the objections
The passage ends with two objections: one concerning al-Qadi's stance on acquisition of concepts, and another clarifying the meaning of 'accident' here. Consider how these objections fit the argument.
The author notes that the remark 'let it not be obscure to you' is itself an objection to al-Qadi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : المشهور أنَّ الشيء )الواحد (قد يكون معلوماً من وجه دون وجه، قال القاضي:) الباقلاني ( المعلوم غير المجهول ضرورة فمتعلق العلم والجهل شيئان ) متغایران قطعاً ( وإن كان أحدهما عارضاً للآخر) كما إذا علم الإنسان باعتبار ضاحكيته وجهل باعتبار حقيقته (أو هما عارضان لثالث) كما إذا علم باعتبار ضحكه وجهل باعتبار كتابته ) أو بينهما تعلق آخر سوى تعلق العروض على أحد الوجهين (أي تعلق التعلقات كالجزئية والكلية والاتصال والمجاورة فإن هذه التعلقات لا تقتضي اتحاد المعلوم والمجهول بل تغايرما ) والتسمية مجاز ) يعني أنه إذا كان المعلوم عارضاً للمجهول، أو كانا عارضين لثالث، أو كان بينهما تعلق بوجه آخر وأطلق على هذه الصور أنها من قبيل كون الشيء الواحد معلوماً من وجه، ومجهولاً من وجه آخر، كان هذا الإطلاق من باب التجوز ) ولا مشاحة ) ولا منازعة ( فيه ) أي في الإطلاق مجازاً فإن بابه مفتوح، ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه لك أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه فيكون العارض معلوماً مع كون حقيقة الشيء مجهولة فيتغاير المعلوم والمجهول، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظة الشيء، وحينئذ يكون ذلك الشيء معلوماً باعتبار عارضه ومجهولاً باعتبار حقيقته فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى ولا استحالة فيه، وبمثل هذا الذي ذكره القاضي استدل الإمام الرازي على نفي العلم الإجمالي في المحصل . فقال : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه والوجهان متغايران والوجه المعلوم لا إجمال فيه والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء قوله : (قال القاضي المعلوم غير المجهول) قيل : يلزم على القاضي حينئذ أن لا يقول باكتساب التصورات بجريان الوجه الأول المار ذكره من متمسكي الإمام مع أنه قائل به . قوله: (أو هما عارضان لثالث قيل : هذا العارض ليس بمعنى الخارج المحمول والا فالضاحك عارض للكاتب بل بمعنى العام ولك أن تقول : عروض الضاحك لذات الكاتب لا لمفهومه الذي كلامه فيه فتأمل قوله : ( ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه اعتراض على القاضي وما أسلفه هو الذي ذكره في الموقف الأول في جوابه استدلال الإمام على امتناع جريان الكسب في التصورات .
The second branch: What is well-known is that a single thing may be known in one respect and not in another. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) said: The known and the unknown are necessarily two different things; thus the objects of knowledge and ignorance are two distinct things, even if one is an accident of the other, such as when a human is known by virtue of his laughing quality and unknown by virtue of his essence, or they are both accidents of a third, such as when he is known by virtue of his laughter and unknown by virtue of his writing, or there is another relation between them besides the relation of accidentalness in one of the two aspects (i.e., relations such as part-whole, connection, contiguity – for these relations do not necessitate the unity of the known and the unknown but rather their diversity). And the naming is metaphorical, meaning that if the known is an accident of the unknown, or they are two accidents of a third, or there is another relation between them, and these cases are described as being of the type 'the single thing is known in one respect and unknown in another', this description is metaphorical – and there is no dispute or objection in that (i.e., in the metaphorical description, for its gate is open). And let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned: that the accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident is known while the essence of the thing is unknown, thus the known and the unknown are distinct; and it may be made a tool for observing the thing, and then that thing is known by virtue of its accident and unknown by virtue of its essence, so the known and the unknown become one, but it is known in one respect and unknown in another, and there is no impossibility in that. And by something similar to what al-Qadi mentioned, Imam al-Razi argued for the negation of summary knowledge in al-Muhassal. He said: That which is known summarily is known in one respect and unknown in another, and the two respects are distinct; the known respect has no summary character, and the unknown respect is not known at all; but because they come together in a thing... His statement: (Al-Qadi said: the known is not the unknown). It is said: It would then be necessary for al-Qadi not to affirm the acquisition of concepts according to the first aspect mentioned before from the partisans of the Imam, while he does affirm it. His statement: (Or they are two accidents of a third). It is said: This accident is not in the sense of an external attribute; otherwise laughter would be an accident of the writer; rather, it is in the sense of the general, and you may say: the occurrence of laughter to the essence of the writer, not to its concept, as the discussion is about that – so ponder. His statement: (And let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned) is an objection against al-Qadi, and what he previously mentioned is what he stated in the first standpoint in answer to the Imam's argument on the impossibility of the acquisition of concepts.

Commentary
This passage is the second branch, addressing whether a single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani asserts that the known and the unknown are necessarily two different things; thus if there appears to be one thing both known and unknown, the objects are actually distinct, even if one is an accident of the other, both are accidents of a third, or related in other ways (part-whole, connection, contiguity). Imam al-Razi used this to argue against summary knowledge. The passage concludes with two objections: one regarding al-Qadi's position on acquisition of concepts (iktisab al-tasawwurat) and another clarifying the meaning of 'accident' (arid) in this context. The author also notes that the remark 'let it not be obscure to you what we previously mentioned' is an objection against al-Qadi.

Key Terms
ملاحظة (observation)
آلة (tool)
حيثية (respect)
اكتساب (acquisition)
عام (general)

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the difference when an accident is observed in itself versus when it is used as a tool?
A) In the first case the known and unknown are unified; in the second they are distinct
B) In both cases the known and unknown are distinct
C) In the first case the known and unknown are distinct; in the second they are unified under different respects
D) There is no difference; both lead to the same result

Key Takeaway
An accident can either be known itself or serve as a tool to know the essence, allowing a single thing to be both known and unknown under different respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been satisfied with a vague, summary knowledge of a critical matter in your faith, thinking it was enough? This lesson shows that summary knowledge is not true knowledge. What specific truth about Allah or your religion have you left as a vague impression, and what will you do today to pursue clear, detailed understanding?
?
The accident can be a tool to know the thing. In your own life, what outward signs or actions (the 'accidents') are you using to gauge your inner faith (the 'essence')? Are you truly seeking to know the reality of your imaan, or are you content with the tool, avoiding the harder work of knowing the essence?
?
Al-Razi argued that the respects are distinct: you either know or you don't in each respect. Are there areas where you claim to know something but in truth you only know it in one respect while remaining ignorant in another? Will you commit to identifying one such area and actively study it until the unknown respect becomes known?

Marginal Notes on al-Qadi's View
Three marginal notes addressing objections to al-Baqillani and related points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three marginal notes that clarify al-Qadi's position regarding the acquisition of concepts and the meaning of 'accident' in theological arguments.

Core Idea
Marginal notes sharpen doctrinal precision by refuting objections and clarifying terminology.

This passage contains three critical marginal notes that respond to points in the main discussion. The first notes a consequence for al-Qadi's position, the second clarifies the meaning of 'accident,' and the third points to an earlier argument. These notes show how scholars refined their arguments through subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice how each note begins with 'قوله' referencing the main text.
2
Identify the three notes
Identify each marginal note: the first objects to al-Qadi on acquisition, the second clarifies 'accident', the third references an earlier argument.
3
Understand the first objection
The first note says if al-Qadi holds known/unknown distinct, he shouldn't accept acquisition of concepts from the first way; yet he does.
This is a logical consequence objection.
4
Grasp the second clarification
The second note says 'accident' here means general, not predicative external. Example: laughter is not an accident of writer in that sense.
This prevents a misunderstanding of the term.
5
Recognize the cross-reference
The third note points to an earlier argument in the first standing about impossibility of acquisition in conceptualizations.
This shows the interconnectedness of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قال القاضي المعلوم غير المجهول) قيل : يلزم على القاضي حينئذ أن لا يقول باكتساب التصورات بجريان الوجه الأول المار ذكره من متمسكي الإمام مع أنه قائل به . قوله: (أو هما عارضان لثالث قيل : هذا العارض ليس بمعنى الخارج المحمول والا فالضاحك عارض للكاتب بل بمعنى العام ولك أن تقول : عروض الضاحك لذات الكاتب لا لمفهومه الذي كلامه فيه فتأمل . قوله : ( ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه اعتراض على القاضي وما أسلفه هو الذي ذكره في الموقف الأول في جوابه استدلال الإمام على امتناع جريان الكسب في التصورات .
His statement: (Al-Qadi said: the known is other than the unknown) It was said: Then it follows for al-Qadi that he should not accept the acquisition of concepts according to the first way mentioned by the adherents of the Imam, while he affirms it. His statement: (Or both are accidents of a third) It was said: This 'accident' is not in the sense of a predicative external, otherwise 'laughter' would be an accident of 'writer', but rather in the sense of the general. And you may say: The occurrence of 'laughter' upon the essence of the writer, not upon his concept which is the subject of discussion; so reflect. His statement: (And let it not be confused by what we have presented) is an objection to al-Qadi, and what he presented is what he mentioned in the first standing in his answer to the Imam's argument for the impossibility of acquisition in conceptualizations.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage contains three marginal notes that respond to points in the main text. The first note objects: If al-Baqillani insists that the known and unknown are always distinct, then he should not accept the acquisition of concepts (iktisāb al-taṣawwurāt) from the first way mentioned by his opponents. The second note clarifies that the term 'accident' in the phrase 'both are accidents of a third' does not mean a predicative external accident; rather it means a general accident. For example, 'laughter' is not an accident of 'writer' in the predicative sense but occurs on the essence of the writer. The third note explains that the statement 'let it not be confused by what we have presented' is an objection to al-Qadi, referring back to an earlier argument in the first standing (al-mawqif al-awwal) regarding the impossibility of acquisition in conceptualizations.

Key Terms
العارض
العام
اكتساب التصورات
المتمسكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the marginal notes are part of the main text.
Correction: They are critical responses to the author's statements, not part of the main exposition.

Checkpoint
What does the second marginal note specify about the term 'accident' when saying 'both are accidents of a third'?
A) It means an external predicative accident.
B) It means a general accident, not a predicative one.
C) It refers to a necessary accident.
D) It refers to a conceptual accident.

Key Takeaway
Marginal notes in kalam literature are essential for clarifying objections and refining technical terms like 'accident'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a position, are you willing to scrutinize it for logical inconsistencies the way the commentator does al-Qadi’s view? What belief of yours have you recently examined and adjusted because of such scrutiny?
?
Scholars went to great lengths to clarify the precise meaning of a single term like 'accident.' Where in your own understanding of Islamic concepts is there ambiguity that you have been too comfortable to resolve? What will you do today to gain clarity?
