The Anatomy of Muscular Force: Nerve, Ligament, Muscle, and Tendon
Anatomical explanation of muscular force components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anatomical structures (nerve, ligament, muscle, tendon) that underlie the concept of muscular force in classical kalam physics.

Core Idea
Muscular strength is physically real, built from nerve, ligament, muscle, and tendon working together.

This passage dives into the physical building blocks of muscular power. Before we can discuss how force moves limbs, we need to know what a muscle is made of and how it connects to the nervous and skeletal systems. The commentary lays out the anatomy in careful detail, showing that kalam thinkers engaged with empirical science.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the term 'tark' (leaving)
Read the opening sentence explaining 'tark' as intentional non-act. Understand that it distinguishes eternal non-existence from deliberate leaving.
This is a key kalam distinction between 'adam (non-existence) and tark (leaving).
2
Identify the anatomical components
List the four key components: nerve (from brain/spine), ligament (from bone), muscle (composite), and tendon (connective).
Notice the hierarchical buildup: nerve + ligament → splinters → filled with flesh → membrane → muscle → tendon.
3
Understand the tendon's function
The tendon bulges when muscle contracts, pulling the limb. When muscle relaxes, tendon slackens and limb returns.
This is a mechanical description of antagonistic movement.
4
Integrate the additional commentary
The quote from al-Mulakhkhas confirms the nerve's origin and properties, and reiterates the muscle-tendon mechanism.
This shows consistency across different kalam sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتركه ) عطف تفسيري لعدم الفعل إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد منه عدم الفعل الأزلي بل تركه سواء فسر الترك بكف النفس أو بعدم الفعل قصداً . قوله: (وهي القوة الخ) اعلم أنه نبت من الدماغ والنخاع عصب ومن العظم الذي يراد حركة ،رباط، فإذا التقى العصب والرباط تشيا شظايا دقاقاً، ويحشى الفرج الواقعة بينهما لحماً آخر ويخلل الغشاء، ويسمى ذلك الجسم المركب من العصب والرباط الجسم الأحمر، والغشاء عضلة ثم ما يبرز من العصب والرباط ينتقل فيصير جسماً واحداً في الحس متوسطاً بين لين العصب، وصلابة الرباط ويسمى الوتر وفائدتها أن يتحدب عند عقاص العضلة فينقبض العضد المراد تحريك وليترخى عند انبساط عائدة إلى وصفها الأولى، أو زائدة منه على مقدارها في طولها حال كونها على وضع المطلوب فيه العضو. قوله : ( وهي القوة العضلية قال في بحث الحروف المصوتة من شرح الملخص: العصب جسم منبته الدماغ والنخاع وهو أبيض لين في الانعطاف صلب في الانفصال خلق ليتم به للأعضاء الحس والحركة والرباط جسم شبيه بالعصب، وإذا عرفت العصب والرباط فاعلم أن العضلة جرم مؤلف من العصب والرباط ومن ليفهما، ومن اللحم الحاشي في خللها والعشاء لها، إذا تصلب حدث الوتر وهو الملتئم من الرباط والعصب النافذ منها إلى جانب العضو وهي فيتشنج فتجذب العضو، وإذا انبسطت استرخى الوتر فتباعد العضو.
His statement: 'and leaving it' is an explanatory appositive to 'non-act', indicating that what is meant is not the eternal non-act but rather its leaving, whether leaving is interpreted as restraint of the self or as intentional non-act. His statement: 'and it is the strength...' Know that from the brain and spinal cord grows a nerve, and from the bone whose movement is intended grows a ligament. When the nerve and ligament meet, they become small splinters, and the gaps between them are filled with another piece of flesh, and the membrane is perforated. That composite body of nerve and ligament is called the red body, and the membrane is called a muscle. Then what protrudes from the nerve and ligament moves and becomes one body perceptually, intermediate between the softness of the nerve and the hardness of the ligament, and is called the tendon. Its benefit is that it bulges at the contraction of the muscle so that the limb intended for movement contracts, and it relaxes when it extends, returning to its first description, or increases from its measure in length when it is at the desired position of that limb. His statement: 'and it is the muscular strength.' He said in the discussion of vocal sounds from the commentary on al-Mulakhkhas: The nerve is a body whose origin is the brain and spinal cord, and it is white, pliable in bending, firm in splitting, created to provide for the limbs sensation and movement. The ligament is a body similar to the nerve. When you know the nerve and ligament, know that the muscle is a body composed of the nerve, the ligament, their fibers, the surrounding flesh in its interstices, and the membrane covering them. When it hardens, the tendon occurs, which is the composite of the ligament and nerve extending from it to the side of the limb. It (the muscle) contracts and pulls the limb, and when it relaxes, the tendon slackens and the limb moves away.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a detailed anatomical commentary explaining the terms related to muscular power (al-quwwa al-'adaliyya). It begins by clarifying the term 'tark' (leaving) as an explanatory appositive to 'adam al-fi'l' (non-act), specifying that it refers to the intentional non-act, not the eternal non-existence. Then it delves into the physical structure: from the brain and spinal cord emerge nerves, and from bones emerge ligaments. Where nerve and ligament meet, they form splinters with gaps filled with flesh, and the whole is covered by a membrane, forming the 'red body' and the muscle. The tendon (watar) is a composite extending from the nerve and ligament, intermediate in texture between the softness of nerve and hardness of ligament. Its function is to bulge during muscle contraction, causing the limb to move, and to relax during extension, allowing the limb to return. A further quote from a commentary on al-Mulakhkhas describes the nerve as originating from the brain/spinal cord, white and pliable, for sensation and movement; the ligament as similar; and the muscle as a composite of nerve, ligament, fibers, flesh, and membrane. When it hardens, the tendon forms. The muscle contracts and pulls the limb; when it relaxes, the tendon slackens and the limb moves away. This anatomical background underlies the concept of muscular strength as a physical force capable of producing movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you next lift your hand or take a step, pause to consider that every movement depends on a chain of creations Allah has placed inside you: nerve, ligament, muscle, tendon. How does this intricate design affect your sense of dependence on your own strength? What would it mean to consciously acknowledge that your ability to move is a gift every single time you act?
?
The tendon is described as intermediate between softness and hardness. In what area of your life have you been either too rigid (hard) or too passive (soft)? How can the balanced design of your body inspire you to seek a similar balance in your character—being firm in truth yet flexible in kindness—starting with one specific relationship today?
?
If muscular strength is a physical reality created by Allah, then every action you perform with that strength—whether good or bad—carries weight. Think of one habitual action you do daily (e.g., typing, speaking, helping). Does that action reflect gratitude to the One who gave you the power to do it? What would change if you consciously offered that action as an act of worship?

The Definition of 'Ajz as an Opposition to Qudra
Definition of 'ajz as an accident opposite to qudra with disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of incapacity ('ajz) as an existent accident opposite to power (qudra) and the two dissenting views regarding its nature.

Core Idea
Incapacity is an existent accident opposing power, but not all theologians agree.

This passage establishes the standard Ash'ari and Mu'tazili position on the nature of incapacity: it is a real, existent accident that directly opposes power. However, two notable dissenters—Abu Hashim and al-Asamm—offered alternative views, which the passage records to clarify the scope of agreement and disagreement. Understanding this definition is foundational for later discussions on divine and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and the main view
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the passage first states that incapacity is an accident existing opposite to power, and that this is agreed upon by Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila.
This is the default position of the school.
2
Identify the first dissenting view
Find where the passage mentions Abu Hashim's later opinion: he considered incapacity to be merely the absence of power, not an existent accident. Note that he still accepted accidents in general.
This is a minority view even among Mu'tazila.
3
Identify the second dissenting view
Find al-Asamm's position: he denied that incapacity is an existent accident because he rejected accidents entirely. This is a more radical denial.
Al-Asamm's view is based on a broader ontological stance.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why theologians care about the ontological status of incapacity. It relates to whether actions are directly created by God or dependent on capacities.
This connects to larger debates about causality and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الثامن : العجز مضاد للقدرة ( العجز عرض ) موجود ( مضاد للقدرة ) باتفاق من الأشاعرة وجمهور المعتزلة خلافاً لأبي هاشم في آخر أقواله حيث ذهب إلى أنه ) أي العجز ( عدم القدرة ) ونفى كونه معنى موجوداً مع أنه معترف بوجود الأعراض (و) خلافاً (للأصم) فإنه نفى كون العجز عرضاً موجود ) من حيث أنه ( نفي الأعراض) مطلقاً
The fourth type - the eighth topic: Incapacity is opposite to power. (Incapacity is an accident) existing (opposite to power) by agreement of the Ash'aris and the majority of the Mu'tazila, contrary to Abu Hashim in his later opinion where he held that it (i.e., incapacity) is the absence of power, and he denied it being an existing meaning despite his acknowledgment of the existence of accidents; and contrary to al-Asamm, for he denied incapacity being an existing accident, due to his denial of accidents absolutely.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines incapacity (al-‘ajz) as an accident (عرض) that is existent (موجود) and stands in opposition to power (القدرة). It records agreement among the Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila on this, but notes two dissenting opinions. First, Abu Hashim in his later view considered ‘ajz to be merely the absence of power (عدم القدرة), not an existent attribute, despite his acceptance of accidents in general. Second, al-Asamm rejected the existence of any accident altogether, so he denied that ‘ajz is an existent accident. The passage thus establishes the default position of the school and states the points of disagreement.

The Necessary Distinction Argument for 'Ajz
Sanusi presents the necessary distinction proof and Abu Hashim's reductionist objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument from the necessary distinction between the paralyzed and the restrained, and how Abu Hashim countered that the distinction can be reduced to the presence or absence of power.

Core Idea
The intuitive distinction alone does not prove 'ajz is an existent quality.

Sanusi begins by presenting a key Ash'ari proof for the existence of 'ajz: the necessary distinction that every rational person makes between a paralyzed person and someone merely prevented from acting. However, this seemingly intuitive argument is challenged by Abu Hashim, who suggests the distinction is simply due to the absence of power in the paralyzed and its presence in the restrained. Let's examine this foundational debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the claimed necessary distinction.
Notice how the argument appeals to common intuition.
2
Identify the two cases
The argument contrasts 'zaman' (paralyzed) and 'mamnu'' (prevented). Understand the difference: one has an internal impairment, the other has a temporary external prohibition.
3
Understand the inference
Sanusi argues that the distinction implies an existent quality in the paralyzed that is absent in the restrained. Find the line that states this.
4
Examine Abu Hashim's objection
Abu Hashim reduces the distinction to the presence or absence of power itself, not a new attribute. Consider why this objection is plausible.
This objection weakens the proof's conclusiveness.
5
Reflect on the debate
Ask yourself: does the distinction force us to accept 'ajz as existent? Or can it be explained by absence? This prepares you for al-Razi's critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا في إثبات كونه وجوديا التفرقة الضرورية بين الزمن والممنوع من الفعل فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه التفرقة بين كونه زمناً وكونه ممنوعاً من القيام مع سلامته، وما هي إلا أن في الزمن صفة وجودية هي العجز، وليس هذه الصفة في الممنوع ( ولأبي هاشم أن يجعلها ) أي التفرقة الضرورية ( عائدة إلى عدم القدرة في الزمن ووجودها في الممنوع، فإن الممنوع قادر على رأيه كما مر قال الإمام الرازي: لا دليل على كون العجز صفة وجودية وما يقال من أن جعل العجز عبارة عن عدم القدرة ليس أولى من العكس ضعيف لأنا نقول كلاهما محتمل، وإذا لم يقم دليل على أحدهما كان الاحتمال باقياً وفي نقد المحصل أن القدرة إن فسرت بسلامة الأعضاء فالعجز حينئذ عبارة عن آفة تعرض للأعضاء، وتكون القدرة أولى بأن لا تكون وجودية لأن السلامة عدم الآفة، وإن فسرت القدرة بهيئة تعرض عند سلامة الأعضاء وتسمى بالتمكن أو بما هو علة له، وجعل العجز عبارة عن عدم تلك الهيئة كانت القدرة وجودية والعجز عدمياً، وإن أريد بالعجز ما يعرض للمرتعش وتمتاز به حركة الارتعاش عن حركة الاختيار فالعجز وجودي ولعل الأشاعرة ذهبوا إلى هذا المعنى فحكموا بكونه وجودياً
Our proof for establishing it as existent: the necessary distinction between the paralyzed and the one prevented from an action. For every rational person finds in himself a distinction between being paralyzed and being prevented from standing while being sound. It is nothing other than that in the paralyzed there is an existent quality which is incapacity, and this quality is not in the prevented one. And Abu Hashim can make this necessary distinction revert to the absence of power in the paralyzed and its existence in the prevented, because the prevented one is capable in his view as previously mentioned. Imam al-Razi said: There is no proof that incapacity is an existent attribute, and what is said that making incapacity an expression for the absence of power is not more appropriate than the opposite is weak, for we say both are probable, and if no proof is established for one of them, the probability remains. And in Naqd al-Muhassal: if power is interpreted as soundness of limbs, then incapacity is an affliction that occurs to the limbs, and power would be more entitled not to be existent because soundness is the absence of affliction. If power is interpreted as a disposition that occurs upon soundness of limbs, called ability (tamakkun) or what is its cause, and incapacity is made an expression for the absence of that disposition, then power is existent and incapacity is nonexistent. If what is meant by incapacity is what occurs to the trembling person and distinguishes the tremor movement from voluntary movement, then incapacity is existent. Perhaps the Ash'aris held this meaning and thus judged it to be existent.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage advances the proof for the existence of 'ajz as an attribute. It begins by claiming a necessary distinction (تفرقة ضرورية) between a person who is paralyzed (زمن) and a person who is prevented from acting (ممنوع) despite being physically sound. Every rational person intuitively distinguishes between the two: the paralyzed cannot move due to an internal impairment, while the restrained could move if the prevention were removed. The argument asserts that this distinction shows that the paralyzed possesses an existent quality ('ajz) that the restrained lacks. Abu Hashim objects that the distinction is due to the absence of power in the paralyzed and its presence in the restrained, since the restrained is still capable (قادر) in his view. Imam al-Razi then critiques the proof, stating that there is no definitive evidence for 'ajz being existent; both interpretations (existent or absent) are merely probable. He cites Naqd al-Muhassal, which analyzes different definitions of power (القدرة). If power means soundness of limbs, then 'ajz would be an affliction (آفة) to the limbs, and power would be nonexistent since soundness is absence of affliction. If power means a disposition (هيئة) called ability (تمكن) or its cause, then power is existent and 'ajz is its absence, hence nonexistent. However, if 'ajz refers to the condition that causes involuntary tremors (الارتعاش) distinct from voluntary action, then 'ajz is an existent attribute. The passage concludes that the Ash'aris likely adopted this last meaning, thus considering 'ajz existent. The commentary guides the reader through the sequence: the proof, the objection, the skeptical response from al-Razi, and the final resolution through semantic analysis.

Key Terms
التفرقة الضرورية
الزمن
الممنوع
القدرة
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the necessary distinction (التفرقة الضرورية) claim to show?
A) That 'ajz is an existent quality in the paralyzed
B) That 'ajz is a nonexistent quality
C) That the paralyzed lacks power
D) That the restrained has power

Key Takeaway
The necessary distinction alone does not settle the ontological status of 'ajz; it invites further analysis and objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel unable to do something good you ought to do, do you immediately assume it’s because you have an genuine incapacity (an existent quality) or might it be that you are simply not using the ability Allah gave you? What would change if you treated your 'inability' as merely a lack of activated power rather than a fixed attribute?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Do you label yourself as 'weak-willed' as if that is an unchangeable quality (like the paralyzed person)? How does this lesson challenge that narrative and call you to seek the removal of barriers instead of accepting an 'ajz as a permanent trait?
?
Abu Hashim’s objection suggests that the distinction we feel can be explained by the absence of something rather than a positive quality. How might this apply to your perception of your own spiritual states? Are there areas where you have assumed a deficiency in your character is a fixed reality, when it might actually be the absence of effort or grace you haven’t sought?

Al-Razi's Critique and the Semantic Resolution
Al-Razi's skeptical critique and the semantic resolution from Naqd al-Muhassal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's critique that no definitive proof exists for 'ajz being existent, and how the Naqd al-Muhassal analysis resolves the debate by showing it depends on the definition of qudra. The Ash'ari position is based on a specific meaning of 'ajz as involuntary tremor.

Core Idea
Whether 'ajz is existent or not depends entirely on the definition of 'qudra'.

Imam al-Razi dismisses the entire argument as inconclusive, stating that both interpretations are merely probable. A later work, Naqd al-Muhassal, provides a semantic analysis that clarifies the issue: the status of 'ajz changes based on how we define qudra. Ultimately, the Ash'aris adopt a definition where 'ajz is an existent quality related to involuntary tremor. Let's explore this nuanced resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال الإمام الرازي' to the end. Al-Razi asserts that there is no proof, and both views are equally probable.
Notice his phrase 'الاحتمال باقياً' – probability remains.
2
Understand the three definitions of qudra
The passage from Naqd al-Muhassal lists three possible definitions of qudra. Identify each one and see how it affects the status of 'ajz.
These definitions are: soundness of limbs, a disposition called tamakkun, and as a cause for voluntary movement.
3
Map the consequences for 'ajz
For each definition, determine whether 'ajz becomes existent or nonexistent. Note the third definition where 'ajz is an existent quality (the trembling case).
4
Identify the Ash'ari position
The passage ends by stating that the Ash'aris likely adopted the third meaning. Reflect on why this choice might be consistent with their overall theology.
This shows that the debate is not purely empirical but depends on conceptual definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is nothing other than that in the paralyzed there is an existent quality which is incapacity, and this quality is not in the prevented one. And in Naqd al-Muhassal: if power is interpreted as soundness of limbs, then incapacity is an affliction that occurs to the limbs, and power would be more entitled not to be existent because soundness is the absence of affliction. Commentary
This passage advances the proof for the existence of 'ajz as an attribute. ?

Are there areas in your life where you have assumed a fixed reality about something (like your own incapacity) that might actually change if you redefined your understanding of power? What would you do if you realized your 'incapacity' is merely a definitional choice?

Al-Ash'ari's Two Opinions on the Attachment of 'Ajz
Presents al-Ash'ari's two opinions on whether 'ajz attaches to the existent or the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's two opinions on the attachment of 'ajz and their implications for precedence and attachment to opposites.

Core Idea
The attachment of 'ajz is a point of contention with direct consequences for its logic.

Al-Ash'ari presents two views on whether incapacity attaches to the existent or non-existent. This distinction affects whether 'ajz can precede its object or attach to two opposites. Let's examine both positions and their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument and identify the two opinions.
2
Distinguish the two opinions
Identify the stronger opinion (attachment to existent) and the weaker opinion (attachment to non-existent), noting their proponents.
The stronger opinion is al-Ash'ari's preferred view, while the weaker is held by the Mu'tazila and many of his followers.
3
Trace the implications
See how each opinion leads to different conclusions about whether 'ajz precedes its object and whether it can attach to two opposites.
The weaker opinion allows precedence and attachment to opposites, while the stronger does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري في الأصح من قوليه: (العجز إنما يتعلق بالموجود) دون المعدوم على قياس القدرة ) فالزمن عاجز عن القعود ) الموجود ) لا عن القيام) المعدوم ) فإن التعلق بالمعدوم خيال محض) لا عبرة به أصلاً، واختار على هذا القول أن العجز لا يسبق المعجوز عنه ولا يتعلق بالضدين على نحو ما ذكره في القدرة ) وله قول ضعيف) هو (أنه) أي العجز (إنما يتعلق بالمعدوم ) دون الموجود ) وإليه ذهب المعتزلة وكثير من أصحابنا ) وعلى هذا فالزمن عاجز عن القيام المعدوم لا عن القعود الموجود وإن كان مضطراً إليه، بحيث لا سبيل له إلى الانفكاك عنه ( وجواز تعلقه ) أي تعلق العجز ) بالضدين فرع ذلك ) أي يجوز إلى هذا القول تعلق العجز الواحد بالضدين وإن لم يجز تعلق القدرة الواحدة بهما، وذلك لأن العجز متعلق بالعدم ويجوز اجتماع الضدين فيه والقدرة متعلقة بالوجود ولا يجوز اجتماعهما فيه وكذا يتقدم العجز على المعجوز عنه في هذا القول، وأما
Then Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said in the more correct of his two statements: 'Incapacity attaches only to the existent,' not to the non-existent, by analogy with power. So the paralyzed is incapable of sitting (the existent), not of standing (the non-existent), for attachment to the non-existent is pure fantasy, having no consideration. And he adopted on this statement that incapacity does not precede its object nor attaches to two opposites, just as he mentioned in the case of power. And he has a weaker statement: that it (i.e., incapacity) attaches only to the non-existent, not to the existent. To this went the Mu'tazila and many of our companions. On this, the paralyzed is incapable of the non-existent standing, not of the existent sitting, even though he is compelled to it, having no way to be free from it. And the permissibility of its attachment (i.e., the attachment of incapacity) to two opposites is a branch of that: i.e., according to this statement, it is permissible for one incapacity to attach to two opposites, even though one power does not attach to both of them. That is because incapacity attaches to non-existence, and it is possible for two opposites to coexist in non-existence, while power attaches to existence and it is not possible for two opposites to coexist in it. Likewise, incapacity precedes its object in this statement. And as for...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's two opinions regarding the attachment (تعلق) of 'ajz to its object. The stronger opinion is that 'ajz attaches only to the existent, by analogy with power (القدرة). Thus, a paralyzed person is incapable of sitting (which exists) but not of standing (which is non-existent), because attachment to non-existence is imaginary. Following this view, 'ajz does not precede its object nor does one 'ajz attach to two opposites. The weaker opinion, adopted by the Mu'tazila and many of al-Ash'ari's followers, holds that 'ajz attaches only to the non-existent. On this view, the paralyzed is incapable of standing (non-existent) but not of sitting (existent), though he is compelled to sit and cannot escape it. This leads to the consequence that one 'ajz can attach to two opposites, since opposites can coexist in non-existence (e.g., both standing and not-standing are non-existent for the paralyzed), whereas power attaches to existence and cannot attach to two opposites. Additionally, on this weaker view, 'ajz can precede its object. The passage ends with 'وأما' (as for...), indicating further elaboration that is not fully captured in this excerpt. The commentary explains the two positions and their implications.

Key Terms
تعلق
الموجود
المعدوم
الضدين
التقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity always attaches to the action one is unable to perform (the non-existent).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's stronger opinion holds that incapacity attaches only to the existent, by analogy with power. The weaker opinion does attach to the non-existent, but it is considered less correct.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's stronger opinion, to what does 'ajz attach?
A) The existent
B) The non-existent
C) Both existent and non-existent
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's two opinions on the attachment of 'ajz lead to different consequences for whether 'ajz can precede its object or attach to two opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own limitations, do you tend to focus on what you cannot do in reality (the existent) or on what you could never do (the non-existent)? How might al-Ash'ari's more correct opinion—that incapacity attaches only to the existent—change the way you think about your own weaknesses? Write one concrete step you will take this week to stop fantasizing about impossible alternatives and instead address the real obstacles in your life.
?
Al-Ash'ari notes that the weaker opinion allows incapacity to attach to two opposites, because opposites can coexist in non-existence. Reflect on a situation where you feel 'incapacitated' by two opposing desires or fears. Are you actually incapable, or are you imagining a conflict that doesn't exist in reality? Show yourself where you have the power to choose one path and leave the other behind.
?
This lesson reveals that even great scholars like al-Ash'ari had two opinions on a seemingly abstract point. How does this humility in acknowledging differing valid interpretations affect your own approach to debates or disagreements with others? Commit today to holding your views with confidence but also with openness to correction, especially in matters where the truth is not immediately obvious.

The Mu'tazilite Argument: Conditions of Perception in Sleep
Mu'tazilite explanation for dreams as false imagination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning that dreams are false imagination because the necessary conditions for perception are absent during sleep.

Core Idea
Dreams lack the required conditions for true perception, so they are mere illusions.

The theologians define dreams as false imagination, but the reasons differ. The Mu'tazilites focus on the conditions required for perception, which are absent in sleep. Let's explore their view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the conditions listed.
2
Identify the conditions of perception
List the specific conditions: facing, emission of rays, transparent air, specific structure, absence of barrier.
These are the necessary conditions for vision in Mu'tazilite theory.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since these conditions are not met in sleep, what the sleeper sees cannot be real perception, only false imagination.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite methodology
They rely on a naturalistic and physical theory of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرؤيا فخيال باطل عند المتكلمين أي جمهورهم. أما عند المعتزلة فلفقد شرائط الإدراك حالة النوم من المقابلة وانبثاث الشعاع وتوسط الهواء الشفاف والبنية المخصوصة وانتفاء الحجاب إلى غير ذلك من الشرائط المعتبرة في الإدراكات، فما يراه النائم ليس من الإدراكات في شيء بل هو من قبيل الخيالات الفاسدة والأوهام الباطلة. وأما عند الأصحاب إذا لم يشترطوا في الإدراك شيئاً من ذلك فلأنه أي الإدراك في حالة النوم خلاف العادة أي لم تجر عادته تعالى بخلق الإدراك في الشخص وهو نائم، ولأن النوم ضد للإدراك فلا يجامعه فلا تكون الرؤيا إدراكاً حقيقة.
As for dreams, they are false imagination according to the theologians, i.e., the majority of them. As for the Mu'tazilites, due to the lack of conditions of perception in the state of sleep, such as facing, emission of rays, transparent air intervening, specific structure, absence of a barrier, and other conditions considered in perceptions. So what the sleeper sees is not at all among perceptions, but rather belongs to corrupt imaginings and false illusions. As for the Ash'arites (al-ashab) since they do not stipulate any of that for perception, it is because perception in the state of sleep is contrary to habit, i.e., His habit, exalted be He, does not occur to create perception in a person while he is asleep, and because sleep is contrary to perception so it does not accompany it, so the dream is not a real perception.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of dreams (al-ru'ya) according to the majority of theologians (al-mutakallimun). It states that dreams are false imagination (khayal batil). It then explains the reasons from two schools: the Mu'tazilites and the Ash'arites (al-ashab). For the Mu'tazilites, perception requires certain conditions such as facing the object, emission of rays, transparent air, specific bodily structure, and absence of barriers. In sleep, these conditions are absent, so what the sleeper experiences is not true perception but false imaginings. For the Ash'arites, they do not require those conditions; rather, they hold that sleep is contrary to habit (khilaf al-'ada): God's habit does not involve creating perception in a sleeping person. Also, sleep is opposite to perception, so they cannot coexist. Therefore, dreams are not real perceptions. The passage thereby clarifies the theological stance on the nature of dreams.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
خيال باطل
المعتزلة
شرائط الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dreams are real sensory experiences that happen without conditions.
Correction: Mu'tazilites argue that true perception requires specific physical conditions that are absent in sleep, so dreams are not real perceptions.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a condition for perception in the passage?
A) Facing the object
B) Emission of rays
C) Presence of light
D) Absence of a barrier

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that dreams are false imagination because the necessary conditions for true perception are missing during sleep.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a dream that felt as real as waking life. According to this lesson, that feeling was an illusion because the conditions for true perception were absent. When in your waking life have you been similarly deceived by a feeling or assumption that seemed real but lacked the proper conditions? How will you train yourself to examine your experiences more critically, especially in matters of your faith and character?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue that sleep lacks the conditions for perception. But spiritually, many of us 'sleepwalk' through our prayers and daily worship—going through the motions without true presence. What specific condition—like 'facing' (turning your heart to Allah) or 'absence of barriers' (removing distractions)—are you neglecting right now? What one step can you take today to restore true perception in your worship?

The Ash'arite View: Dreams as Contrary to Habit
Ash'arite argument that dreams are not perceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite reasoning that dreams are not real perceptions because sleep is contrary to God's habitual creation of perception and because sleep is opposite to perception.

Core Idea
God does not habitually create perception during sleep, and sleep inherently opposes perception.

Unlike the Mu'tazilites, the Ash'arites do not require physical conditions for perception. Instead, they argue that it is against God's established habit to create perception in a sleeping person, and sleep itself is the opposite of perception, so dreams cannot be real.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'arite part of the passage
Focus on their two-part argument.
2
Understand 'contrary to habit'
God's customary practice does not include creating perception in a sleeping person.
3
Understand 'sleep is opposite to perception'
Sleep and perception are mutually exclusive; they cannot coexist.
4
Compare with Mu'tazilites
Note the different approaches: Mu'tazilites rely on physical conditions, Ash'arites rely on divine habit and the nature of sleep.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرؤيا فخيال باطل عند المتكلمين أي جمهورهم. أما عند المعتزلة فلفقد شرائط الإدراك حالة النوم من المقابلة وانبثاث الشعاع وتوسط الهواء الشفاف والبنية المخصوصة وانتفاء الحجاب إلى غير ذلك من الشرائط المعتبرة في الإدراكات، فما يراه النائم ليس من الإدراكات في شيء بل هو من قبيل الخيالات الفاسدة والأوهام الباطلة. وأما عند الأصحاب إذا لم يشترطوا في الإدراك شيئاً من ذلك فلأنه أي الإدراك في حالة النوم خلاف العادة أي لم تجر عادته تعالى بخلق الإدراك في الشخص وهو نائم، ولأن النوم ضد للإدراك فلا يجامعه فلا تكون الرؤيا إدراكاً حقيقة.
As for dreams, they are false imagination according to the theologians, i.e., the majority of them. As for the Mu'tazilites, due to the lack of conditions of perception in the state of sleep, such as facing, emission of rays, transparent air intervening, specific structure, absence of a barrier, and other conditions considered in perceptions. So what the sleeper sees is not at all among perceptions, but rather belongs to corrupt imaginings and false illusions. As for the Ash'arites (al-ashab) since they do not stipulate any of that for perception, it is because perception in the state of sleep is contrary to habit, i.e., His habit, exalted be He, does not occur to create perception in a person while he is asleep, and because sleep is contrary to perception so it does not accompany it, so the dream is not a real perception.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of dreams (al-ru'ya) according to the majority of theologians (al-mutakallimun). It states that dreams are false imagination (khayal batil). It then explains the reasons from two schools: the Mu'tazilites and the Ash'arites (al-ashab). For the Mu'tazilites, perception requires certain conditions such as facing the object, emission of rays, transparent air, specific bodily structure, and absence of barriers. In sleep, these conditions are absent, so what the sleeper experiences is not true perception but false imaginings. For the Ash'arites, they do not require those conditions; rather, they hold that sleep is contrary to habit (khilaf al-'ada): God's habit does not involve creating perception in a sleeping person. Also, sleep is opposite to perception, so they cannot coexist. Therefore, dreams are not real perceptions. The passage thereby clarifies the theological stance on the nature of dreams.

Key Terms
الأصحاب
خلاف العادة
النوم ضد الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'arites deny the possibility of any perception in dreams.
Correction: They only deny that it is a real perception according to the usual habit of God; they do not deny that something appears to the sleeper.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite view in this passage, why are dreams not real perceptions?
A) Because the conditions of perception are absent in sleep
B) Because sleep is contrary to habit and opposite to perception
C) Because the sleeper does not have a body
D) Because dreams are illusions created by Shaytan

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arites conclude that dreams are not real perceptions because sleep is contrary to God's habitual creation and is the opposite of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If sleep is contrary to perception, and dreams are not real, then what you experience in dreams cannot be evidence for anything. Do you ever base decisions or beliefs on dreams? After this lesson, will you commit to weigh only waking evidence in your faith and actions?

Dreams as True Perceptions: The Argument from Indistinguishability
Abu Ishaq defines dreams as true perceptions via indistinguishability argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's argument that dreams are true perceptions and why doubting them leads to skepticism.

Core Idea
Dreams are as real as waking experiences; doubting them leads to total skepticism.

Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini defends the reality of dream perceptions against skeptics. He argues that since the sensory experiences in sleep are indistinguishable from those in wakefulness, rejecting dreams would undermine all knowledge. This passage establishes the epistemological foundation for prophetic visions and other spiritual perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Identify the core analogy
Notice how Abu Ishaq compares the perceptions of a sleeper (seeing, hearing, tasting) to those of an awake person. The point is that they are qualitatively identical.
3
Understand the consequence
If we doubt dream perceptions, consistency forces us to doubt waking perceptions, leading to skepticism (safsata) and denying self-evident truths.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأستاذ ( أبو إسحاق : ( إنه ) أي المنام ( إدراك حق) بلا شبهة ( إذ لا فرق بين ما يجده النائم من نفسه ) في نومه ( من إبصار ) للمبصرات ( وسمع ) للمسموعات وذوق للمذوقات وغيرها من الإدراكات ) وبين ما يجده اليقظان ) في يقظته من إدراكاته ( فلو جاز التشكيك فيه ) أي فيما يجده النائم ( لجاز التشكيك فيما يجده اليقظان ولزم السفسطة والقدح في الأمور المعلومة حقيقتها بالبديهة ( ولم يخالف) الأستاذ ( في كون النوم (ضداً للإدراك ( لكنه زعم أن الإدراك يقوم بجزء من أجزاء الإنسان ) غير ما يقوم به النوم من أجزائه، فلا يلزم اجتماع الضدين في محل واحد )
And the master (Abu Ishaq) said: "It (i.e., the dream) is a true perception without doubt, for there is no difference between what the sleeper finds from himself during his sleep—of seeing visible things, hearing audible things, tasting tastables, and other perceptions—and what the awake person finds during his wakefulness from his perceptions. So if doubt were allowed in it (i.e., what the sleeper finds), then doubt would be allowed in what the awake person finds, and skepticism would follow, as well as disparagement of things whose truth is known by intuition." And the master did not disagree that sleep is opposite to perception, but he claimed that perception subsists in a part of the human parts other than that in which sleep subsists from his parts, so the conjunction of two opposites in one locus does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The author begins by quoting Abu Ishaq (al-Isfarayini) defining dreams as true perception (idrak haqq) without any doubt. The proof follows: there is no difference between the sensory experiences of a sleeper (seeing, hearing, tasting) and those of an awake person. If one were to doubt the reality of dream perceptions, consistency would require doubting waking perceptions as well, which leads to absolute skepticism (safsata) and undermines self-evident truths. The author then notes that Abu Ishaq does not deny that sleep is opposite (didd) to perception. Instead, he resolves the apparent contradiction by arguing that perception and sleep subsist in different parts of the human being, so they do not coexist in the same locus. Thus, the logical problem of opposites in the same subject is avoided.

Key Terms
المنام
إدراك حق
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dreams are purely imaginary or illusory and have no basis in reality.
Correction: Abu Ishaq argues that dreams are true perceptions (idrak haqq) because they are indistinguishable from waking perceptions in terms of sensory experience.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument Abu Ishaq uses to prove that dreams are true perceptions?
A) Dreams are caused by external objects.
B) There is no difference between the perceptions of a sleeper and an awake person.
C) Sleep is not opposite to perception.
D) The soul creates images directly.

Key Takeaway
Dreams are true perceptions because they are qualitatively identical to waking perceptions, and doubting them leads to skepticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a dream as meaningless fantasy? After reading this argument, will you now consider that Allah might be speaking to you through your dreams? What would change if you took every dream seriously as a potential truth?
?
If dreams are true perceptions, then the dreams you have about your own sins or your death are not just random thoughts. Are you willing to let those dreams convict you and drive you to repentance, or will you continue to write them off as imagination?
?
This passage warns that doubting dreams leads to doubting all perception. Do you doubt the spiritual significance of your dreams because modern culture says they are subjective? How can you resist that skepticism and embrace the Islamic view that dreams are part of divine communication?

Resolving the Conflict: Sleep as Opposite but Not Contradictory
Abu Ishaq resolves the objection that sleep is opposite to perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Ishaq resolves the apparent contradiction between sleep as opposite to perception while maintaining dreams as true perceptions.

Core Idea
Sleep and perception occupy different parts; no contradiction occurs.

A potential objection to Abu Ishaq's view is that sleep is considered the opposite of perception. How can dreams be true perceptions if sleep is their opposite? Abu Ishaq clarifies that sleep and perception affect different parts of the human being, so they do not conflict. This lesson examines that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the part where the author notes that sleep is considered a 'didd' (opposite) to perception, then Abu Ishaq's response.
2
Understand the concept of different loci
Abu Ishaq claims that perception and sleep subsist in different parts of the human being. This avoids the logical rule that two opposites cannot coexist in the same locus.
This is a key move in Islamic philosophy to avoid contradictions.
3
Reflect on the significance
This resolution preserves both the reality of dream perceptions and the fact that sleep is a state opposite to wakeful perception, without logical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأستاذ ( أبو إسحاق : ( إنه ) أي المنام ( إدراك حق) بلا شبهة ( إذ لا فرق بين ما يجده النائم من نفسه ) في نومه ( من إبصار ) للمبصرات ( وسمع ) للمسموعات وذوق للمذوقات وغيرها من الإدراكات ) وبين ما يجده اليقظان ) في يقظته من إدراكاته ( فلو جاز التشكيك فيه ) أي فيما يجده النائم ( لجاز التشكيك فيما يجده اليقظان ولزم السفسطة والقدح في الأمور المعلومة حقيقتها بالبديهة ( ولم يخالف) الأستاذ ( في كون النوم (ضداً للإدراك ( لكنه زعم أن الإدراك يقوم بجزء من أجزاء الإنسان ) غير ما يقوم به النوم من أجزائه، فلا يلزم اجتماع الضدين في محل واحد )

So if doubt were allowed in it (i.e., what the sleeper finds), then doubt would be allowed in what the awake person finds, and skepticism would follow, as well as disparagement of things whose truth is known by intuition." And the master did not disagree that sleep is opposite to perception, but he claimed that perception subsists in a part of the human parts other than that in which sleep subsists from his parts, so the conjunction of two opposites in one locus does not necessarily follow.

Philosophers' Mechanism of Dream Perception
Philosophers explain dream mechanism with two impediments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers account for dreams: perception occurs in the common sense when the imaginative faculty is freed from two impediments—influx of external images and control by the intellect or estimation.

Core Idea
Dreams arise from the imagination when external inputs and mental control are suspended.

The philosophers provide a natural explanation for how we perceive objects in dreams. They locate the dream experience in a faculty called the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), which normally receives images from the external senses. But during sleep, two obstacles that block the imaginative faculty are removed, allowing it to produce the vivid images we call dreams. This passage explains that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the philosophers assert the perceiver in sleep exists in the common sense.
2
Identify the two impediments
Find the two factors that normally prevent the imaginative faculty from acting freely: (1) the continuous arrival of external images, and (2) the controlling influence of the intellect or estimation.
The Arabic terms for these are 'توارد الصور من الخارج' and 'تسلط العقل أو الوهم'.
3
Understand the removal during sleep
Notice how during sleep, both impediments are removed: external images cease to arrive, and intellectual control relaxes. This frees the imaginative faculty to depict images in the common sense.
The passage explicitly says 'إذا نام انقطع عن الحس المشترك توارد الصور من خارج'.
4
Reflect on the role of common sense
Consider why the common sense is described as the 'gathering place' of sensations. It is the faculty that combines sensory data, and in dreams it receives composed images from imagination as if they were external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحكماء : المدرك في النوم يوجد في الحس المشترك ) قوله : (المنام) فتذكير الضمير بتأويل الرؤيا بالمنام . قوله: (إدراك حق أي الإحساس بالحواس الظاهرة والشرائط التي ذكرتم إنما هي للإحساس الذي في اليقظة وهو إنما يقتضي وجود متعلقاتها في الرؤيا لا في الخارج. قوله : ( وقال الحكماء (الخ في الطبي شرح المشكاة قال المازني : مذهب أهل السنة أن حقيقة الرؤيا خلق الله في النائم اعتقادات كخلقها في قلب اليقظان وهو سبحانه وتعالى يفعل ما يشاء ولا يمنعه نوم ولا يقظة ويخلق هذه الاعتقادات في اللازم على أمورياتيها في ثاني الحال كالغيم علماً على المطر انتهى والمراد بالاعتقادات ما يعم المتخيلة والمتحققة أعني رسم الصور في الحس المشترك، إلا أن هناك أمرين صارفين لها عن فعلها أحدهما توارد الصور من الخارج على الحس المشترك، فإنه إذا انتقش بهذه الصور لم يتسع لانتقاشه بالصور التي تركبها المتخيلة فيعوقها ذلك عن عملها لعدم القابل وثانيهما: تسلط العقل أو الوهم عليها بالضبط عندما يستعملانها فتتعوق بذلك عن عملها وإذا انتفى هذان الشاغلان أو أحدهما تفرعت لفعلها وظهر سلطانها في التصوير، ولا شك أن الشخص إذا نام انقطع عن الحس المشترك توارد الصور من ( قوله : ( صارفين لها عن (فعلها بوجود كل واحد منهما بضعف عملها على ما في الشفاء والوجدان لا أن كلاً منها يبطل على ما وهم فأشكل عليه كلمة أو في قوله : فإذا انتفى هذان الشاغلان أو أحدهما إلخ وفي قول الشارح وظهر سلطانها إشارة إلى ما قبلها . قوله : (لم يتسع من الوسع بمعنى الوسع بمعنى : الطاقة لا من ضد الضيق على ما وهم فاعترض بأن الصور الذهنية لا تمانع فيها، وأجاب سواء كان الشاغلان عبارة عن العقل والوهم أو عن تسلط أحدهما وتوارد الصور من خارج. قوله : إذا) نام انقطع عن الحس الخ والأظهر أن يضم إليه وقد لا يتسلط العقل والوهم عليها بالضبط حينئذ فيرتفع المانع بالكلية.
And the philosophers said: 'The perceiver in sleep exists in the common sense.' (Commentary follows) ... This act, namely the depiction of images in the common sense, except that there are two diverters preventing it from its action: one is the influx of images from outside to the common sense, for when it is imprinted with these images it does not have capacity to be imprinted with the images composed by the imaginative faculty, so that hinders it from its work due to lack of receptivity; and the second is the control of the intellect or the estimation over it by restraint when they use it, so it is hindered by that from its work. When these two preoccupations or one of them are removed, it branches out to its action and its authority appears in depiction, and there is no doubt that when a person sleeps, the influx of images from outside to the common sense is cut off...
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the philosophers' account of dreams. The main text states that the perceiver in sleep is found in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). The commentary first clarifies that the term 'dream' (al-manam) refers to the vision. Then it discusses the Sunni view from al-Mazini that dreams are creations of God in the sleeper, akin to waking beliefs. The main argument then describes the mechanism: the common sense is the gathering place of external sensations. The imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) composes images and imprints them on the common sense, making them observed like external objects. However, two factors normally prevent this: (1) the constant influx of external images, which leaves no room in the common sense, and (2) the control of the intellect (al-'aql) or estimation (al-wahm) which restrains the imaginative faculty. During sleep, these preoccupations are removed (external images stop, intellect relaxes), allowing the imaginative faculty free reign to produce dream images. The commentary further addresses objections about the capacity of the common sense and clarifies that both factors act independently to weaken, not completely nullify, the imaginative faculty's action.

القوة المتخيلة

Misconception: That dreams are purely subjective with no real mechanism involving distinct faculties.
Correction: The philosophers provide a specific causal mechanism: the common sense receives images from the imagination during sleep when external and intellectual impediments are removed.

Key Takeaway
Dreams, according to the philosophers, are perceptions in the common sense generated by the imaginative faculty when freed from external input and intellectual control during sleep.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you go to sleep, do you consciously prepare your mind by reflecting on what you have seen and thought during the day, knowing that those impressions will shape your dreams? How much of your waking life are you willing to surrender to unguarded imagination?
?
If the philosophers are right that the intellect relaxes control during sleep, what does that reveal about the importance of training your intellect while awake? Are you feeding your intellect with truth so that even in sleep, your imagination reflects that?
?
Consider a recurring dream you have had. What does it reveal about the state of your heart and the images your imagination has stored while you were awake? Will you take steps to guard your senses and thoughts so that your sleep becomes a source of clarity, not confusion?

Sunni View: Dreams as Divine Creations
Al-Mazini states the Sunni view that dreams are divine creations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni theological perspective that dreams are directly created by God, similar to waking beliefs, as stated by al-Mazini.

Core Idea
Dreams are created by God, not by innate faculties.

While the philosophers explain dreams through natural faculties, Sunni theologians maintain that dreams are direct creations of God. Al-Mazini, as quoted in the commentary, articulates this view, emphasizing that God creates beliefs in the sleeper just as He does in the wakeful person. This lesson examines that alternative perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni statement
Read al-Mazini's statement in Arabic and English, noting that he describes dreams as 'creation of beliefs' by God.
2
Contrast with philosophers
Compare this view with the previous philosophers' explanation. Ask: What is the role of human faculties here?
The Sunni view attributes the dream directly to God, while the philosophers describe a natural process.
3
Understand the theological implication
Reflect on why this matters: maintaining God's absolute power (qudra) over all things, including dreams.
The passage says 'He does what He wills, and neither sleep nor wakefulness prevents Him'.

This act, namely the depiction of images in the common sense, except that there are two diverters preventing it from its action: one is the influx of images from outside to the common sense, for when it is imprinted with these images it does not have capacity to be imprinted with the images composed by the imaginative faculty, so that hinders it from its work due to lack of receptivity; and the second is the control of the intellect or the estimation over it by restraint when they use it, so it is hindered by that from its work. Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the philosophers' account of dreams. The main text states that the perceiver in sleep is found in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). The commentary first clarifies that the term 'dream' (al-manam) refers to the vision. Then it discusses the Sunni view from al-Mazini that dreams are creations of God in the sleeper, akin to waking beliefs. The main argument then describes the mechanism: the common sense is the gathering place of external sensations. The imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) composes images and imprints them on the common sense, making them observed like external objects. However, two factors normally prevent this: (1) the constant influx of external images, which leaves no room in the common sense, and (2) the control of the intellect (al-'aql) or estimation (al-wahm) which restrains the imaginative faculty. During sleep, these preoccupations are removed (external images stop, intellect relaxes), allowing the imaginative faculty free reign to produce dream images. The commentary further addresses objections about the capacity of the common sense and clarifies that both factors act independently to weaken, not completely nullify, the imaginative faculty's action.

Two Hindrances to the Imaginative Faculty
Al-Iji lists two impediments to the imaginative faculty and explains how sleep removes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two factors that prevent the imaginative faculty from composing images and why sleep removes one of them.

Core Idea
The imagination is blocked by external images and rational control; sleep frees it.

Al-Iji explains why the imaginative faculty sometimes cannot compose images: either the senses are overwhelmed by incoming data, or the intellect/estimation commandeers it. Sleep, by cutting off external input, allows the imagination to work freely — which is why we dream.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noticing how al-Iji first states the action of the imaginative faculty, then lists two impediments, and finally explains how sleep removes the first.
Pay attention to the terms الحس المشترك, المتخيلة, العقل, الوهم.
2
Identify the two impediments
Find the two hindrances: one is external images arriving at the common sense; the other is control by intellect or estimation.
The second impediment can come from either the intellect (عقل) or the estimative faculty (وهم).
3
Understand how sleep helps
Sleep cuts off the arrival of external images, so the common sense becomes available for internal images composed by the imagination.
This explains why dreams occur during sleep.
4
Apply the explanation
Describe in your own words how these two impediments work and why sleep removes only the first.
Consider: Even in sleep, the intellect is not entirely inactive, but the passage focuses on external input.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الفعل، أعني رسم الصور في الحس المشترك، إلا أن هناك أمرين صارفين لها عن فعلها أحدهما توارد الصور من الخارج على الحس المشترك، فإنه إذا انتقش بهذه الصور لم يتسع لانتقاشه بالصور التي تركبها المتخيلة فيعوقها ذلك عن عملها لعدم القابل وثانيهما: تسلط العقل أو الوهم عليها بالضبط عندما يستعملانها فتتعوق بذلك عن عملها وإذا انتفى هذان الشاغلان أو أحدهما تفرعت لفعلها وظهر سلطانها في التصوير، ولا شك أن الشخص إذا نام انقطع عن الحس المشترك توارد الصور من الخارج فيتسع لانتقاش الصور من الداخل.
This action, I mean the drawing of images in the common sense, except that there are two impediments diverting it from its action: one of them is the arrival of images from outside upon the common sense, for when it is impressed with these images, it does not have capacity to be impressed with the images that the imaginative faculty composes, so that impedes it from its work due to lack of receptivity; and the second is the domination of the intellect or estimation over it by controlling when they use it, so it becomes impeded thereby from its work. And when these two preoccupations or one of them are removed, it becomes free for its action and its dominion in depiction appears, and there is no doubt that when a person sleeps, the arrival of images from outside is cut off from the common sense, so it becomes capacious for the impression of images from within.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the two impediments to the imaginative faculty's work of forming images in the common sense, and explains how sleep removes them. The author first states that the action of the imaginative faculty is to draw images in the common sense (الحس المشترك). Then he lists two things that divert it from its action. The first is the influx of external sensory images into the common sense, which fills it up so it cannot receive the images composed by the imagination. The second is the control of the intellect (عقل) or estimation (وهم) when they use the imagination for their own purposes. When these two preoccupations are removed—either both or even one—the imagination becomes free to do its work, and its power in depicting appears. Then he asserts that in sleep the influx from outside is cut off, so the common sense becomes available for internal images. This sets up the explanation of dreams.

Quwwa and Qudra: Principle and Concomitant
Al-Razi defines quwwa and relates it to qudra as principle and concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's distinction between quwwa as a genus and qudra as its principle, and non-passivity as its necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
Quwwa is broader than qudra; qudra is its principle, non-passivity its concomitant.

This lesson unpacks a key conceptual analysis by Imam al-Razi regarding the term quwwa (strength). He explains that quwwa originally referred to a capacity in animals for strenuous acts. From this original meaning, he extracts two essential aspects: a principle (qudra) and a necessary concomitant (non-passivity). Understanding this relationship clarifies why quwwa is considered the genus of qudra in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully to get a sense of al-Razi's concise style. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'mabda'' and 'lazim' as they are central.
2
Identify the two aspects of quwwa
Notice that al-Razi first gives the original meaning, then says quwwa has a principle and a concomitant. Identify what each refers to: qudra and non-passivity.
3
Understand the reasoning for the concomitant
Al-Razi explains why non-passivity is a necessary concomitant: because if one were easily passive, strenuous actions would be hindered. Grasp this causal link.
4
Trace the semantic transfer
See how the term quwwa was later transferred to refer to either the principle or the concomitant, explaining why qudra is considered a type of quwwa.
This semantic shift is key to the claim that 'quwwa is the genus of qudra'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث عشر: القوة هي جنس القدرة قال الإمام الرازي: لفظ القوة وضع أولاً للمعنى الموجود في الحيوان الذي يمكنه به أن يصدر عنه أفعال شاقة من باب الحركات ليست بأكثرية الوجود عن الناس، ثم إن للقوة بهذا المعنى مبداً ولازماً، أما المبدأ فهو القدرة أعني كون الحيوان إذا شاء فعل، وإذا لم يشأ لم يفعل، وأما اللازم فهو أن لا ينفعل عن الشيء بسهولة، وذلك لأن مزاول التحريكات الشاقة إذا انفعل عنها صده ذلك عن إتمام فعله، فلا جرم صار اللا انفعال دليلاً على الشدة ثم إنهم نقلوا اسم القوة إلى ذلك
The thirteenth topic: Strength (quwwa) is the genus of power (qudra). Imam al-Razi said: The term quwwa was initially placed for the meaning present in animals by which they can perform strenuous actions of the type of movements that are not of majority existence among people. Then for quwwa in this sense there is a principle (mabda') and a necessary concomitant (lazim). As for the principle, it is qudra, meaning that the animal, if it wills, it acts, and if it does not will, it does not act. As for the necessary concomitant, it is that it is not easily affected (passive) by a thing, because one who undergoes strenuous movements, if he is affected by them, that hinders him from completing his action. Therefore, non-passivity became an evidence for severity. Then they transferred the term quwwa to that (i.e., the principle or the concomitant).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's linguistic and conceptual analysis of the term quwwa (strength/potency). He argues that the original meaning of quwwa is the capacity in animals to perform strenuous movements that are not common. This quwwa has two aspects: a principle (mabda') which is qudra—the power to act or refrain at will—and a necessary concomitant (lazim) which is the characteristic of not being easily passive (i.e., resilience). The reasoning for the concomitant: if someone engaged in strenuous activity were easily overcome (passive), they would be prevented from completing the action; so the absence of such passivity indicates severity/sharpness (shidda). Later, the term quwwa was transferred (by metonymy) to refer either to that principle (qudra) or to that concomitant (non-passivity). This prepared the ground for the statement 'quwwa is the genus of qudra,' meaning that qudra falls under the broader concept of quwwa.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
مبدأ
لازم
لا انفعال
شدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quwwa and qudra are synonymous terms meaning the same thing.
Correction: Quwwa is broader than qudra; qudra is only one aspect (the principle) of quwwa. Quwwa also includes non-passivity as a concomitant.

Checkpoint
What is the principle (mabda') of quwwa according to Imam al-Razi?
A) Lack of passivity
B) Qudra (the ability to act or not when willing)
C) Temperament
D) Exertion

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is the genus that includes qudra as its defining principle and non-passivity as its necessary concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson defines quwwa originally as a capacity for strenuous movement. In your daily life, what 'strenuous movements' do you undertake for your deen? Are you relying on your own qudra, or have you recognized that true quwa comes from Allah? How might this realization change the way you approach tasks that seem overwhelming?

Al-Razi says that qudra is the principle of quwwa: the ability to choose to act or not. Reflect on a recent situation where you felt powerless. Did you still have the choice in how to respond? How can this understanding of qudra as a principle shape your attitude when facing adversity?
?
Non-passivity is the necessary concomitant of quwwa. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you are too passive, easily influenced by doubts or desires? What would it mean for you to cultivate a quwwa that is not easily overcome? Commit to one specific action this week that strengthens your resistance to spiritual weakness.

Power as Genus of Capacity and the Opposition Argument
Defines power and refutes that temperament is capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of power (quwwa) as the genus of capacity (qudra) and the key argument that temperament (mizaj) is distinct from capacity because it opposes capacity.

Core Idea
Temperament opposes capacity; a thing does not oppose itself — so capacity is not temperament.

In this passage, the author sets out the thirteenth objective: defining power as the genus of capacity. He immediately addresses an objection: that temperament might be identical to capacity. He argues that since temperament opposes capacity, and a thing cannot oppose itself, they must be distinct. This lesson introduces this fundamental distinction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the definition and the argument
Find where the author defines power as the genus of capacity. Then locate the argument that temperament is not capacity because it opposes it.
Notice the phrase 'a thing does not oppose itself'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. How does it protect the concept of capacity as a direct attribute?
This distinction prevents confusing a physical quality (temperament) with a metaphysical attribute.

النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة
( الثاني : المزاج قد يمانع القدرة، كما عند اللغوب ) فإن من أصابه لغوب وإعياء يصدر عنه أفعاله بقدرته واختياره ومزاجه يمانع قدرته في تلك الأفعال والشيء لا يمانع نفسه فالقدرة غير المزاج.

المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة ]
بل الثاني عشر قال الإمام الرازي : لفظ القوة وضع أولاً للمعنى الموجود في الحيوان الذي يمكنه به أن يصدر عنه أفعال شاقة من باب الحركات ليست بأكثرية الوجود عن الناس، ثم إن للقوة بهذا المعنى مبداً ولازماً، أما المبدأ فهو القدرة أعني كون الحيوان إذا شاء فعل، وإذا لم يشأ لم يفعل، وأما اللازم فهو أن لا ينفعل عن الشيء بسهولة، وذلك لأن مزاول التحريكات الشاقة إذا انفعل عنها صده ذلك عن إتمام فعله، فلا جرم صار اللا انفعال دليلاً على الشدة ثم إنهم نقلوا اسم القوة إلى ذلك

أن المزاج من جنس الكيفيات الأربع ولا شيء من القدرة كذلك فلا شيء من المزاج بقدرة فلا شيء من القدرة بمزاج .
قوله : (المبدأ وهو القدرة كونها مبدأ باعتبار أن القوة بذلك المعنى هي القدرة الشديدة فكأنه القدرة مع وصف الشدة .

يستدل على التعدد بأن المزاج يوجد في المعادن والنباتات ولا قدرة فيهما فقد ثبت تغايرهما
وفيه أن هذا لا يدل على مغايرة المزاج الحيواني للقدرة الموجودة في الحيوان وهو المقصود. قوله: (قد يمانع القدرة كما عند اللغوب ) فإن قلت : الممانع هو الثقل . قلت : بل المزاج يمانعها باعتبار الثقل والكلال العارض ،له وقد يقال : المتمسك في تحقق القدرة فيمن أصابه الإعياء هو الوجدان فليتمسك به في المغايرة من أول الأمر، وفيه نظر لأنه يتوقف على كون المزاج مدركاً بالوجدان .

قوله : ( أن يصدر إلخ ) المراد من الباء السببية القريبة التي يتبادر منها فلا يصدق التعريف على مبدأ هذا المعنى أعني القدرة المطلقة. قوله : (أما المبدأ فهو القدرة أعني كون الحيوان إلخ ) قال رحمه الله تعالى : القدرة بهذا المعنى مبدأ لجميع الأفعال الاختيارية ولا تختص مبدئيته بمعنى يمكن به صدور الأفعال الشاقة قطعاً، وليس مراده الاختصاص أيضاً، بل بيان مبدئيتها ثم قال : والحاصل أن القوة هي القدرة الكاملة ومبدؤها أصل ،القدرة هذا وفي تفسير القدرة بالكون المذكور مسامحة، لأنه اعتباري بخلافها فالمراد صفة بها الكون .

قوله : فلا جرم صار اللا انفعال دليلاً على الشدة لا يخفى أن وجود اللازم من حيث هو ( لازم، لا يدل على وجود الملزوم إلا أن يثبت المساواة بينهما ولم يصرح بها هاهنا فالأقرب أن يقال : الضعف يلزمه الانفعال فعدم الانفعال يدل على الشدة والقوة.
Fourth Type – Thirteenth Objective: Power (al-quwwa) is the genus of capacity (al-qudra). (Second: Temperament (al-mizāj) may oppose capacity, as in the case of exhaustion (al-lughūb).) For one who is stricken with exhaustion and fatigue performs his actions by his capacity and choice, but his temperament opposes his capacity in those actions; and a thing does not oppose itself, so capacity is not temperament.

Thirteenth Objective: Power is the genus of capacity. Rather, the twelfth [objective]. Imam al-Razi said: The word 'power' was first placed for the meaning found in an animal by which it is able to produce difficult actions of the type of movements that are not most of existence in people. Then for power in this sense there is a principle (mabda') and a necessary concomitant (lāzim). As for the principle, it is capacity, i.e., the animal's being such that if it wills it does, and if it does not will it does not do. As for the necessary concomitant, it is that it is not easily affected by a thing, because one who undertakes difficult movements, if he is affected by them, that prevents him from completing his action. Hence, necessarily, non-affection (al-infiʿāl) became evidence of intensity (al-shidda). Then they transferred the name 'power' to that.

And that temperament is one of the four qualities, and nothing of capacity is like that, so nothing of temperament is capacity, and nothing of capacity is temperament.

His saying: (The principle, and it is capacity: it is a principle because power in that sense is intense capacity, so it is as if it is capacity with the description of intensity.)

One argues for multiplicity by saying that temperament exists in minerals and plants, and there is no capacity in them, so their distinctness is established. But this does not indicate the distinctness of animal temperament from the capacity present in the animal, which is what is intended.

His saying: (It may oppose capacity, as in the case of exhaustion.) For if you say: The opponent is heaviness. I say: Rather, the temperament opposes it by way of the heaviness and fatigue that occur to it. And it may be said: The one who relies on the realization of capacity in one afflicted with fatigue is direct experience (al-wijdān); so let him rely on it for distinctness from the beginning. But there is a problem: it depends on temperament being perceptible by direct experience.

His saying: (that he can produce [such] etc.) The intended meaning of the letter 'bā' is the proximate causative which comes to mind, so the definition does not apply to the principle of this meaning, i.e., absolute capacity.

His saying: (As for the principle, it is capacity, i.e., the animal's being etc.) He said, may God have mercy on him: Capacity in this sense is the principle of all voluntary actions, and its being a principle is not restricted to a meaning by which difficult actions can be produced definitively; nor does he intend restriction, but rather to show its principality. Then he said: The summary is that power is complete capacity, and its principle is the origin of capacity. And in the interpretation of capacity as the aforementioned being (al-kawn) there is lenience, because it is mental consideration (iʿtibārī) unlike it; so what is intended is a quality by which is the being.

His saying: Hence, necessarily, non-affection became evidence of intensity. It is not hidden that the existence of the concomitant insofar as it is a concomitant does not indicate the existence of the necessary condition (malzūm) unless equality between them is established, and it is not explicitly stated here. So the closer [view] is to say: Weakness is necessarily accompanied by affection, so absence of affection indicates intensity and strength.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It opens by stating the objective: power (al-quwwa) is the genus of capacity (al-qudra). The first point is a second objection that temperament (al-mizāj) may oppose capacity, as in exhaustion. The argument is that a person acts by his capacity and choice even when exhausted, but his temperament opposes his capacity, and since a thing does not oppose itself, capacity and temperament are distinct. The text then presents Imam al-Razi's definition: the word 'power' was first used for the ability in animals to perform difficult voluntary actions. This power has a principle (mabda') which is capacity—the state of being able to act if one wills—and a necessary concomitant (lāzim) which is non-affection, i.e., not being easily overcome. Non-affection became evidence of intensity because if one undertaking difficult movements is affected by them, it hinders completion. Then the text discusses a further argument that temperament is among the four qualities, unlike capacity, so they are different. The commentary (qāla) that follows explains nuances: the principle is intense capacity, and an objection about multiplicity is answered by noting that the existence of temperament in minerals does not prove the distinction of animal temperament from capacity. Another objection asks whether the opposing factor is heaviness rather than temperament; the answer is that temperament opposes through heaviness and fatigue. The reliance on direct experience (wijdān) is criticized because it assumes temperament is perceptible. Further commentary explains that the definition of power does not apply to absolute capacity but to a specific kind, and that capacity is the principle of all voluntary actions, not just difficult ones. The summary is that power is complete capacity. Finally, the commentary on non-affection as evidence notes that a concomitant does not indicate the necessary condition unless equality is established, so the stronger argument is that weakness entails affection, so absence of affection indicates strength.

Key Terms
القوة
القدرة
المزاج
اللغوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temperament (al-mizāj) is the same as capacity (al-qudra).
Correction: The passage argues that temperament opposes capacity, and a thing cannot oppose itself, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main argument that temperament (al-mizāj) is not capacity (al-qudra)?
A) Temperament is found in minerals and plants, but capacity is not.
B) Temperament opposes capacity, and a thing does not oppose itself.
C) Capacity is a quality, while temperament is a substance.
D) Direct experience shows they are different.

Key Takeaway
Power is the genus of capacity, and temperament is not capacity because it opposes the subject's capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel exhausted, do you still believe you have the capacity to choose righteous actions, or do you use fatigue as an excuse? What does this passage demand from you?
?
This lesson shows that a thing does not oppose itself. Is there an area in your life where you claim a created thing (like your temperament, habits, or environment) is preventing you from fulfilling a duty, yet you still attribute that to your own capacity? Are you contradictory?
?
How does knowing that your capacity is distinct from your physical state affect your accountability before Allah in moments of weakness?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Al-Razi's Analysis: Power, Capacity, and Non-Affection
Analyses power's linguistic origin and its relation to capacity and non-affection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's analysis of the term 'power', its principle (capacity), and its necessary concomitant (non-affection), and how this supports the distinction from temperament.

Core Idea
Power originally meant ability for difficult acts; its principle is capacity, its concomitant is non-affection.

Imam al-Razi provides a detailed linguistic and conceptual analysis of the term 'power' (quwwa). He explains that it was first used for the ability in animals to perform difficult voluntary actions. This ability has a principle, which is capacity (qudra), and a necessary concomitant, which is non-affection (la infi'al). This lesson explores this framework and the additional argument that temperament is among the four qualities, unlike capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage from al-Razi's quote through the commentary. Understand the flow of definitions and arguments.
2
Identify the three elements
Find the original meaning of power, its principle (capacity), and its concomitant (non-affection). Note how each is defined.
3
Understand the reasoning
Follow the reasoning that non-affection is evidence of intensity. Then see how the quality argument distinguishes temperament from capacity.
4
Reflect on the objections
Consider the objections raised in the commentary, such as the reliance on direct experience and the scope of the definition. How are they resolved?

?
