The Transfer of Power to Possibility
Explains the transference of power to possibility and the renaming of existence as act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of power (al-quwwa) is extended from capacity (al-qudra) to the possibility (al-imkān) of an act, and the associated terminological shifts.

Core Idea
Power is transferred from the capacity of an agent to the possibility of a state.

This lesson unpacks how the term 'power' (قوة) was extended beyond its original meaning of capacity. By tracing the logical steps from capacity to possibility, we see how theological and philosophical language developed a richer vocabulary for discussing potentiality and actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the recurring terms: القوة, القدرة, الإمكان.
The passage begins by restating the principle and necessary concomitant from the previous lesson.
2
Identify the two key extensions
The passage presents two things related to capacity: the effective attribute (الصفة المؤثرة) and the necessary concomitant (اللازم) which is possibility (الإمكان). Locate these in the text.
3
Understand the transference of 'power'
The text says they 'transferred the name power to that'—i.e., to possibility. Reflect on why they did this: because a capable agent can either act or not act, so possibility necessarily accompanies capacity.
This is a crucial step: power now names the possibility itself.
4
Note the further shift: existence called 'act'
They also called existence (الوجود) and obtaining (الحصول) 'act' (فعل), even though these are passive, because the original meaning of power was related to act.
This shows how language adapts to logical relations.
5
Apply to the example of white
The passage gives the example: 'they say of white: it is potentially, i.e., it can become black'. See how the transferred meaning works in practice.
This solidifies the conceptual shift.

النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة
المبدأ وهو القدرة، وإلى ذلك اللازم وهو اللاانفعال، ثم إن للقدرة وصفاً هو كالجنس لها أعني الصفة المؤثرة في الغير ولها لازم هو الإمكان لأن القادر لما صح منه أن يفعل منه أن لا يفعل كان إمكان الفعل لازماً للقدرة، فنقلوا اسم القوة إلى ذلك وصح الجنس، وذلك اللازم فيقولون للأبيض: إنه بالقوة أي يمكن أن يصير وسموا الحصول والوجود فعلاً، وإن كان في الحقيقة انفعالاً بناء على أن المعنى الذي وضع له لفظ القوة أولاً كان متعلقاً بالفعل، فلما سموا هاهنا الإمكان قوة سموا الأمر الذي تعلق به الإمكان، وهو الوجود والحصول فعلاً، والمهندسون يجعلون مربع الخط قوة له كأنه أمر ممكن في ذلك الخط، خصوصاً إذا اعتقد ما ذهب إليه بعضهم من أن حدوث المربع بحركة ذلك الخط على مثله ولذلك قالوا : وتر القائمة قوى على ضلعيها أي مربعه يساوي مربعيهما، وإذا انتقش هذه المعاني على صحيفة خاطرك، فلنرجع إلى ما

قوله : ( أعني الصفة المؤثرة فإن أريد بالصفة ما يعم الجوهر والعرض كان شاملاً للطبيعة والصور النوعية كما مر وإن خص بالعرض فلا .
قوله : ( أعني الصفة المؤثرة لو بدل الصفة بالأمر ليظهر تناوله للصورة الجوهرية المؤثرة لكان أولى .
قوله : ( ولها لازم هو الإمكان بين الإمام العلاقة بين القوة والإمكان المطلق والذي يطلق عليه القوة هو الإمكان المقارن للعدم فلا تقريب وسيشير إليه المصنف . قوله : ( بناء على أن المعنى الذي إلخ) حاصل ما ذكره أنه شبه الحصول والوجود بالفعل في كونه متعلقاً للقوة وإن كانت القوة في الموضعين بمعنيين . قوله: (والمهندسون يجعلون إلخ قال في المباحث المشرقية ثم إن المهندسين لما وجدوا بعض الخطوط من شأنه أن يكون ضلعاً لمربع وبعضها ليس ممكناً له ذلك جعلوا ذلك المربع كانه أمر ممكن في ذلك الخط ، وخصوصاً لما اعتقد بعضهم أن حدوث المربع هو بحركة ذلك الضلع على مثل نفسه ثم قال : فإذا عرفت القوة عرفت القوي وعرفت أن غير القوي إما الضعيف وإما العاجز وإما سهل الانفعال وإما الضروري، وإما غير المؤثر أن لا يكون المقدار ضلعاً لمقدار سطحي مفروض فقد ظهر من كلامه مقابل كل معنى من معاني القوة وأنه
الخطي
مشتق من القوة القوي على جميع المعاني . قوله : (خصوصاً إذا اعتقد ما ذهب إليه بعضهم إلخ ) قال الله رحمه تعالى : هذا الاعتقاد ضعیف لأن السطح لا يحصل من الخط كما أن الخط لا يحصل من النقطة وكما أن الجسم لا يحصل من السطح بل هي أعراض لها ولا يتقدم العرض بالوجود على محله القائم هو به نعم يتوهم حركة الخط على مثله بتخيل المربع، وأما أن حدوثه فيها فكلا . قوله : ( ولذلك قالوا : وتر القائمة قوي على ضلعيها أي مربعه يساوي مربعيهما أي ولأن المهندسين يجعلون مربع الخط قوة له قالوا وتر القائمة قوي على ضلعيها أي على مربع ضلعيها،
Fourth Type – Thirteenth Objective: Power is the genus of capacity. The principle, and it is capacity, and that necessary concomitant is non-affection. Then for capacity there is a description that is like a genus for it, meaning the effective attribute (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira) in another, and for it there is a necessary concomitant which is possibility (al-imkān), because when the capable one is such that it is valid for him to act and valid for him not to act, the possibility of the act is a necessary concomitant of capacity. So they transferred the name 'power' to that and the genus became correct, and that necessary concomitant. So they say of white: it is potentially (bi-al-quwwa), i.e., it can become. And they named obtaining (al-ḥuṣūl) and existence (al-wujūd) 'act', even though in reality it is passivity (infiʿāl), based on the fact that the meaning for which the word 'power' was first placed was related to act. So when they here named possibility 'power', they named the thing to which possibility pertains, which is existence and obtaining, 'act'. And the geometricians make the square of a line a power for it, as if it is something possible in that line, especially if one believes what some have held, that the occurrence of the square is by the movement of that line upon its like. That is why they said: The hypotenuse of a right-angled [triangle] is powerful over its two sides, i.e., its square equals the squares of the two sides. And when these meanings are engraved on the tablet of your mind, then let us return to what...

His saying: (I mean the effective attribute; if by attribute is meant what includes substance and accident, it includes nature and specific forms as previously, but if restricted to accident, then no.)
His saying: (I mean the effective attribute; if 'attribute' were replaced by 'thing' to show its inclusion of the effective substantial form, it would be better.)
His saying: (And for it there is a necessary concomitant which is possibility. The imam explained the relation between power and absolute possibility, and what is called 'power' is possibility accompanied by non-existence, so there is no approximation, and the author will indicate it.) His saying: (Based on the fact that the meaning etc.) The summary of what he mentioned is that he likened obtaining and existence to an act in being related to power, even though power in the two places has two meanings. His saying: (And the geometricians make etc.) He said in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: Then the geometricians, when they found that some lines are such that they can be a side of a square and some are not possible for that, made that square as if it is something possible in that line, especially when some believed that the occurrence of the square is by the movement of that side upon its like. Then he said: So if you know power, you know the powerful, and you know that what is not powerful is either the weak, or the incapable, or easily affected, or the necessary, or non-effective—that the magnitude is not a side of a presupposed surface area. So it appears from his speech the counterpart of each meaning of power, and that the powerful is derived from power in all meanings. His saying: (Especially if one believes what some have held etc.) He said, may God have mercy on him: This belief is weak because a surface does not come from a line, just as a line does not come from a point, and just as a body does not come from a surface; rather, they are accidents of it, and an accident does not precede in existence its substrate which subsists by it. Yes, the movement of a line upon its like can be imagined by imagining the square, but as for its occurrence in it, no. His saying: (That is why they said: The hypotenuse of a right-angled [triangle] is powerful over its two sides, i.e., its square equals the squares of the two sides, i.e., because the geometricians make the square of a line a power for it, they said the hypotenuse of a right-angled [triangle] is powerful over its two sides, i.e., over the square of its two sides.)
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an extension of the concept of power (al-quwwa) beyond its original meaning related to capacity (al-qudra). It begins by restating the principle (mabda') and necessary concomitant (lāzim) from the previous lesson. It then explains that capacity has a description like a genus: the effective attribute (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira) in another, and a necessary concomitant which is possibility (al-imkān). Because a capable agent can either act or not act, the possibility of the act necessarily accompanies capacity. The term 'power' was then transferred to this possibility, and also to the necessary concomitant (non-affection). For example, white is said to be 'potentially' (bi-al-quwwa) black, meaning it can become black. The text notes that they called obtaining and existence 'act' even though it is actually passivity, because the original meaning of power was related to act. The passage then applies this to geometry: geometricians consider the square of a line as a power of that line, as if the square is a possibility inherent in the line, especially if one believes that a square is generated by the movement of a line along its like. Hence, the Pythagorean theorem is expressed as 'the hypotenuse is powerful over its two sides' meaning its square equals the sum of the squares of the sides. The commentary (qāla) that follows clarifies several points: the effective attribute can include substance and accident; replacing 'attribute' with 'thing' would better include substantial forms; and the relation between power and absolute possibility is explained, noting that what is called 'power' here is possibility concomitant with non-existence. The geometric example is further elaborated with a citation from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, explaining that lines that can form a square are considered to have the square as a power. The commentary then critiques the belief that a square is generated by the movement of a line, stating that a surface does not actually come from a line; the line's movement is only imaginary. Finally, it reaffirms the geometric usage of 'power' in the Pythagorean theorem.

Key Terms

القدرة
الصفة المؤثرة
الإمكان
الوجود
الحصول
الانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power (al-quwwa) always refers to the capacity of an agent.
Correction: In this passage, 'power' is extended to mean the possibility of a state, as in 'potentially black', and also to the square of a line in geometry.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did they call existence and obtaining 'act'?
A) Because existence is always an action.
B) Because the original meaning of power was related to act, and existence is what possibility pertains to.
C) Because existence is the same as capacity.
D) Because the capable agent acts directly.

Key Takeaway
The term 'power' is extended from the capacity of an agent to the possibility of a state, and existence is metaphorically called 'act' due to its relation to power.

Geometric Power: The Square of the Hypotenuse
Explains the geometric extension of 'power' to the square of a line and the Pythagorean theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how geometricians apply the term 'power' to the square of a line and how the Pythagorean theorem is expressed in this terminology.

Core Idea
In geometry, a line's 'power' is its square, and the hypotenuse's power equals the sum of the powers of the sides.

This lesson explores the geometric extension of 'power'. Geometricians took the transferred meaning of possibility and applied it to the relationship between a line and its square. This led to a unique way of stating the Pythagorean theorem: the hypotenuse is 'powerful' over its two sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometric passage
Read the Arabic passage about geometricians and the Pythagorean theorem. Note the key terms: المربع, الخط, وتر القائمة.
The passage continues from the previous lesson and applies the same concept of power.
2
Understand the geometricians' usage
Geometricians considered the square of a line as a 'power' of that line, as if the square is a possibility inherent in the line. This is analogous to the logical possibility discussed earlier.
They also believed that a square could be generated by the movement of a line upon its like.
3
Examine the Pythagorean theorem example
The passage states: 'وتر القائمة قوى على ضلعيها أي مربعه يساوي مربعيهما' meaning the hypotenuse's square equals the sum of the squares of the two sides. Here 'powerful' means having a square relationship.
This is a direct application of the term 'power' in geometry.
4
Read the commentary's critique
The commentary criticizes the belief that a square arises from the movement of a line, arguing that a surface does not come from a line. This shows another level of analysis.
The critique does not affect the linguistic usage; it only challenges the generative theory.

According to the passage, why did geometricians say 'the hypotenuse is powerful over its two sides'?
A) Because the hypotenuse is longer than the sides.
B) Because the square of the hypotenuse equals the squares of the sides.
C) Because the hypotenuse can generate a square by movement.

Key Takeaway
In geometry, the term 'power' is used to denote the square of a line, leading to the expression of the Pythagorean theorem as the hypotenuse being 'powerful' over the legs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You use words like 'power' in many contexts. When you learned that a line has a 'power'—its square—did you pause to consider that the same Arabic root (quwwa) ties geometry, logic, and even your own abilities? What does that unity of language teach you about the unity of knowledge from Allah?
?
The commentary says the movement of a line to generate a square is 'only imaginary.' How often do you treat mental constructs—like your own plans or assumptions—as if they are physical realities? Will you examine today one such construct and ask whether it is real or imaginary?
?
If the hypotenuse's 'power' is not a separate thing but an inherent possibility, what does that tell you about how hidden capabilities in you (like patience, gratitude) might be actualized? Will you identify one 'line' in your character today and nurture its 'square'?

Quwwa as Genus of Qudra and Its Necessary Concomitant Imkān
Defines quwwa as genus of qudra and its necessary concomitant imkān

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quwwa (power/potentiality) functions as the genus of qudra (capacity), and the relationship between qudra, the influential attribute, and its necessary concomitant imkān (possibility).

Core Idea
Quwwa is the genus of qudra; its necessary implication is imkān (possibility).

This lesson establishes the foundational conceptual relationship between qudra and quwwa. The author identifies qudra as the principle and distinguishes its necessary concomitant, lā-infi‘āl. Then he introduces the attribute that influences as a genus-like description, and derives imkān as a necessary implication. Understanding these relationships is essential for grasping the broader semantic field of quwwa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on quwwa as genus
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It defines quwwa as the genus of qudra and explains the principle and necessary concomitant.
Notice the term 'lā-infi‘āl' (non-affectedness).
2
Identify the influential attribute
The author describes qudra as having a description that is like its genus: the attribute that influences others. Understand why this attribute is mentioned.
This attribute is the source of qudra's efficacy.
3
Derive imkān as necessary concomitant
The passage argues that because a capable being can act or not act, the possibility of the act is necessary for qudra. This possibility is called quwwa by transfer.
The example of a white thing being 'in potentiality' illustrates this.
4
Reflect on the transfer of meaning
Notice how the term quwwa shifts from active power to potentiality. The author explains why existence is called 'act' even when it is a passion.
This sets the stage for other transferred meanings.

النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة
المبدأ وهو القدرة، وإلى ذلك اللازم وهو اللاانفعال، ثم إن للقدرة وصفاً هو كالجنس لها أعني الصفة المؤثرة في الغير ولها لازم هو الإمكان لأن القادر لما صح منه أن يفعل منه أن لا يفعل كان إمكان الفعل لازماً للقدرة، فنقلوا اسم القوة إلى ذلك وصح الجنس، وذلك اللازم فيقولون للأبيض: إنه بالقوة أي يمكن أن يصير وسموا الحصول والوجود فعلاً، وإن كان في الحقيقة انفعالاً بناء على أن المعنى الذي وضع له لفظ القوة أولاً كان متعلقاً بالفعل، فلما سموا هاهنا الإمكان قوة سموا الأمر الذي تعلق به الإمكان، وهو الوجود والحصول فعلاً، والمهندسون يجعلون مربع الخط قوة له كأنه أمر ممكن في ذلك الخط، خصوصاً إذا اعتقد ما ذهب إليه بعضهم من أن حدوث المربع بحركة ذلك الخط على مثله ولذلك قالوا : وتر القائمة قوى على ضلعيها أي مربعه يساوي مربعيهما، وإذا انتقش هذه المعاني على صحيفة خاطرك، فلنرجع إلى ما
قوله : ( أعني الصفة المؤثرة فإن أريد بالصفة ما يعم الجوهر والعرض كان شاملاً للطبيعة والصور النوعية كما مر وإن خص بالعرض فلا .
قوله : ( أعني الصفة المؤثرة لو بدل الصفة بالأمر ليظهر تناوله للصورة الجوهرية المؤثرة
لكان أولى .
قوله : ( ولها لازم هو الإمكان بين الإمام العلاقة بين القوة والإمكان المطلق والذي يطلق عليه القوة هو الإمكان المقارن للعدم فلا تقريب وسيشير إليه المصنف . قوله : ( بناء على أن المعنى الذي إلخ) حاصل ما ذكره أنه شبه الحصول والوجود بالفعل في
كونه متعلقاً للقوة وإن كانت القوة في الموضعين بمعنيين . قوله: (والمهندسون يجعلون إلخ قال في المباحث المشرقية ثم إن المهندسين لما وجدوا بعض الخطوط من شأنه أن يكون ضلعاً لمربع وبعضها ليس ممكناً له ذلك جعلوا ذلك المربع كانه أمر ممكن في ذلك الخط ، وخصوصاً لما اعتقد بعضهم أن حدوث المربع هو بحركة ذلك الضلع على مثل نفسه ثم قال : فإذا عرفت القوة عرفت القوي وعرفت أن غير القوي إما الضعيف وإما العاجز وإما سهل الانفعال وإما الضروري، وإما غير المؤثر أن لا يكون المقدار ضلعاً لمقدار سطحي مفروض فقد ظهر من كلامه مقابل كل معنى من معاني القوة وأنه
الخطي
مشتق من القوة القوي على جميع المعاني . قوله : (خصوصاً إذا اعتقد ما ذهب إليه بعضهم إلخ ) قال الله رحمه تعالى : هذا الاعتقاد ضعیف لأن السطح لا يحصل من الخط كما أن الخط لا يحصل من النقطة وكما أن الجسم لا يحصل من السطح بل هي أعراض لها ولا يتقدم العرض بالوجود على محله القائم هو به نعم يتوهم حركة الخط على مثله بتخيل المربع، وأما أن حدوثه فيها فكلا . قوله : ( ولذلك قالوا : وتر القائمة قوي على ضلعيها أي مربعه يساوي مربعيهما أي ولأن المهندسين يجعلون مربع الخط قوة له قالوا وتر القائمة قوي على ضلعيها أي على مربع ضلعيها،
The fourth type - the thirteenth objective: Al-quwwa (power/potentiality) is the genus of al-qudra (capacity).
The principle is al-qudra, and to that the necessary [luzum] is al-lā-infi‘āl (non-affectedness). Then, for qudra there is a description that is like its genus, namely the attribute that influences others (al-ṣifa al-mu’aththira fī l-ghayr), and it has a necessary implication which is al-imkān (possibility), because when it is valid for the capable one to act or not to act, the possibility of the act is necessary for qudra. So they transferred the name of quwwa to that and the genus became correct, and that necessary [thing] they say about the white [thing]: 'it is in potentiality' i.e., it can become. And they named obtaining (al-ḥuṣūl) and existence (al-wujūd) 'act' (fi‘l), even though in reality it is 'passion' (infi‘āl), based on the fact that the meaning for which the word quwwa was first placed was related to act. So when they here named imkān as quwwa, they named the thing to which imkān is related, which is existence and obtaining, as 'act'. And the engineers make the square of a line (murabba‘ al-khaṭṭ) a power for it, as if it is a possible matter in that line, especially if one believes what some have said that the occurrence of the square is by the movement of that line upon its like. That is why they said: the chord of a right angle is 'powerful' (qawī) upon its two sides, i.e., its square equals the squares of the two sides. And when these meanings are engraved upon the tablet of your mind, let us return to what...
His saying: (I mean the attribute that influences) if by 'attribute' is meant what includes substance and accident, it is comprehensive of nature and specific forms as has passed; and if it is restricted to accident, then no.
His saying: (I mean the attribute that influences) if only 'attribute' were replaced with 'thing' to show its inclusion of the substantial form that influences, it would be better.
His saying: (And it has a necessary implication which is imkān) between al-Imām the relationship between quwwa and absolute imkān, and what is called quwwa is the imkān concomitant with non-existence, so it is not close, and the author will refer to it.
His saying: (Based on the fact that the meaning etc.) The summary of what he mentioned is that he likened obtaining and existence to act, in that it is related to quwwa, even though quwwa in the two places is two different meanings.
His saying: (And the engineers make etc.) He said in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: "Then the engineers, when they found that some lines are such that they can be a side of a square and some are not possible for that, they made that square as if it is a possible matter in that line, and especially when some believed that the occurrence of the square is by the movement of that side upon itself." Then he said: "And if you know quwwa, you know al-qawī (the powerful), and you know that other than al-qawī is either al-ḍa‘īf (the weak), al-‘ājiz (the incapable), sahl al-infi‘āl (easily affected), al-ḍarūrī (the necessary), or ghayr al-mu’aththir (the non-influential), that the measure cannot be a side of a superficial measure supposed. So it appeared from his speech the counterpart of each meaning of the meanings of quwwa and that it is derived from quwwa, al-qawī, in all meanings.
His saying: (Especially if one believes what some have gone to etc.) He said - may God have mercy on him -: This belief is weak, because a surface does not come about from a line just as a line does not come about from a point, and just as a body does not come about from a surface. Rather, they are accidents of them, and an accident does not precede in existence its substrate in which it subsists. Yes, one can imagine the movement of a line upon its like by imagining the square, but that its occurrence in it is not.
His saying: (And therefore they said: the chord of a right angle is powerful upon its two sides, i.e., its square equals the squares of its two sides) i.e., because the engineers make the square of the line a power for it, they said the chord of a right angle is powerful upon its two sides, i.e., upon the square of its two sides.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the term 'quwwa' (power/potentiality) as the genus of 'qudra' (capacity). The author first identifies the principle as qudra and then describes its necessary concomitant: non-affectedness (lā-infi‘āl). Then he discusses a description of qudra that is like its genus—the attribute that influences others—and its necessary implication, imkān (possibility). Because a capable being can either act or not act, possibility of the act is necessary for qudra. Therefore, the name quwwa was transferred to this possibility. The author gives the example of a white thing: we say it is 'in potentiality' (bi-l-quwwa) meaning it can become black. Similarly, they called obtaining and existence 'act' (fi‘l) even though they are passions, because the original meaning of quwwa pertained to act. Then he mentions the engineers' usage: they call the square of a line its quwwa, as if the square is a possible matter in the line, especially if one holds that the square occurs by the movement of that line upon itself. Hence they say 'the chord of a right angle is powerful upon its two sides,' meaning its square equals the sum of the squares of the two sides. The passage then includes marginal glosses that comment on the phrasing of the main text: whether 'attribute' should be taken broadly to include substance and accident, and whether it should be replaced with 'thing' to include substantial forms. Further glosses clarify the relationship between quwwa and imkān, and criticize the belief that a surface can arise from a line, arguing that such generation is impossible. The glosses also note that the term 'qawī' (powerful) is derived from quwwa in all its meanings.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
إمكان
صفة مؤثرة
فعل
انفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quwwa (power) always means active power like qudra.
Correction: Quwwa is a genus that includes both active power (qudra) and potentiality (imkān); here it is used for possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean to say a white thing is 'in potentiality' (bi-l-quwwa)?
A) It has the power to become white.
B) It is potentially black.
C) It is actually white.
D) It is a necessary being.

Key Takeaway
Quwwa as a genus includes both the attribute that influences (qudra) and its necessary concomitant imkān, which is the potentiality for act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You possess qudra (capacity) to obey Allah, but this passage insists that qudra necessarily implies imkān—the possibility of not acting. When you fail to perform an obligation, do you attribute that failure to your imkān or to a defect in your qudra? How does your answer change the way you seek forgiveness and resolve to act next time? Pinpoint one act of worship you have the capacity to do today but might omit, and commit right now to performing it as an actualization of your quwwa.
?
The passage distinguishes between quwwa as potentiality (imkān) and qudra as actual capacity. In your daily life, do you live more from your potential (what you could be) or from your actual capacity (what you do)? Describe a concrete situation today where you can move from mere possibility to actual performance of a good deed, and how that movement reflects the relationship between quwwa and fi‘l described in this lesson.
?
Consider the statement: 'The capable one can either act or not act.' If you truly believe that your capacity does not compel you to act, what does that mean for your accountability? Do you use your qudra to choose obedience, or do you let the possibility of omission become a reality? Name one specific decision you will make today to ensure that your qudra results in a good act rather than remaining mere imkān.

Transferred Meanings of Quwwa: Geometric Usage and Critique
Explains geometric usage of quwwa and critique of surface generation from line

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how engineers use quwwa to denote potentiality in geometry, and why the author critiques the idea that a surface can arise from a line.

Core Idea
Engineers use quwwa to mean potentiality, but the author rejects that a surface is generated from a line.

This lesson explores a transferred meaning of quwwa in geometry. Engineers refer to the square of a line as its power (quwwa), indicating that the line can potentially be a side of a square. However, the author critiques this by arguing that a surface does not come into existence from a line, because accidents cannot precede their substrates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on geometric quwwa
Read the Arabic passage that describes how engineers use quwwa for the square of a line.
Note the phrase 'murabba‘ al-khaṭṭ' (square of the line).
2
Understand the geometric analogy
Engineers say the square is 'quwwa' for the line, meaning the line is potentially a side of that square. They also say the chord of a right angle is 'powerful' upon its two sides.
This is linked to the Pythagorean theorem.
3
Examine the critique
The author criticizes the belief that a square comes about by movement of a line. He argues that a surface cannot be generated from a line, as accidents do not precede their substances.
The critique uses an analogy: line from point, body from surface.
4
Reflect on the overall meaning of quwwa
Consider how this geometric usage fits with the other meanings of quwwa as potentiality.
The glosses also discuss other aspects of quwwa.

The fourth type - the thirteenth objective: Al-quwwa (power/potentiality) is the genus of al-qudra (capacity).
The principle is al-qudra, and to that the necessary [luzum] is al-lā-infi‘āl (non-affectedness). Then, for qudra there is a description that is like its genus, namely the attribute that influences others (al-ṣifa al-mu’aththira fī l-ghayr), and it has a necessary implication which is al-imkān (possibility), because when it is valid for the capable one to act or not to act, the possibility of the act is necessary for qudra. And they named obtaining (al-ḥuṣūl) and existence (al-wujūd) 'act' (fi‘l), even though in reality it is 'passion' (infi‘āl), based on the fact that the meaning for which the word quwwa was first placed was related to act. His saying: (I mean the attribute that influences) if only 'attribute' were replaced with 'thing' to show its inclusion of the substantial form that influences, it would be better.
His saying: (And it has a necessary implication which is imkān) between al-Imām the relationship between quwwa and absolute imkān, and what is called quwwa is the imkān concomitant with non-existence, so it is not close, and the author will refer to it.
His saying: (Based on the fact that the meaning etc.) The summary of what he mentioned is that he likened obtaining and existence to act, in that it is related to quwwa, even though quwwa in the two places is two different meanings.
His saying: (And the engineers make etc.) He said in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: "Then the engineers, when they found that some lines are such that they can be a side of a square and some are not possible for that, they made that square as if it is a possible matter in that line, and especially when some believed that the occurrence of the square is by the movement of that side upon itself." Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the term 'quwwa' (power/potentiality) as the genus of 'qudra' (capacity). Then he mentions the engineers' usage: they call the square of a line its quwwa, as if the square is a possible matter in the line, especially if one holds that the square occurs by the movement of that line upon itself. Checkpoint
What do engineers mean when they say 'the square of a line is a power (quwwa) for it'?
A) The line has the active power to produce the square.
B) The line is potentially a side of that square.
C) The line is the cause of the square.
D) The line and the square are identical.

Key Takeaway
Engineers use quwwa to denote potentiality in geometry, but the author critiques the underlying assumption that a surface can be generated from a line.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author warns that the engineers' usage of quwwa in geometry is not real but only a mental picture. Think of something in your own life—a skill, a possession, a relationship—that you treat as though it has inherent power to produce results. In what ways have you mistaken a conceptual relationship (like the formula for success) for a real cause? What would it mean for you to stop attributing power to that abstraction and instead recognize that all true power belongs to Allah alone?
?
The critique in this passage hinges on the impossibility of a surface coming from a line. In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed that one small step (like a single good deed or a moment of worship) automatically 'generates' a major outcome (like Jannah or a change of heart)? How does the author's insistence that generation does not happen in this way challenge your assumptions about how Allah's creation works?
?
When you recite the Pythagorean theorem or think about mathematical relationships, do you ever feel a sense of awe at the 'power' of numbers? This lesson reminds us that such feelings can be misplaced. Ask yourself: where in your daily routine do you find yourself awed by created things rather than the Creator? What would it take to redirect that awe fully to Allah?

Ibn Sina's Definition of Quwwa as a Principle of Change in Another
Ibn Sina's formal definition of quwwa with commentary on its qualifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as a principle of change in another and the significance of the phrase 'insofar as it is another.'

Core Idea
Quwwa is a principle of change in another, not in itself.

Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa is precise and includes an important qualifier to cover cases like self-treatment. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows why the nuance matters for distinguishing active power.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its qualifier
Read the Arabic definition of quwwa and notice the phrase 'insofar as it is another'.
This phrase is the key to the definition.
2
Understand why the qualifier was added
The qualifier is intended to include cases where the agent and patient are the same entity but considered in different respects.
3
Examine the example of a doctor treating himself
The doctor influences his own body insofar as he is a knower of medicine, acting according to its requirements, and he is affected.
This shows how the definition applies to self-action.
4
Recognize the broader context
This definition is part of the discussion on qudra, and other meanings of quwwa are discussed elsewhere.
The author notes that this discussion is digressive to explain usages of quwwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتاب فنقول : ( القوة تقال للقدرة والمراد هاهنا (جنسها) أي المقصود في المقصد بيان القوة التي هي جنس القدرة ( وهو ) كما قاله ابن سينا : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وقولنا : من حيث هو آخر ليدخل فيه ) أي في هذا الحد ) المعالج لنفسه فإنه يؤثر من حيث هو عالم بصناعة الطب ( عامل بمقتضاها ) ويتأثر
قوله : ( القوة تقال للقدرة إلخ ) فبهذه المناسبة أورد بيان معاني القوة في بحث القدرة . قوله : ( يعني أن المقصود إلخ بخلاف المعاني الآخر فإن بيانها موكول في المقاصد الأخرى، وذكرها هاهنا استطرادي لبيان إطلاقات القوة فإن بيان الإمكان قد مر في الأمور العامة والقدرة قد مر في المقاصد السابقة، والانفعال سيجيء في الكيفيات الاستعدادية.
هو
الخط
وأرادوا أن مربعه يساوي مربعيهما والقريب أن مجموع مربعيهما، حينئذ يكون قوة للوتر فيندرج في قولهم: مربع الخط قوة ،له وهذا الكلام يستدعي نوع توضيح فنقول : وتر القائمة له، الواصل بين ضلعيها والمربع قد يطلق على العدد الحاصل من ضرب عدد في نفسه، مثلا إذا ضرب عشرة في نفسها حصل مئة فالمئة مربع العشرة والعشرة جذر المئة، وقد يطلق على سطح يحيط به أربعة أضلاع متساوية، وهو المراد هاهنا كما هو الظاهر ومساواة مربع الوتر بمربعي الضلعين يستقيم على كلا المعنيين فلنتصور على المعنى الأول ليتضح على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فنقول : إذا فرضنا كلا من ضلعي القائمة عشرة أجزاء متساوية فمربع كل ضلع مئة مثلها لأنها الحاصلة من ضرب عشرة في نفسها ومربع الوتر يساوي مربع مجموع الضلعين، أعني المئتين فالوتر جذر مئتين وإنه فوق أربعة عشر جزءا وأقل من خمسة عشر وذلك لأن الحاصل من ضرب أربعة عشر في نفسها مئة وستة وتسعون من ضرب خمسة عشر في نفسها مئتان وخسمة وعشرون فلا بد أن يكون جذر المئتين فيما بينهما، وإذا تخيلت هذا تخيلت المساواة على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فليتأمل.
The book, so we say: 'Al-quwwa is said of al-qudra, and what is intended here is its genus, i.e., the objective in this chapter is to explain the quwwa that is the genus of qudra. And it is, as Ibn Sīnā said: 'a principle of change in another insofar as it is another.' And our saying 'insofar as it is another' is to include in it (i.e., in this definition) the one who treats himself, for he influences insofar as he is a knower of the medical profession, acting according to its requirements, and he is affected.' 
His saying: 'Al-quwwa is said of qudra etc.' So on this occasion he presented the explanation of the meanings of quwwa in the discussion of qudra. His saying: 'He means that the objective etc.' unlike the other meanings, for their explanation is relegated to other chapters, and their mention here is digressive to explain the usages of quwwa, for the explanation of imkān has already passed in the general matters, and qudra has passed in the previous chapters, and infi‘āl will come in the preparatory qualities.
He [said]: the line. And they intended that its square equals the squares of the two sides, and the correct [view] is that the sum of the squares of the two sides, then it is a power for the chord, so it is included in their saying: the square of the line is a power for it. And this speech requires a kind of clarification. So we say: the chord of a right angle is the line connecting its two sides. And 'square' may be applied to the number resulting from multiplying a number by itself, for example, if you multiply ten by itself you get one hundred, so one hundred is the square of ten, and ten is the root of one hundred. And it may be applied to a surface bounded by four equal sides, and that is intended here as is apparent. And the equality of the square of the chord to the squares of the two sides holds for both meanings. So let us conceive it according to the first meaning to make it clear for the second meaning as well. So we say: if we suppose each of the two sides of the right angle to be ten equal parts, then the square of each side is one hundred like them because it results from multiplying ten by itself, and the square of the chord equals the square of the sum of the two sides, namely two hundred. So the chord is the root of two hundred, and it is greater than fourteen parts and less than fifteen, because the result of multiplying fourteen by itself is one hundred and ninety-six, and the result of multiplying fifteen by itself is two hundred and twenty-five. So the root of two hundred must be between them. And if you imagine this, you imagine the equality for the second meaning as well. So contemplate.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a two-part lesson. First, it presents the formal definition of quwwa from Ibn Sīnā: 'a principle of change in another insofar as it is another.' The phrase 'insofar as it is another' is crucial: it includes the case of a doctor treating himself, because the doctor influences his own body insofar as he is a knower of medicine, even though he is both agent and patient. The definition thus distinguishes quwwa as active power. The commentary then explains that this discussion is part of the chapter on qudra, and that other meanings of quwwa (like imkān and infi‘āl) are dealt with elsewhere. The second part provides a geometric illustration of the term quwwa as used by engineers. The author explains that when engineers say 'the square of a line is its power (quwwa),' they mean that the line is potentially the side of a square. The phrase 'the chord of a right angle is powerful upon its two sides' means that the square of the chord equals the sum of the squares of the two sides. The author then clarifies the two meanings of 'square' (murabba‘): a numerical square (product of a number by itself) and a geometric square (a plane figure bounded by four equal sides). He shows that the Pythagorean theorem holds for both meanings. Using a numerical example with sides of 10, he demonstrates that the chord's square is 200, so its root is between 14 and 15. He concludes that one should contemplate the equality in both senses.

Key Terms
مبدأ التغير
آخر
قوة
قدرة
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of quwwa as 'principle of change in another' excludes self-action because the agent and patient are the same.
Correction: The qualifier 'insofar as it is another' allows inclusion of self-treatment by considering the agent in one respect and patient in another.

Checkpoint
Why did Ibn Sīnā add 'insofar as it is another' to the definition of quwwa?
A) To exclude passive powers.
B) To include the case of a doctor treating himself.
C) To limit quwwa to physical actions.
D) To distinguish quwwa from qudra.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa includes a crucial qualifier to cover cases where the agent and patient are the same entity in different respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to change someone else, do you see them as truly 'another' or as an extension of yourself? How can you honor their otherness by acting only 'insofar as they are another'?
?
Think of a situation where you are both the agent and patient—like the doctor treating himself. In what area of your life do you need to step back and act as a 'knower' to bring about change in yourself?
?
The definition of quwwa forces you to examine your intentions when influencing others. Will you, from today, consciously remind yourself that your influence is valid only 'insofar as the other is another'? What would that change in your relationships?

Geometric Illustration of Quwwa as Power of a Line
Geometric explanation of quwwa as the power of a line with Pythagorean theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term quwwa is used in geometry to denote the power of a line, specifically in relation to the Pythagorean theorem.

Core Idea
The square of a line is its power; the chord's square equals sum of squares of sides.

In addition to its philosophical meaning, quwwa is used in geometry to describe the power of a line. This lesson explores that usage through the Pythagorean theorem and explains the two meanings of 'square.'

Guided Path
1
Read the engineers' statement
Read the statement that the square of a line is its power, and the chord's square equals the squares of the sides.
This is the starting point of the geometric illustration.
2
Understand the two meanings of murabba'
Murabba' can mean a numerical square or a geometric square. Both are relevant.
3
Follow the numerical example
The example uses sides of 10. Compute squares and find the chord's square as 200.
The chord's root is between 14 and 15.
4
See the equality for both meanings
The Pythagorean equality holds for both numerical and geometric squares. Contemplate this.

Does Ibn Sina's Definition of Quwwa Include Passive Power?
Examines whether Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa covers both active and passive powers, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'principle of change in another' includes passive power, and the key arguments on both sides.

Core Idea
The phrase 'in another' may restrict quwwa to active power, but some interpret it broadly.

Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa (power) is a foundational concept in Islamic philosophy and kalam. However, there is a dispute about whether it covers both active and passive powers. This passage from the commentary examines the objection that the phrase 'in another' limits the definition to active power, and considers the counterargument that 'otherness' can be relative. Understanding this debate clarifies the precise scope of quwwa as a technical term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the translation. Pay attention to the flow: the commentator first states the wider usage, then raises an objection, quotes support, and presents a discussion.
Notice the structure: inclusive definition -> objection -> support -> criticism -> counter.
2
Identify the two sides
Find the commentator's argument that 'in another' implies a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change. This seems to favor active power.
3
Reflect on the counterargument
Consider the possibility that 'otherness' is relative to the agent, allowing inclusion of passive power. Ask yourself which interpretation seems stronger based on the text.
The passage leaves the matter open for discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : القوة بحسب الاصطلاح يتناول القوة الفعلية والانفعالية أعني التي تهيأ محلها نحو الفعل أو الانفعال فلذلك أخذوا في تعريفها التغير الشامل للفعل والانفعال، وقال أيضاً : فإن قلت : هل يطلق القوة المؤثرة على القوة الانفعالية . قلت : صرح بذلك بعضهم وادعى أنهم أرادوا بالتأثير هاهنا التغير مطلقاً سواء كان تأثيراً أو تأثراً، ثم قال بعد نقل قول الكاتبي : القوة بمعنى الصفة المؤثرة عرفها الشيخ بأنها مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وهذا الكلام منه يؤيد إطلاق التأثير على المعنى المتناول للتأثير والقبول كما مر فقد ظهر لك من سياق كلامه أنه حمل التغير في تعريف الشيخ على المعنى الشامل للتأثير والتأثر وجعل التعريف شاملاً للقوة الفعلية والانفعالية، وفيه بحث لأن التغير بحسب مفهومه وإن كان أعم، إلا أن اعتبار كون ذلك التغير في آخر يدل على تخصيص التعريف بالقوة الفعلية لأن المتبادر هو المغايرة بين محل المبدأ ومحل التغير ولو بالاعتبار كما لا يخفى، اللهم إلا أن يقال : الآخرية معتبرة بالنسبة إلى الفاعل مطلقاً فحاصل التعريف أنها مبدأ التغير من شيء في آخر .
His saying: 'A principle of change in another etc.' The commentator said in the glosses of al-Tajrīd: 'Al-quwwa according to technical usage includes active quwwa and passive quwwa, i.e., that which prepares its subject for action or passion. Therefore they took in its definition change (taghayyur) inclusive of action and passion.' And he also said: 'If you say: Is al-quwwa al-mu’aththira (influential power) applied to al-quwwa al-infi‘āliyya (passive power)? I say: Some have explicitly stated that and claimed that by 'influence' here they meant change absolutely, whether it be influence or being influenced.' Then he said after quoting the words of al-Kātibī: 'Al-quwwa in the sense of the influential attribute was defined by the Shaykh [Ibn Sīnā] as a principle of change in another insofar as it is another, and this speech from him supports applying influence to the meaning that includes influence and reception as has passed. So it has appeared to you from the context of his speech that he interpreted change in the Shaykh's definition as including influence and being influenced, and he made the definition inclusive of active and passive power. And in this there is discussion, because change according to its concept, although more general, yet the consideration that that change is in another indicates a specification of the definition to active power, because the obvious meaning is the otherness (al-mughāyara) between the locus of the principle and the locus of change, even if by consideration, as is not hidden. However, it could be said that the otherness is considered relative to the agent absolutely, so the summary of the definition is that it is a principle of change from something in another.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about the scope of Ibn Sīnā's definition of quwwa. The commentator begins by stating that quwwa in technical usage includes both active and passive powers, and that the definition should be taken to include change (taghayyur) broadly covering action and passion. He then raises an objection: 'Is the term 'influential quwwa' applied to passive quwwa?' Answer: Yes, some have explicitly said so, claiming that 'influence' here means change absolutely. To support this, he quotes al-Kātibī, who reports that the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) defined quwwa as 'principle of change in another' and that this supports the inclusive interpretation. However, the commentator then presents a discussion (baḥth): although the concept of change is general, the stipulation 'in another' indicates that the definition is specific to active power, because it implies a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change—they are different even if only in consideration. Nonetheless, he offers a possible counter: 'otherness' can be considered relative to the agent absolutely, so the definition could be read as 'principle of change from something in another,' which might accommodate both. Thus, the passage leaves the matter open, with the objection that the definition seems restricted to active power and the response that the otherness might be relative.

Key Terms
قوة فعالية
قوة انفعالية
تأثير
تأثر
تغير
الشيخ
آخرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'principle of change in another' clearly includes both active and passive powers.
Correction: The phrase 'in another' suggests a distinction between the locus of the principle and the locus of change, which may restrict it to active power; the inclusion of passive power is a matter of interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main objection to including passive power in Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa?
A) Passive power does not involve change.
B) The definition says 'in another', implying the locus of the principle differs from the locus of change.
C) Ibn Sina explicitly excluded passive power.
D) Change cannot be attributed to inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of passive power in Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa is contentious because the phrase 'in another' suggests a distinction between cause and effect that applies primarily to active power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you usually think of your 'power' (quwwa) as only your ability to act and influence, or are you equally aware of your capacity to be changed and affected by truth and by Allah? Examine a recent instance where you had to choose between asserting your influence or being receptive to correction.
?
The commentator notes that some interpret 'in another' as referring to the agent only. In your spiritual life, do you tend to view God as the sole agent and yourself merely passive, or do you balance between active striving and receptive submission? How does this lesson challenge your default posture?
?
If the definition of quwwa were to include only active power, what would that say about your readiness to accept influence from the Quran and the scholars? Are you more comfortable being the one who changes things, or being changed by the truth? Pause and ask Allah to make you soft to His guidance.

Defining Quwwa as the Genus of Capability
Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa and its explanation with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of quwwa as 'the principle of change in another insofar as it is another' and how it functions as the genus for capability, with the example of the self-treating physician.

Core Idea
Quwwa is the principle of change in another, encompassing both acting and being acted upon.

This lesson introduces the precise definition of power (quwwa) that is foundational for the discussion of capability (qudra). Ibn Sina's formulation pinpoints the essence of power as a source of change in something else, and the commentator clarifies its scope with an illustrative example that includes cases where the agent and patient are the same entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that states the definition of quwwa and its intended meaning as the genus of capability. Notice the phrasing 'مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر' and the condition 'من حيث هو آخر'.
The definition is concise; every word is significant.
2
Understand the example of the self-treating physician
Consider why Ibn Sina adds 'insofar as it is another' – to include cases like a physician treating himself, where he acts as a physician on himself as patient. This shows quwwa involves both action and passion.
3
Recognize the different meanings of quwwa
The commentator lists other usages of quwwa (possibility, capability, passivity) that are treated elsewhere. This helps to isolate the specific meaning under discussion.
Only the meaning as genus of capability is focused on here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ( القوة تقال للقدرة والمراد هاهنا (جنسها) أي المقصود في المقصد بيان القوة التي هي جنس القدرة ( وهو ) كما قاله ابن سينا : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وقولنا : من حيث هو آخر ليدخل فيه ) أي في هذا الحد ) المعالج لنفسه فإنه يؤثر من حيث هو عالم بصناعة الطب ( عامل بمقتضاها ) ويتأثر

قوله : ( القوة تقال للقدرة إلخ ) فبهذه المناسبة أورد بيان معاني القوة في بحث القدرة . قوله : ( يعني أن المقصود إلخ بخلاف المعاني الآخر فإن بيانها موكول في المقاصد الأخرى، وذكرها هاهنا استطرادي لبيان إطلاقات القوة فإن بيان الإمكان قد مر في الأمور العامة والقدرة قد مر في المقاصد السابقة، والانفعال سيجيء في الكيفيات الاستعدادية.

وأرادوا أن مربعه يساوي مربعيهما والقريب أن مجموع مربعيهما، حينئذ يكون قوة للوتر فيندرج في قولهم: مربع الخط قوة له وهذا الكلام يستدعي نوع توضيح فنقول : وتر القائمة له، الواصل بين ضلعيها والمربع قد يطلق على العدد الحاصل من ضرب عدد في نفسه، مثلا إذا ضرب عشرة في نفسها حصل مئة فالمئة مربع العشرة والعشرة جذر المئة، وقد يطلق على سطح يحيط به أربعة أضلاع متساوية، وهو المراد هاهنا كما هو الظاهر ومساواة مربع الوتر بمربعي الضلعين يستقيم على كلا المعنيين فلنتصور على المعنى الأول ليتضح على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فنقول : إذا فرضنا كلا من ضلعي القائمة عشرة أجزاء متساوية فمربع كل ضلع مئة مثلها لأنها الحاصلة من ضرب عشرة في نفسها ومربع الوتر يساوي مربع مجموع الضلعين، أعني المئتين فالوتر جذر مئتين وإنه فوق أربعة عشر جزءا وأقل من خمسة عشر وذلك لأن الحاصل من ضرب أربعة عشر في نفسها مئة وستة وتسعون من ضرب خمسة عشر في نفسها مئتان وخسمة وعشرون فلا بد أن يكون جذر المئتين فيما بينهما، وإذا تخيلت هذا تخيلت المساواة على المعنى الثاني أيضاً فليتأمل.
We say: (The term 'power' is said for capability and what is meant here is (its genus), i.e., the intended [matter] in the objective is the explanation of the power that is the genus of capability. And it is, as Ibn Sina said: (the principle of change in another insofar as it is another. And our saying 'insofar as it is another' is to include in it, i.e., in this definition, the one who treats himself, for he acts insofar as he is knowledgeable in the art of medicine and acts according to it, and he is acted upon.)

His saying: (The term 'power' is said for capability, etc.) On this occasion, he presented the explanation of the meanings of power in the discussion of capability. His saying: (He means that the intended [matter], etc.) Unlike the other meanings, for their explanation is delegated to other objectives, and their mention here is digressive to explain the usages of power, for the explanation of possibility has already passed in the general matters, and capability has passed in the previous objectives, and passivity will come in the dispositonal qualities.

And they intended that its square equals the square of the two [sides] and the approximate [meaning] is that the sum of the two squares, then it is a power for the hypotenuse, so it falls under their saying: the square of a line is a power for it. This speech requires some clarification. We say: The hypotenuse of a right angle connects its two sides. The term 'square' may be applied to the number resulting from multiplying a number by itself, e.g., if ten is multiplied by itself, a hundred results; so a hundred is the square of ten, and ten is the root of a hundred. And it may be applied to a surface bounded by four equal sides, which is what is meant here as is apparent. And the equality of the square of the hypotenuse to the squares of the two sides holds on both meanings. So let us conceptualize it according to the first meaning to make it clear also according to the second meaning. We say: If we assume each of the two sides of the right angle to be ten equal parts, then the square of each side is one hundred like them, because it results from multiplying ten by itself. And the square of the hypotenuse equals the square of the sum of the two sides, i.e., two hundred. So the hypotenuse is the root of two hundred, and it is above fourteen parts and less than fifteen, because the product of fourteen multiplied by itself is one hundred ninety-six, and the product of fifteen multiplied by itself is two hundred twenty-five. So the root of two hundred must be between them. And if you have imagined this, you have imagined the equality on the second meaning as well, so reflect.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'quwwa' (power) as used in this objective. It begins by stating that the intended meaning is the genus of capability (qudra). It then presents the definition given by Ibn Sina: 'the principle of change in another insofar as it is another.' The definition is explained with the example of a physician who treats himself — he acts insofar as he is a physician, and he is acted upon insofar as he is a patient. The commentator then notes that this discussion is occasioned by the need to clarify the different meanings of 'quwwa'. He mentions that other meanings (such as possibility, capability, and passivity) are treated elsewhere, and their mention here is digressive to show the usages of the term. The passage then delves into a mathematical illustration of 'quwwa' in geometry, where the square of the hypotenuse is described as a 'quwwa' for the hypotenuse. This demonstrates the technical usage of the term in mathematics. The commentator explains the concept of square as both a number and a geometric figure, and demonstrates the Pythagorean theorem by numerical example. This illustrates that 'quwwa' can refer to a mathematical power or capacity. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the definition of quwwa applies only to active power, excluding cases where the same entity is acted upon.
Correction: The definition explicitly includes the self-treating physician, showing that quwwa can involve both acting and being acted upon, as long as the change occurs in another (even if that 'another' is the same individual under a different aspect).

Checkpoint

A) The principle of change in itself

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is defined as the principle of change in another, and this definition intentionally includes cases where the agent and patient are the same entity under different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit today to consciously view your efforts as a single process of guided action and humble receptivity.
?
Think of a time you tried to change but felt you were only acting without allowing yourself to be acted upon. How did that imbalance affect the outcome? What would it look like to embrace the reality that change requires you to be both the physician and the patient? ?

Write down one action you will take today that reflects both your effort and your openness to being transformed.

Mathematical Usage of Quwwa in Geometry
Illustration of the term quwwa as applied to the square of the hypotenuse in geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term quwwa is used in geometry to denote the square of a side as a 'power' for the hypotenuse, and how the Pythagorean theorem illustrates this usage.

Core Idea
In geometry, the square of a side is called a 'quwwa' for the hypotenuse, showing the term's extension beyond physical power.

This lesson delves into a mathematical digression where the commentator explains the usage of quwwa in the context of the Pythagorean theorem. The passage clarifies that the square of a line can be called a 'quwwa' for that line, and illustrates this with both numerical and geometric interpretations of squaring.

Guided Path
1
Read the mathematical passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the relationship between the sides of a right triangle and how the term quwwa is applied to the square of the hypotenuse.
This is a technical illustration; read slowly.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'square'
The commentator explains that 'square' can refer to the product of a number multiplied by itself (numerical square) or to a geometric figure with four equal sides. Both meanings are used in the theorem.
3
Follow the numerical example
The passage provides a numerical example with sides of length 10, showing that the square of the hypotenuse is 200, and thus the hypotenuse is the root of 200, which lies between 14 and 15.
This demonstrates the equality of squares in a concrete way.

Quwwa as Genus of Qudra and Its Four Types
Defines quwwa as genus of qudra and divides it into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quwwa as the genus of qudra and its classification into four types based on two criteria: number of actions and presence of consciousness.

Core Idea
Quwwa is potential for action, divided by consciousness and whether it produces one or many acts.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif defines quwwa (power) as the genus of qudra (capability). It clarifies that the true agent is the rational soul while the body is affected, and then classifies quwwa into four types. Understanding this classification is essential for Islamic philosophical theology, as it applies to celestial souls, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, clarification, then division.
The passage begins with a specific context about man and medicine, but the main point is the classification.
2
Identify the two criteria for classification
Look for the sentence that introduces the division. It says 'because what issues from quwwa is either one act or different acts, and in both cases either it has consciousness or not.' These are the two criteria: number of actions and consciousness.
3
List the four types and their criteria
Using the two criteria, map each type: celestial soul (one act, with consciousness), elemental nature (one act, without consciousness), animal power (multiple acts, with consciousness), vegetative soul (multiple acts, without consciousness).
Notice that the passage calls the celestial soul 'al-nafs al-falakiyya' and animal power 'al-quwwa al-hayawaniyya'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الإنسان هو هذا النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة من حيث هو جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا مبني على ما يتبادر إلى الجسم، والتحقيق أن المعالج المؤثر هو النفس الناطقة والمعالج المتأثر هو البدن وهما متغايران بالذات فالأولى أن يمثل بمعالجة الإنسان نفسه في إزالة الأخلاق الرديئة التي هي أمراض نفسانية، وإنما كان هذا القيد موجباً لعموم الحدود ودخول ما كان خارجاً عنه لأن المتبادر من لفظ الآخر هو المغاير بالذات فلما قيد بالحيثية علم أن التغاير بالاعتبار كاف والقوة بهذا المعنى تنقسم إلى أقسام أربعة لأن الصادر من القوة إما فعل واحد، أو أفعال مختلفة، وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون لها شعور بما يصدر عنها أو لا، فالأول : النفس الفلكية والثاني: الطبيعة العنصرية وما في معناها والثالث : القوة الحيوانية والرابع: النفس النباتية، وقد مرت
That man is this fourth type - the thirteenth objective: al-quwwa is the genus of qudra in terms of him being a body that is affected by what encounters it of medicine, and this is based on what occurs to the body, and the verification is that the effective agent is the rational soul and the affected patient is the body, and they are distinct in essence, so it is more appropriate to exemplify by man's treatment of himself in removing bad morals which are psychological diseases. And this qualification necessitated the generality of the definition and the inclusion of what was outside it because what is immediately understood from the word 'other' is that which is distinct in essence, but when it was qualified by 'by virtue of', it became known that distinction by consideration is sufficient. And al-quwwa in this sense is divided into four divisions because what issues from al-quwwa is either one act or different acts, and in both cases either it has consciousness of what issues from it or not. So the first: the celestial soul; the second: elemental nature and what is in its meaning; the third: animal power; the fourth: the vegetative soul, and they have been mentioned.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines al-quwwa as the genus of qudra and divides it into four types. It begins by explaining that man, as a body, is acted upon by medicine, but the true agent is the rational soul, while the body is the patient. The qualification 'by virtue of' broadens the definition because the term 'other' normally implies essential distinction, but here relational distinction suffices. Then it divides quwwa into four: (1) celestial soul (one action, with consciousness), (2) elemental nature (one action, without consciousness), (3) animal power (multiple actions, with consciousness), (4) vegetative soul (multiple actions, without consciousness). This classification is based on two criteria: whether the power produces one or many actions, and whether it has awareness of its action.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
قدرة (qudra)
نفس ناطقة (rational soul)
نفس فلكية (celestial soul)
طبيعة عنصرية (elemental nature)
قوة حيوانية (animal power)
نفس نباتية (vegetative soul)
الحيثية (by virtue of, relational distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of quwwa is purely physical, involving only bodies acting on each other.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the effective agent is the rational soul, and the qualification 'by virtue of' shows that relational distinction is sufficient, not essential distinction.

Checkpoint
What are the two criteria used to classify quwwa into four types?
A) Number of actions and presence of consciousness
B) Material vs. immaterial, and eternal vs. temporal
C) Essential vs. accidental, and agent vs. patient
D) One act vs. many acts, and rational vs. irrational

Key Takeaway
Quwwa is classified into four types based on whether its action is single or multiple and whether it has consciousness of its action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of your daily habits are driven by unconscious powers (like elemental nature) and which by conscious choice (like animal or rational soul)? What would change if you brought more consciousness to your automatic actions?
?
The passage illustrates that the rational soul is the true agent, not the body. In your struggle to change a bad moral trait, have you been treating your body as the cause rather than commanding your soul? How can you shift that today?
?
You are a human, granted the power of many acts with consciousness. Yet how often do you live like a vegetative soul—growing and moving but without awareness of why? What one step can you take this week to act from your rational soul instead?

The Broadening Qualification and the True Agent in Treatment
Explains the broadening qualification and identifies the true agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a qualification in a definition can broaden rather than restrict, and why the soul is considered the true agent in treatment, with bodily instruments being mere tools.

Core Idea
Qualifications can generalize, and the soul alone is the true agent.

This passage from the commentary addresses two key issues: first, how a qualification that appears to restrict actually broadens the definition by reinterpreting a key term; second, why the rational soul is the true agent in treatment, even though bodily instruments are used. These clarifications are essential for understanding the kalam approach to definitions and agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the repeated structure of objection and answer.
2
Identify the first objection and its answer
Look for the objection that a qualification should exclude what was previously included. The answer distinguishes between specification and interpretation.
The key phrase is 'if it is an interpretation from one meaning to a more general meaning, then it is generalization.'
3
Identify the second objection and its answer
Find the objection that treatment involves bodily instruments, so the agent seems composite. The answer states that instruments are not real agents; only the soul is.
The commentator says 'the instruments of the act are not attributed to the act in reality; rather it is attributed to the agent.'
4
Reflect on the final remark about the qualification
The passage ends with a note that the qualification 'by virtue of' is not a real qualification but removes a mistaken one, sufficiency of relational distinction.
This ties back to the first point about broadening.

قوله : (هو النفس الناطقة) وإن كان باعتبار تعلقه بالبدن إذ لا بد في العلاج من استعمال الأعضاء. قوله : ( هو البدن لأنه من حيث كونه متعلق النفس، إذ لا بد من الحياة حين العلاج. قوله : ( وإنما كان إلخ ) دفع لما يتراءى من أنه مخالف لما تقرر من أن القيد في الإثبات للإخراج وحاصل الدفع أنه فيما إذا كان القيد تخصيصاً للإثبات السابق، أما إذا كان تفسيراً له من معنى إلى معنى أعم فهو التعميم. قوله : والتحقيق أن المعالج إلخ) قيل : المعالجة هي الطلب العملي، فهي لا تختص بالنفس لأنها لا يكون إلا بآلات بدنية نعم يمكن ادعاء اختصاص الطب العملي بالنفس وإن كان حصوله لها بمعاونة الآلات البدنية فالمعالج بكسر اللام ليس في التحقيق إلا المجموع، وأما المعالج بالفتح فيحتمل أن يكون هو المجموع أيضاً، لأن النفس لما جاز تأثرها عما ارتسم في قواها بأن ينتقش بكليات تلك الجزئيات المرتسمة في القوى، ويحصل لها بواسطة تلك الانتقاش أعراض نفسانية كالغضب ونحوه جاز أن يتأثر أيضاً من الأحوال البدنية العارضة للقوى من الصحة والمرض والجواب أن آلات الفعل لا يسند إليها الفعل حقيقة وإنما يسند إلى الفاعل. قوله : ( وإنما كان هذا القيد موجباً إلخ إشارة إلى جواب ما يقال : المعقول كون القيد في الإثبات موجباً لخصوص الحد وخروج ما كان داخلاً قبله فكيف انعكس الأمر هاهنا، وحاصل الجواب أن المذكور هاهنا ليس بقيد لما قبله من التحقيق، بل إزالة للقيد المتوهم منه، وهو وجوب المغايرة الذاتية لكن في دلالة قيد الحيثية على كفاية التغاير الاعتباري نوع مناقشة يندفع بلزوم استدراكها وإذا لم يحمل عليه فتأمل
His saying: ('He is the rational soul') even if it is with consideration of its relation to the body, since treatment necessarily involves the use of limbs. His saying: ('He is the body') because in terms of it being related to the soul, since life is necessarily present during treatment. His saying: ('And this qualification' etc.) is a rebuttal of what appears to be contradictory to what is established that a qualification in affirmation is for exclusion, and the gist of the rebuttal is that it applies when the qualification is a specification of the previous affirmation, but if it is an interpretation from one meaning to a more general meaning, then it is generalization. His saying: ('And the verification is that the agent' etc.) It was said: al-mu'ālaja (treatment) is practical seeking, so it is not specific to the soul because it only occurs with bodily instruments. Yes, it can be claimed that practical medicine is specific to the soul even if its attainment is with the assistance of bodily instruments. So the agent (mu'ālij with kasr of lām) in reality is nothing but the composite. As for the patient (mu'ālaj with fatḥ of lām), it is possible that it is also the composite, because when the soul is susceptible to being affected by what is imprinted in its powers such that it is impressed by the universals of those particulars imprinted in the powers, and by means of that impression psychological accidents like anger and the like occur to it, it is possible that it also be affected by the physical states that occur to the powers from health and disease. And the answer is that the instruments of the act are not attributed to the act in reality; rather it is attributed to the agent. His saying: ('And this qualification necessitated' etc.) is an indication of the answer to what is said: It is reasonable that a qualification in affirmation necessitates specificity of the definition and exclusion of what was previously included, so how is the matter reversed here? The gist of the answer is that what is mentioned here is not a qualification of what preceded it of verification, rather it is removing the misunderstanding of a qualification, which is the necessity of essential distinction. But there is some contention in the indication of the qualification 'by virtue of' for the sufficiency of relational distinction, which is repelled by the necessity of its completion. And if it is not taken thus, then reflect.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that addresses objections to the earlier definition. It explains that the qualification 'by virtue of' does not restrict but expands the definition, because it interprets the term 'other' in a broader sense, making relational difference sufficient. It also discusses the true agent: although the body and soul both seem to be involved, the act is truly attributed to the soul alone, as instruments are not real agents. Finally, it answers the objection that a qualification should specify: here it removes a mistaken qualification (that essential distinction is needed), and the sufficiency of relational distinction is accepted.

Key Terms
قيد (qualification)
مغايرة بالاعتبار (relational distinction)
معالج (agent)
آلات (instruments)
فاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qualifications always restrict definitions, and the composite of soul and body is the true agent in treatment.
Correction: Qualifications can generalize when they reinterpret terms, and the soul alone is the true agent; bodily instruments are not agents.

Checkpoint
Why does the qualification 'by virtue of' broaden the definition rather than restrict it?
A) Because it specifies that the distinction must be essential
B) Because it interprets 'other' to mean merely relational difference
C) Because it limits the definition to the soul only
D) Because it removes the need for any distinction

Key Takeaway
Qualifications can generalize by reinterpreting terms, and the soul alone is the true agent; instruments are not agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily actions—study, work, worship—do you habitually attribute outcomes to your own effort (body) or to your soul's intention? How does realizing that the soul is the true agent change how you prepare and perform these actions? Will you consciously remind yourself that the body is merely an instrument before your next task?
?
When you fall short in a religious duty, do you excuse yourself by saying 'my body was weak' or 'my circumstances were difficult'? According to this lesson, the soul remains the agent. Does this responsibility make you uncomfortable? What will you do differently tomorrow to align your soul's will with the command of Allah?
?
The qualification in the definition broadened rather than restricted. Are there verses or hadith you have understood in a restrictive way that might actually be open to a broader, more merciful meaning? What presumption prevents you from reconsidering? Will you read one such text this week with the intention of seeing its wider interpretation?
